State v. Chappell


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-07-0384-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


225 Ariz. 229; 236 P.3d 1176; 2010 Ariz. LEXIS 34; 588 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 12



August 3, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Chappell v. Arizona, 562 U.S. 1227, 131 S. Ct. 1485, 179 L. Ed. 2d 320, 2011 U.S. LEXIS 1501 (2011)
Subsequent appeal at, Remanded by, Sub nomine at State v. Rayes, 2012 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 914 (2012)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Stay denied by, Motion denied by Chappell v. Ryan, 2017 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 13976 (D. Ariz., Feb. 1, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR2004-037319. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Robert L. Gottsfield, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Maricopa County, Arizona, that convicted him of child abuse and first-degree murder and that imposed a capital sentence, arguing that the evidence was insufficient to support the finding of the (F)(6) aggravator under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-751(F)(6) to support the imposition of the death sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant intentionally drowned his girlfriend's two-year-old son. On review, the court held that the evidence supported the jurors' finding that the murder was especially cruel under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-751(F)(6) because the medical examiner testified that the victim was likely was conscious for 30 seconds to two minutes while being held underwater and that the two-year-old would have experienced extreme discomfort due to the inability to breathe. Further, defendant told reporters during a post-arrest press conference that the victim struggled and that, hours after the drowning, he could still remember the victim looking at him as he was in the water; those facts supported a finding that the victim consciously experienced mental anguish before his death, and the jurors also could have reasonably inferred from this evidence that defendant knew that the victim would suffer. Therefore, sufficient evidence supported the jurors' finding that the murder was especially cruel. The mere fact that the victim was two years old and possibly did not comprehend that he was dying did not prevent the jury from finding that he consciously experienced mental anguish before his death.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction and sentence was affirmed.

State v. Womble


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-07-0139-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


225 Ariz. 91; 235 P.3d 244; 2010 Ariz. LEXIS 30; 586 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 4



July 12, 2010, Decided 
July 12, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Womble v. Arizona, 131 S. Ct. 832, 178 L. Ed. 2d 566, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 9777 (U.S., 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR2002-010926. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Andrew G. Klein, Judge.
State v. Speer, 221 Ariz. 449, 212 P.3d 787, 2009 Ariz. LEXIS 242 (Ariz., 2009)



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County, AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Maricopa County Superior Court (Arizona) that convicted him of first-degree murder, attempted first-degree murder, conspiracy to commit first-degree murder, burglary, misconduct involving weapons, and two counts of aggravated assault and that imposed a capital sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and his brother broke into an apartment, robbed a couple, and were apprehended shortly thereafter. When their efforts to persuade the victims not to testify failed, they devised a plan to kill the victims. Thereafter, defendant broke into the victims' apartment a second time and shot both the husband and wife as they slept. The husband was killed, but the wife survived. Their infant son, who was in bed with his parents, was uninjured. On review, the court held that State proved the (F)(6) aggravator under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-751 (2010), which required a showing of heinousness or depravity, because the elimination of a witness to prior crime supported a finding of heinousness or depravity. However, the (F)(3) aggravator--which required a showing that defendant knowingly created a grave risk of death to the infant--was not established because it was dark in the apartment, and the evidence showed only that defendant saw the outlines of the adults' bodies and likely did not notice their infant son. Because the State did not prove beyond a reasonable doubt that defendant knew the baby was in the bed, the evidence was insufficient to establish the (F)(3) aggravator.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction and sentence was affirmed.

 

65 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


State v. Villalobos


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-08-0098-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


225 Ariz. 74; 235 P.3d 227; 2010 Ariz. LEXIS 26; 585 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 4



July 2, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Villalobos v. Arizona, 131 S. Ct. 901, 178 L. Ed. 2d 759, 2011 U.S. LEXIS 169 (U.S., Jan. 10, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR2004-005523-001. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Raymond P. Lee, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court in Maricopa County, Arizona, that convicted him of first-degree murder and child abuse, arguing that the trial court erred under Ariz. R. Evid. 404 in admitting prior acts evidence and erred under Ariz. R. Evid. 403 in permitting the State to introduce autopsy photographs into evidence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant killed his two-year-old daughter, who died of blunt force trauma to the abdomen. On review, the court held that the trial court did not err in permitting the State to introduce testimony that he had violently shaken and struck his daughter in the months preceding the fatal attack because the child abuse charge required proof that defendant intentionally or knowingly injured the victim, and the prior abuse evidence was relevant to establish defendant's mental state. Further, this evidence also rebutted defendant's claim that he did not intend to hurt the victim and hit her as a "reflex," as well as his contention that the child's mother could have caused the fatal injuries. Further, the trial court did not err in permitting the State to introduce autopsy photographs into evidence because the photographs were relevant to prove the cause of death and the extent of the abuse and because each photograph illustrated a different aspect of the medical examiner's testimony; this evidence was also relevant to rebut defendant's argument that the victim seemed fine after the beating and his suggestion that she died because of a lack of prompt medical attention.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction and sentence was affirmed.

State v. Hargrave


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-06-0061-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


225 Ariz. 1; 234 P.3d 569; 2010 Ariz. LEXIS 22; 584 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 36



June 14, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Hargrave v. Arizona, 562 U.S. 928, 131 S. Ct. 317, 178 L. Ed. 2d 207, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 7205 (2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR2002-009759. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Roland J. Steinle, Judge.

DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County, REMANDED FOR RESENTENCING ON BURGLARY, AFFIRMED IN ALL OTHER RESPECTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Maricopa County Superior Court, Arizona, convicted defendant of three counts of first degree murder, one count of armed robbery, one count of burglary, and three counts of kidnapping. He was sentenced to death for each of the murders. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court found that evidence of defendant's affiliation with the Imperial Royal Guard was relevant to establish a motive for the crimes and its probative value was not substantially outweighed by the prejudice it might have caused. Defendant did not satisfy his initial burden of establishing that the trial court committed any error in admitting this evidence. The supreme court has rejected the claim that Arizona's method of execution by lethal injection automatically constituted cruel and unusual punishment. The aggravating factors, Ariz. Rev. Stat. §§ 13-751(F)(5), (6), and (8) were properly found.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed as to defendant's convictions and death sentences, and remanded for resentencing on the burglary conviction.

State v. Garcia


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-07-0438-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


224 Ariz. 1; 226 P.3d 370; 2010 Ariz. LEXIS 17; 578 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 4



March 18, 2010, Decided

Habeas corpus proceeding at, Stay denied by, Motion denied by Garcia v. Ryan, 2017 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 65806 (D. Ariz., May 1, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR2002-016160A. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable John R. Ditsworth, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County, AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court in Maricopa County, Arizona, convicted defendant of armed robbery and first degree murder. He was sentenced to death for murder. The state supreme court heard his appeal.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with armed robbery and first degree murder. The trial court did not abuse its discretion in allowing the State to ask prospective jurors if they could consider imposing a death sentence if a defendant had not actually shot the victim. A juror was properly stricken after testifying that he was not positive he could vote for death. The State offered race-neutral explanations for striking a juror who could not read English. While the trial court ordered a mistrial of the aggravation phase after a juror disclosed misconduct, the trial court was not required to grant a mistrial for the already completed guilt phase. In reconsidering an earlier ruling, the second judge properly gave effect to Arizona law. Defendant was not entitled to bifurcate the death eligibility and aggravation issues. The trial court properly instructed the jurors that they could consider any relevant factor as mitigating. The Supreme Court of Arizona upheld the death sentence. Substantial evidence supported the jury's finding that defendant was a major participant in the underlying robbery, and he acted with reckless indifference to human life.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Cropper


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-08-0116-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


223 Ariz. 522; 225 P.3d 579; 2010 Ariz. LEXIS 16; 577 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 4



March 11, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Cropper v. Arizona, 131 S. Ct. 415, 178 L. Ed. 2d 324, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 8306 (U.S., 2010)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Kyzar v. Schriro, 2012 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 162765 (D. Ariz., May 16, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR-1997-003949. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Joseph B. Heilman, Judge.
State v. Cropper, 206 Ariz. 153, 76 P.3d 424, 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 114 (Ariz., 2003)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Maricopa County Superior Court, Arizona, convicted defendant of first degree murder and was sentenced to death. The Arizona Supreme Court vacated defendant's sentence and remanded for resentencing under the appropriate statutes. On remand, the jury concluded that death was the appropriate punishment. An automatic appeal followed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the second penalty-phase trial violated his rights under the Ex Post Facto Clauses of the United States and Arizona Constitutions, U.S. Const. art. I, § 10; Ariz. Const. art. 2, § 25. The supreme court noted that the change in the law permitting the state to retry the penalty phase when the first jury could not reach a decision neither added a new element to the crime of first degree murder nor increased the punishment for the crime. The prosecutor did not commit misconduct in his arguments regarding the Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-751(F)(6) cruelty aggravator because the comments did not dispute the essential elements of physical cruelty. Because the argument focused on considerations proper for the jury in light of the instruction defendant requested, the prosecutor did not commit misconduct. The facts established that the officer consciously suffered physical pain and defendant knew or should have known he would experience such pain. In light of the evidence, it was difficult to conclude that defendant's later remorse was genuine. The aggravators were entitled to substantial weight.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Kuhs


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-07-0301-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


223 Ariz. 376; 224 P.3d 192; 2010 Ariz. LEXIS 13; 576 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 13



February 24, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Kuhs v. Arizona, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 6196 (U.S., Oct. 4, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR 2005-138481. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Paul J. McMurdie, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury in the Maricopa County Superior Court, Arizona, convicted defendant of first degree burglary and first degree murder. He was sentenced to death. An automatic appeal followed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant entered the victim's apartment and stabbed him twenty-one times. Defendant confessed to the killing. He was charged with first degree burglary and first degree murder. The trial court did not abuse its discretion in making its competency determination without holding an evidentiary hearing. The State's evidence showed that defendant entered the victim's apartment uninvited and armed with a knife. The evidence supported the jury's finding that he entered the victim's apartment intending to commit aggravated assault, and was sufficient to support his conviction for first degree murder. Defendant was not entitled to a mistrial when the victim's stepmother cried audibly. The instructions appropriately informed the jury of the verdict and sentencing options. The trial court did not err by sending the jury back to deliberate after two separate impasse or deadlock notes. The jury did not abuse its discretion in finding that the State proved an aggravator under Ariz. Rev. Stat. 13-751(F)(6), because the murder was especially cruel. The Supreme Court of Arizona held that the jury did not abuse its discretion in determining that death was the appropriate sentence.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction and death sentence were affirmed.

In re General Adjudication of All Rights To Use Water In the Gila River Sys. & Source


Arizona Supreme Court Nos. WC-07-0001-IR and WC-07-0003-IR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


223 Ariz. 362; 224 P.3d 178; 2010 Ariz. LEXIS 12; 576 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 6



February 19, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at, Decision reached on appeal by In re General Adjudication of All Rights to Use Water in the Gila River Sys., 2012 Ariz. LEXIS 195 (Ariz., Sept. 12, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Maricopa County Superior Court Nos. W-1, W-2, W-3, W-4 (Consolidated). (Contested Case No. W1-207). Interlocutory Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Eddward P. Ballinger, Jr., Judge.
In re Gen. Adjudication of All Rights to Use Water, 217 Ariz. 276, 173 P.3d 440, 2007 Ariz. LEXIS 138 (Ariz., 2007)



DISPOSITION: Interlocutory Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County, AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the Apache Tribes and various appropriators of Gila River water, filed an interlocutory appeal of the judgment and decree of the Superior Court in Maricopa County, Arizona, adjudicating rights to use water in the Gila River and approving the settlement agreement of the Gila River Indian Community (GRIC). The state supreme court accepted interlocutory review.


OVERVIEW: In 1991, the Arizona Supreme Court issued a special order governing the approval of settlement agreements involving the claims of Indian tribes to use water in the Gila River system and source. In 2006, the settling parties applied for approval of the GRIC settlement agreement to allow GRIC to receive 653, 500 acre-feet of water per year. The adjudication court determined that the Apache Tribes had no viable objections, because the agreement did not affect their water rights. The limited scope of settlement review provided in the special order did not unfairly prevent the Tribe and other objectors them from challenging settlements. The adjudication court did not have an inherent duty to consider the constitutionality, legality, and fairness of the settlement agreement. The Apache Tribes' objection about water quality was outside the special order's scope of review. The lower Gila water users were not bound by the settlement or judgment, because they were not settling parties and did not sign the agreement. Their constitutional and statutory arguments were outside the scope of review. A contract claim also fell outside the scope of review allowed by the special order.


OUTCOME: The judgment and decree of the adjudication court were affirmed.

State v. Guillen


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-09-0188-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


223 Ariz. 314; 223 P.3d 658; 2010 Ariz. LEXIS 6; 573 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 11



January 15, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Court of Appeals Division Two No. 2 CA-CR 07-0365. Pima County Superior Court No. CR20064552. Appeal from the Superior Court in Pima County. The Honorable Michael J. Cruikshank, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals Division Two.
State v. Guillen, 222 Ariz. 81, 213 P.3d 230, 2009 Ariz. App. LEXIS 648 (Ariz. Ct. App., 2009)



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court in Pima County, AFFIRMED. Opinion of the Court of Appeals Division Two, VACATED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of Pima County, Arizona, convicted defendant of possession of marijuana for sale and drug paraphernalia. The Arizona Court of Appeals reversed and remanded. The State petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: The State argued that the court of appeals erred in interpreting the statute constitution, Ariz. Const. art. 2, § 8, and, in any event, defendant's wife's voluntary consent obviated the need to reach the state constitutional question. The supreme court concluded that defendant's wife's consent was valid because intervening circumstances obviated any alleged taint and the first dog sniff conducted from outside the garage was not flagrant police misconduct. The wife's consent was obtained within a few hours after the dog sniff took place. The wife's lack of knowledge of the dog sniff constituted a major break in the casual chain. Because the wife knew nothing of the dog sniff, no link between the alleged illegality and the consent was established. Thus, she was in the same posture as a person not previously subject to an illegal entry. The wife's consent was sufficiently attenuated from any alleged prior illegality.


OUTCOME: The opinion of the court of appeals was vacated and defendant's conviction was affirmed.

Brewer v. Burns


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-09-0168-SA


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


222 Ariz. 234; 213 P.3d 671; 2009 Ariz. LEXIS 253; 562 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 32



August 13, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended August 28, 2009.


DISPOSITION:  [***1] JURISDICTION ACCEPTED; RELIEF DENIED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the Governor of Arizona, filed suit against respondents, the Arizona Legislature and various associated members thereof, seeking an order that they present budget bills to her without delay. The matter arose from a dispute between the parties regarding the state budget for a particular fiscal year. The Legislature sought reconsideration after a prior ruling by the court.


OVERVIEW: The Arizona Senate and then the House of Representatives passed several appropriations bills regarding the budget. However, the Governor announced her opposition and her intent to partially veto the bills, and the Legislature declined to present them to her. The Governor filed her petition. It was noted that the court had previously ruled that pursuant to Ariz. Const. art. 4, pt. 2, § 12, the Legislature could not pass bills and then withhold them in order to prevent the Governor from exercising her power to approve or veto the legislation. The Legislature sought reconsideration, seeking delay of presentment for some "reasonable time." The court initially determined that it had special action jurisdiction over the issue pursuant to Ariz. Const. art. 6, § 5(1), that the Governor had standing, and that the matter was ripe for judicial resolution. It held that based on the language of § 12, the Legislature was required to present passed bills to the Governor with no more delay than was reasonably necessary to complete any ministerial tasks and otherwise effect their orderly transmittal. However, mandamus relief was not warranted due to the lack of necessity in the circumstances.


OUTCOME: The court accepted jurisdiction over the matter, and it denied the requested relief.

Yuma County Superior Court Nos. SC89C15444 and SC89C15577. Appeal from the Superior Court in Yuma & Maricopa Counties. The Honorable Kirby D. Kongable, Judge Pro Tempore 1.

CASE SUMMARY:

OVERVIEW: Defendant's convictions arose from the murder of his girlfriend and her two young children. On appeal, defendant asserted that the trial court erred with regard to instructing the jury on premeditation. However, the court found that the jury instruction given was consistent with Ariz. Rev. The court found no error with regard to any procedural of evidentiary aspect of the case.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the verdict and sentence.

Ariz. Early Childhood Dev. & Health Bd. v. Brewer


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-09-0078-SA


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


221 Ariz. 467; 212 P.3d 805; 2009 Ariz. LEXIS 243; 561 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 58



July 24, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Az. Early Childhood v. Brewer, 2009 Ariz. LEXIS 274 (Ariz., June 1, 2009)


DISPOSITION:  [***1] JURISDICTION ACCEPTED; RELIEF GRANTED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Arizona Early Childhood Development & Health Board (Board) argued in a special action that the legislature did not act within its authority when it transferred $ 7 million in income earned on revenue from the Early Childhood Development and Health Fund (Fund) into respondent State's general fund.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court noted that the structure and purpose of the Early Childhood Initiative, and specifically Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 8-1181, demonstrated that the phrase "as otherwise provided by law" in § 8-1181(E) (2007) did not provide the legislature with authority to transfer interest and income from funds generated by the tobacco tax to the general fund. The Board had authority over the distribution of revenues and investment income. Read in the context of the other provisions of the Early Childhood Initiative, the final clause of § 8-1181(E) was most logically read as providing the legislature only a limited power to credit interest and other income to a Fund account other than the account of origin. Once such income was credited, however, it was subject to § 8-1181's comprehensive scheme. Allowing monies to be siphoned from the Fund to the general fund was not consistent with the purpose of the initiative. Rather, the purpose of the initiative was to ensure that revenues serve the specific program aims of the initiative. The transfer violated Ariz. Const. art. 4, pt. 1, § 1(6)(D).


OUTCOME: The supreme court accepted jurisdiction, granted relief to the Board, and ordered the $ 7 million fund sweep, along with the interest that would have been earned on this amount, be returned to the Fund.

State v. Speer


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-07-0103-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


221 Ariz. 449; 212 P.3d 787; 2009 Ariz. LEXIS 242; 561 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 48



July 24, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Speer v. Arizona, 130 S. Ct. 1520, 176 L. Ed. 2d 126, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 1479 (U.S., 2010)
Related proceeding at State v. Womble, 2010 Ariz. LEXIS 30 (Ariz., July 12, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR2002-010926. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Andrew G. Klein, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County, AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an automatic appeal of a judgment of the Superior Court of Maricopa County, Arizona, that convicted defendant of first-degree murder and imposed the death penalty, defendant argued that State failed to prove aggravating factors under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-751(F) (Supp. 2008) sufficient to support the imposition of the death penalty.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and his half-brother committed burglary. After being arrested, defendant devised a plan to murder the victims to prevent them from testifying. On review of the capital sentence imposed following defendant's murder conviction, the court held that the State did not establish the (F)(3) aggravator because the evidence did not establish beyond a reasonable doubt that defendant knew that the murder plot would place the victims' infant in danger. While defendant knew that children lived in the apartment, he could not have known that one of the murders would take place in the parents' bedroom or that the couple's infant would be in bed with the father. As such, there was no showing that defendant knew that the child would be in the zone of danger. However, the (F)(2) aggravator was established because the murders were committed during a robbery, which was a serious offense, the (F)(6) aggravator was also established because the murders were perpetrated to eliminate witnesses, and the (F)(7) aggravator was established because the murders were committed while defendant was on parole from an armed robbery conviction and while released on bail on the burglary charge.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the superior court was affirmed.

State v. Moore


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-07-0164-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


222 Ariz. 1; 213 P.3d 150; 2009 Ariz. LEXIS 244



July 24, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Moore v. Arizona, 558 U.S. 1053, 130 S. Ct. 747, 175 L. Ed. 2d 524, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 8562 (U.S., 2009)
Subsequent appeal at, Remanded by, Sub nomine at State v. Rayes, 2012 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 908 (2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR1999-016742-001 DT. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Norman D. Hall, Judge (Deceased).



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County, CONVICTIONS OTHER THAN FIRST-DEGREE PREMEDITATED MURDER AFFIRMED; FIRST-DEGREE PREMEDITATED MURDER CONVICTIONS AFFIRMED IN PART, REVERSED IN PART; SENTENCES AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court in Maricopa County, Arizona, that convicted him of murder and imposed a death sentence, arguing that his conviction of felony murder was precluded by the merger doctrine, that the trial court impermissibly limited his voir dire, and that the trial court erred in precluding evidence of actual innocence at sentencing.


OVERVIEW: On review, the court rejected defendant's argument that, under the merger doctrine, felony murder could not be predicated upon a burglary that was itself based on the intent to murder because the felony murder statute, Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-1105(A)(2) (Supp. 1999), did not distinguish between burglaries defined by intent to commit assault versus intent to murder. It would be anomalous to conclude that first-degree murder occurs if a burglary with intent to assault results in death but not if the burglary is based on the more culpable intent to murder. Further, while defendant was afforded an opportunity during voir dire to identify and to strike for cause prospective jurors who would automatically impose the death penalty once guilt is found, he was not entitled to ask prospective jurors to identify circumstances they would find mitigating or to answer open-ended questions about their views on mitigation. Further, the court rejected defendant's argument that the trial court erred in precluding evidence of actual innocence because the trial court was not constitutionally or statutorily required to admit evidence or permit argument regarding residual doubt at a sentencing trial.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the superior court was affirmed.

State v. Bearup


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-07-0048-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


221 Ariz. 163; 211 P.3d 684; 2009 Ariz. LEXIS 184; 561 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 42



July 17, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Bearup v. Arizona, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 3569 (U.S., Apr. 26, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Maricopa County Superior Court. No. CR2003-024938-001. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Warren J. Granville, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County, AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Maricopa County Superior Court, Arizona, convicted defendant of one count of kidnapping and one count of first degree murder, for which he was sentenced to death. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, defendant raised several issued contending the State presented insufficient evidence to support his convictions and the imposition of the death sentence. The supreme court disagreed, finding first that the evidence supported a finding that he intended to participate in inflicting injury on the victim, including testimony from his ex-wife that he went with some friends to beat up somebody. As defendant's theory of the case denied all involvement in the killing, and no evidence provided a basis for a second degree murder conviction, the lesser-included offense instruction was properly refused. The evidence also showed that defendant was a major participant in the crime, including that he was armed with a knife and encircled the victim to prevent him from leaving as his codefendant administered a savage beating. Defendant also helped dump the body and cut off the victim's finger after the beating. The evidence showed that the murder was especially cruel and heinous or depraved, that defendant was not a minor participant, and supported the imposition of the death penalty.


OUTCOME: The convictions and death sentence were affirmed.

Chronis v. Steinle


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-08-0394-SA


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


220 Ariz. 559; 208 P.3d 210; 2009 Ariz. LEXIS 105



June 3, 2009, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR2008-006808-001 DT. Special Action from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Roland J. Steinle, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Special Action from the Superior Court in Maricopa County, VACATED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a petition for a special action, challenging the denial of his motion to dismiss the death penalty notice by the Maricopa County Superior Court (Arizona), arguing that dismissal was the proper remedy because no finding of probable cause had been made as to the aggravating factor.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested after police found the body of his girlfriend in the car he was driving. A grand jury indicted him for first-degree murder, and the State subsequently filed an Allegation of Death Penalty and Notice of Aggravating Factors, alleging that defendant killed the victim in a cruel manner. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the death penalty notice because no finding of probable cause had been made as to the aggravator. The trial court denied the motion, ruling that Ariz. R. Crim. P. 13.5(c) could include a challenge to the factual underpinnings of a capital aggravator but that defendant had not carried his burden of proving that probable cause did not exist. After the trial court denied defendant's motion for reconsideration, defendant petitioned for a special action. On review, the court held that Ariz. R. Crim. P. 13.5(c) allowed a defendant in a capital case to request a probable cause determination for alleged aggravating circumstances. Such determinations were to be made following the procedure in Ariz. R. Crim. P. 5, under which the State would bear the burden of proof.


OUTCOME: The special relief action was granted, the trial court order denying a probable cause hearing was vacated, and the case was remanded for further proceedings.

Ariz. Minority Coalition for Fair Redistricting v. Ariz. Indep. Redistricting Comm'n


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-08-0161-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


220 Ariz. 587; 208 P.3d 676; 2009 Ariz. LEXIS 102



May 20, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Court of Appeals Division One No. 1 CA-CV 07-0301. Maricopa County Superior Court No. CV2002-004380. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Kenneth L. Fields, Judge (Retired). Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, 219 Ariz. 50, 192 P.3d 409 (App. 2008).
Ariz. Minority Coalition for Fair Redistricting v. Ariz. Indep. Redistricting Comm'n, 219 Ariz. 50, 192 P.3d 409, 2008 Ariz. App. LEXIS 59 (Ariz. Ct. App., 2008)



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County, VACATED AND REMANDED. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, VACATED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Maricopa County Superior Court, Arizona, concluded that appellant Arizona's Independent Redistricting Commission (Commission) failed to favor the creation of competitive legislative districts and that this failure was arbitrary and capricious and a violation of Ariz. Const. art. 4, pt. 2, § 1(14)(F). The Arizona Court of Appeals reversed. Appellees, coalition and others, petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court noted that the Commission acted as a legislative body. The supreme court had to determine whether the Commission followed the constitutionally required procedure in adopting its final redistricting plan. The record demonstrated that the Commission did engage in the required deliberative process in meetings open to the public. The Commission followed the mandatory constitutional procedure by attempting to accommodate the competitiveness goal, while taking into account whether greater competitiveness would cause significant detriment to the other goals. The multi-step approach the Commission followed, which allowed public comment on more than one draft map, did not violate any constitutionally mandated procedure. The coalition did not meet its burden of establishing that the plan lacked a reasonable basis. The fact that a "better" plan existed did not establish that the Commission's plan lacked a reasonable basis.


OUTCOME: The opinion of the court of appeals was vacated, the judgment of the trial court was reversed and remanded to the trial court with instructions to enter judgment in favor of the Commission.

State v. Dann


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-07-0153-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


220 Ariz. 351; 207 P.3d 604; 2009 Ariz. LEXIS 103; 557 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 3



May 19, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Dann v. Arizona, 130 S. Ct. 466, 175 L. Ed. 2d 313, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 7496 (U.S., 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR1999-003536. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Boyd T. Johnson, Visiting Judge from Pinal County.
State v. Dann, 206 Ariz. 371, 79 P.3d 58, 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 131 (Ariz., 2003)



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County, AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an automatic appeal pursuant to Ariz. R. Crim. P. 31.2.6, the death penalties imposed by the Superior Court of Maricopa County, Arizona, following defendant's murder convictions were submitted for review.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court held that the evidence was sufficient to permit the jury to find the existence of the F.8 aggravating factor--that defendant committed multiple murders during the commission of the offense--under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-751 (Supp. 2008) beyond a reasonable doubt. The State proved that the homicides were temporarily, spatially, and motivationally related and took place during a continuous course of criminal conduct. The victims were killed in close proximity to one another. All died in the same room of the apartment in which one of the victims lived and had been seated near one another; this satisfied the spatial relationship requirement. Similarly, the evidence showed that all victims were killed within moments of one another where defendant's girlfriend testified that, immediately after the murders, defendant came to her apartment and told her that he shot and killed the victims in quick succession; this satisfied the temporal relationship requirement. Finally, the State established the motivational requirement through evidence that defendant went to the apartment intending to kill one of the victims and that he killed the others because they witnessed the murder.


OUTCOME: Defendant's death sentences were affirmed.

Cain v. Horne


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-08-0189-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


220 Ariz. 77; 202 P.3d 1178; 2009 Ariz. LEXIS 56



March 25, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Court of Appeals Division Two No. 2 CA-CV 07-0143. Maricopa County Superior Court No. CV2007-002986. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Bethany G. Hicks, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division Two 218 Ariz. 301, 183 P.3d 1269 (App. 2008).
Cain v. Horne, 218 Ariz. 301, 183 P.3d 1269, 2008 Ariz. App. LEXIS 77 (Ariz. Ct. App., 2008)



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County, REVERSED AND REMANDED. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division Two, VACATED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Maricopa Superior Court, Arizona, dismissed appellants', resident and others, complaint seeking to enjoin implementation of the school voucher programs against appellees, school superintendent and intervenors. The Arizona Court of Appeals held that the voucher programs did not violate Ariz. Const. art. 2, § 12 but did violate Ariz. Const. art. 9, § 10. The superintended and intervenors petitioned for review; appellants cross-petitioned.


OVERVIEW: The superintended and intervenors argued that the Aid Clause, Ariz. Const. art. 9, § 10 had to be interpreted just as the United States Supreme Court has interpreted the Establishment Clause of the United States Constitution, U.S. Const. amend. I, and that parental choice involved in signing the state checks over to a private or sectarian school saved the voucher programs from unconstitutionality. The supreme court found that the text and purpose of the Aid Clause supported the conclusion that the clause required a construction independent from that of the Religion Clause, Ariz. Const. art. 2, § 12. The Aid Clause encompassed more than did the Religion Clause. The Aid Clause was designed to protect the public fisc and to protect public schools. Both clauses prohibited certain appropriations of public money. The voucher programs transferred state funds directly from the state treasury to private schools. The Aid Clause did not permit the appropriations the voucher programs provided as it would amount to aid of private or sectarian schools, Ariz. Const. art. 9, § 10, and render the clause a nullity. The voucher programs violated Ariz. Const. art. 9, § 10.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the superior court was reversed and the court of appeals' opinion was vacated and remanded to the superior court for further proceedings.

Davis v. Agua Sierra Res., LLC


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-08-0163-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


220 Ariz. 108; 203 P.3d 506; 2009 Ariz. LEXIS 53; 553 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 32



March 19, 2009, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at, Remanded by Davis v. Agua Sierra Res., L.L.C., 2009 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 979 (2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Court of Appeals Division One No. 1 CA-CV 06-0806. Yavapai County Superior Court No. CV 20040716. Appeal from the Superior Court in Yavapai County. The Honorable David L. Mackey, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One 217 Ariz. 386, 174 P.3d 298 (App. 2008).
Davis v. Agua Sierra Res., LLC., 217 Ariz. 386, 174 P.3d 298, 2008 Ariz. App. LEXIS 5 (Ariz. Ct. App., 2008)



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court in Yavapai County. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, VACATED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff trustee filed a complaint against defendants, a company, a corporation, a partnership, and a family partnership, seeking to invalidate commercial water rights reservations associated with a ranch. The company separately filed a counterclaim. The superior court held a reservation invalid and entered judgment for the trustee. Defendants appealed, and the Arizona Court of Appeals vacated the trial court's judgment. Review was accepted.


OVERVIEW: The corporation bought certain land from a ranch. The deed reserved all mineral rights and commercial water rights but did not otherwise retain any ownership interest in the land. The corporation conveyed the property to the trustee. One-half interest in the commercial water rights for the ranch was conveyed to the company. The trustee granted the city an option to purchase the ranch and an adjacent ranch, but the trustee was unable to purchase the water rights from the company. The case involved the potential use of groundwater that had never been captured and put to reasonable use. The Groundwater Management Act (GMA), Ariz. Rev. Stat. §§ 45-401 to -704 (2003 & Supp. 2008) regulated any withdrawal and transportation of groundwater from the ranch to the city. The GMA did not recognize the existence of anything called a commercial water right. The company and its predecessors had not identified any pre-existing or current use of the groundwater underlying the ranch that was embraced by the reservation of commercial water rights. Moreover, the cases cited by the intermediate appellate court did not establish a severable right to the potential future use of groundwater.


OUTCOME: The supreme court vacated the opinion of the intermediate court of appeals and remanded so that the superior court could consider other properly preserved arguments.

 

83 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


Seisinger v. Siebel


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-08-0224-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


220 Ariz. 85; 203 P.3d 483; 2009 Ariz. LEXIS 55; 551 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 38



March 13, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Court of Appeals Division One. No. 1 CA-CV 07-0266. Maricopa County Superior Court. No. CV2004-015659. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Michael D. Jones, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One 219 Ariz. 163, 195 P.3d 200 (App. 2008).
Seisinger v. Siebel, 219 Ariz. 163, 195 P.3d 200, 2008 Ariz. App. LEXIS 96 (Ariz. Ct. App., 2008)



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County, VACATED AND REMANDED. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, VACATED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a medical malpractice action, defendant anesthesiologist appealed a judgment of the Court of Appeals of Arizona that reversed the dismissal of plaintiff patient's case, arguing that the appellate court erred in finding that Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 12-2604(A) violated the separation of powers doctrine.


OVERVIEW: The anesthesiologist filed a motion contending that the patient's expert witness, a retired anesthesiologist, did not meet the requirement of Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 12-2604(A) that an expert on the standard of care must have devoted a majority of his professional time in the year preceding the incident at issue to active clinical practice or teaching in the same specialty. The trial court agreed that the witness was unqualified and, when the patient failed to produce a qualified expert, dismissed the case. The appellate court reversed and held that § 12-2604(A) directly conflicted with Ariz. R. Evid. 702 by excluding experts qualified to testify under the rule and violated the separation of powers doctrine under Ariz. Const. art. 3 by fringing upon judicial authority to promulgate procedural rules. On review, the court concluded that the statute did not violate the separation of powers doctrine because, although it conflicted with Ariz. R. Evid. 702, the statute was substantive. It did not merely alter procedure but changed the substantive law as to what a plaintiff must prove in a medical malpractice action. However, the superior court erred in retroactively applying the statute.


OUTCOME: The opinion of the appellate court was vacated. The judgment of the superior court was vacated as well, and the case was remanded for further proceedings.

League of Ariz. Cities & Towns v. Martin


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-08-0379-SA


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


219 Ariz. 556; 201 P.3d 517; 2009 Ariz. LEXIS 41



February 3, 2009, Decided


DISPOSITION:  [***1] JURISDICTION ACCEPTED; RELIEF GRANTED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner organization filed a special action in the Arizona Supreme Court to express concern over the constitutionality of 2008 Ariz. Sess. Laws 285, naming as respondents the Governor of Arizona and the Arizona State Treasurer.


OVERVIEW: The Governor argued the by waiting to bring its action until November, the organization prevented prompt judicial resolution of the matter and exacerbated the already precarious budget situation. The supreme court found that the organization did not wait until an imminent deadline approached to file its special action. The delay in the case did not cause prejudice. The question remained whether § 47 identified and reduced something that met the definition of appropriation under Ariz. Const. art. 4, pt. 2, § 20. The supreme court found that it did not. The legislature did not expressly attach the assessed amount to any public revenue that it had previously set aside for the cities and towns. Because § 47 of 2008 Ariz. Sess. Laws 285 failed to identify any prior appropriation, it failed to meet the definition of an appropriation. Because § 47 did not reduce any identified prior appropriation, it violated Ariz. Const. art. 4, pt. 2, § 20, and was therefore unconstitutional. The organization could not be awarded attorneys' fees under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 12-348(A) (2003).


OUTCOME: Jurisdiction was accepted and relief granted to the organization.

 

85 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


State v. Stummer


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-07-0429-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


219 Ariz. 137; 194 P.3d 1043; 2008 Ariz. LEXIS 196; 540 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 15



October 9, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Court of Appeals Division One Nos. 1 CA-CR 06-0874. 1 CA-CR 06-0877 (Consolidated). Maricopa County Superior Court Nos. CR2006-006957-001 DT. CR2006-006958-001 DT. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable James H. Keppel, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, 217 Ariz. 188, 171 P.3d 1229 (App. 2007).
State v. Stummer, 217 Ariz. 188, 171 P.3d 1229, 2007 Ariz. App. LEXIS 224 (Ariz. Ct. App., 2007)



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County, VACATED AND REMANDED. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, VACATED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners were charged with violating Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-1422 (2005), which prohibited adult bookstores from remaining open during certain early morning hours. The trial court dismissed the charges. The Arizona Court of Appeals, Division One, reversed. Review was granted to resolve a conflict between the opinion in Empress Adult Video & Bookstore v. City of Tucson and the appellate court's opinion in this case.


OVERVIEW: The statute required adult bookstores to close for 53 hours each week: from 1:00 a.m. to 8:00 a.m. Monday through Saturday, and from 1:00 a.m. to noon on Sunday. The instant court concluded that petitioners established that their protected speech was burdened by a content-based regulation. The State, in turn, met its burden of demonstrating that the hours provision of § 13-1422 was designed to curb the secondary effects of speech, not to prohibit the speech itself. The State was required to establish that, during their early morning operation, adult bookstores disproportionately caused negative secondary effects and that these negative effects were or would be significantly lessened by closure during those hours. However, the State did not show that any substantial interests would be achieved less effectively without the bookstores' closure for 53 hours each week. Because this case was decided on motion to dismiss, the record contained no evidence of the significance of the infringement on speech, the effectiveness of the statute in reducing negative secondary effects, the nexus between the ends sought and the means employed, or the availability of alternative measures.


OUTCOME: The appellate court's opinion was vacated, and the case was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.

State v. Schmidt


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-08-0308-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


220 Ariz. 563; 208 P.3d 214; 2008 Ariz. LEXIS 246



September 26, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at, Petition granted by Ariz. v. Michael Dimetrius Schmidt, 2009 Ariz. LEXIS 137 (Ariz., Feb. 10, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Court of Appeals Division One No. 1 CA-CR 07-0935 PRPC. Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR1992-003695. Petition for Review from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Carey Snyder Hyatt, Judge. Order of the Court of Appeals, Division One.



DISPOSITION: Petition for Review from the Superior Court in Maricopa County, VACATED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Maricopa Superior Court, Arizona, revoked defendant's probation on his attempted sexual abuse count and instead of the presumptive sentence of ten years, the trial court imposed an aggravated sentence of 12.5 years. Defendant's petition for post-conviction relief was denied. The Arizona Court of Appeals denied review. Defendant petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court noted that when defendant committed his offenses, the relevant statute provided that a defendant's sentence could be increased based on twelve specific aggravating factors, Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-702(D)(1)-(12) (Supp. 1991). In addition to the twelve listed aggravating factors, the statute included a "catch-all" provision. The supreme court found that the use of the catch-all as the sole factor to increase a defendant's statutory maximum sentence violated due process, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, because it gave the sentencing court virtually unlimited post hoc discretion to determine whether the defendant's prior conduct was the functional equivalent of an element of the aggravated offense. When one or more clearly enumerated aggravators were found consistent with Apprendi, and they allowed imposition of an aggravated sentence under the relevant statute, the "elements" of the aggravated offense would have been identified with sufficient clarity to satisfy due process. Subsequent reliance on other factors embraced by a catch-all provision to justify a sentence up to the statutory maximum comported with the traditional discretionary role afforded judges in sentencing.


OUTCOME: The sentence was vacated and remanded.

State v. Armstrong


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-06-0443-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


218 Ariz. 451; 189 P.3d 378; 2008 Ariz. LEXIS 127



July 29, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Armstrong v. Arizona, 556 U.S. 1106, 129 S. Ct. 1581, 173 L. Ed. 2d 679, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 1913 (2009)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Stay denied by Armstrong v. Ryan, 2017 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 45485 (D. Ariz., Mar. 27, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Pima County Superior Court No. CR-61846. Appeal from the Superior Court in Pima County. The Honorable Christopher Browning, Judge.
State v. Armstrong, 208 Ariz. 345, 93 P.3d 1061, 2004 Ariz. LEXIS 90 (Ariz., 2004)



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court in Pima County, AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following resentencing, defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court in Pima County, Arizona, that imposed death sentences for defendant's convictions for murdering his sister and her fiance, arguing that the State failed to establish a motivational relationship between the murders to support the multiple murders aggravating circumstance under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(F)(8).


OVERVIEW: Defendant and his sister burglarized a home in Texas. When they learned the Oklahoma authorities were looking for them, they fled to Arizona. Defendant's sister desired to return to Oklahoma with her fiance, and she informed a friend that she planned to turn herself in to authorities and to disclose defendant's whereabouts. After the friend informed defendant of his sister's plans, defendant and the friend killed defendant's sister and her finance. Defendant was convicted of both murders and sentenced to death. After his original sentence was vacated, defendant was again sentenced to death after the jury found the multiple murders aggravating factor under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(F)(8). On review, the court held that the multiple murders aggravating circumstance was proper where defendant killed his sister and her fiance in order to prevent the sister from returning to Oklahoma and disclosing his involvement in another crime. Although defendant claimed that he killed the fiance because he hated him, such motivation was inextricably intertwined with his motivation for killing his sister, which was his desire not to be pursued by Oklahoma authorities.


OUTCOME: The death sentences were affirmed.

State v. Martinez


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-05-0507-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


218 Ariz. 421; 189 P.3d 348; 2008 Ariz. LEXIS 126



July 25, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Martinez v. Arizona, 129 S. Ct. 494, 172 L. Ed. 2d 364, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 8083 (U.S., Nov. 3, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Pima County Superior Court No. CR20031993. Appeal from the Superior Court in Pima County. The Honorable Howard L. Fell, Judge Pro Tempore.



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court in Pima County, Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court in Pima County (Arizona) convicting him of premeditated first-degree murder, felony murder, and kidnapping, and sentencing him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for shooting the victim to death after robbing him. On appeal, the court held that defendant's Sixth Amendment right to compel a witness to testify on his behalf was not violated by the trial court's failure to require one of defendant's companions on the night of the murder to testify because the companion had continued reason to fear prosecution, as his postconviction relief proceedings were still pending, and therefore he was entitled to invoke his Fifth Amendment right against self-incrimination. OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions and sentence were affirmed.

State v. Boggs


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-05-0174-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


180 P.3d 392; 2008 Ariz. LEXIS 48; 527 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 7



April 9, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable John Foreman, Judge (retired). Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR2002-009759.



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County, AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Superior Court in Maricopa County (Arizona) of three counts of murder and was sentenced to death. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the State did not prove aggravating factors necessary for the imposition of the death penalty. The supreme court disagreed. The evidence allowed the jury to find the pecuniary gain aggravator beyond a reasonable doubt. Defendant's confession clearly indicated his monetary motivation: defendant told a detective that money was his motivation. Moreover, the evidence demonstrated that money was taken from two registers and one victim's bank card was used in an attempt to withdraw money from an ATM. Defendant admitted that the victims were forced at gunpoint to lie down in the work area of the restaurant, ordered to remove everything from their pockets, ordered to march through the cooler into the back freezer with their hands interlaced on top of their heads, forced to kneel down, and then shot in rapid succession. All the murders involved a continuous course of criminal conduct. The evidence, including defendant's admissions, demonstrated that the victims were killed, at least in part, as a means of witness elimination so that they could not identify the perpetrators.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Harrod


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-05-0461-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


218 Ariz. 268; 183 P.3d 519; 2008 Ariz. LEXIS 22



February 14, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Harrod v. Arizona, 555 U.S. 830, 129 S. Ct. 38, 172 L. Ed. 2d 49, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 6592 (2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR1995-009046. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Ronald S. Reinstein, Judge (Retired). The Honorable Brian R. Hauser, Judge.
State v. Harrod, 204 Ariz. 567, 65 P.3d 948, 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 31 (Ariz., 2003)



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County, AFFIRMED AS MODIFIED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury for the Superior Court in Maricopa County (Arizona) found defendant guilty of the victim's murder and determined that he should be sentenced to death. He appealed. Eventually, the United States Supreme Court vacated the judgment and remanded the case to the supreme court, which remanded it to the trial court for resentencing. After the resentencing proceeding, the trial court again sentenced him to death by lethal injection. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: Because a suspect was still a target of the State's investigation, he demonstrated a reasonable ground to apprehend danger to himself from his being compelled to answer under the Sixth Amendment. Thus, the trial court did not abuse its discretion in excusing him from testifying because he legitimately invoked the Fifth Amendment privilege against self-incrimination on the questions proffered by defendant. Also, defendant did not have a constitutional or statutory right to present residual doubt evidence at his resentencing proceeding, including the results of a polygraph examination and assertions of innocence during allocution. Furthermore, overwhelming evidence supported the Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(F)(5) (Supp. 1988) aggravating factor that the motivation for the murder was financial gain. In light of the compelling aggravating circumstance, the mitigation evidence that included family support; the divorce of his parents, his father's alcoholism, and his father's mental abuse; and his good character, which deserved less weight in a case involving a murder planned in advance, failed to rise to a level that would call for leniency. Thus, the death sentence was properly imposed.


OUTCOME: The supreme court modified defendant's sentence to permit him to choose execution either by lethal injection or lethal gas; as modified, the supreme court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

State v. Price


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-06-0435-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


217 Ariz. 182; 171 P.3d 1223; 2007 Ariz. LEXIS 140



December 3, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Court of Appeals, Division One, No. 1 CA-CR 04-0508. Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR 2002-017550. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Gregory H. Martin, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, 213 Ariz. 550, 145 P.3d 647 (2006).
State v. Price, 213 Ariz. 550, 145 P.3d 647, 2006 Ariz. App. LEXIS 134 (Ariz. Ct. App., 2006)



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County, VACATED IN PART AND REMANDED. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, VACATED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury convicted defendant of drive-by shooting, aggravated assault of passengers in the car, and two other counts. The trial court aggravated defendant's sentences and imposed sentences totaling thirty-one years. The Court of Appeals, Division One, Arizona, affirmed the sentence but relied on a different rationale than the trial court. The instant court accepted review to determine whether the sentence was aggravated in violation of Apprendi.


OVERVIEW: The jury did not find and defendant did not admit any of the aggravating factors that subjected him to a sentence above the statutory presumptive sentence. The instant court found that the trial court erred when it increased defendant's sentence based on the judge's findings that defendant had committed an unrelated homicide and was a danger to the community. The trial court's comment that defendant had a juvenile record was not a finding that juvenile adjudications should serve as an aggravating factor. A reviewing court should not assume the role of a sentencing judge and seek out new aggravating circumstances, not found below, to save the constitutionally flawed sentence. The trial court denied defendant the right to have certain facts decided by a jury beyond a reasonable doubt, in violation of the Fifth and Sixth Amendments to the U.S. Constitution. Because this unconstitutional procedure went to the foundation of defendant's case, there was fundamental error. Finally, defendant established prejudicial error because the instant court was not convinced that a reasonable jury would have necessarily concluded that he committed the homicide or was a danger to the community.


OUTCOME: The convictions were affirmed. The opinion of the court of appeals was vacated in part. The sentence was vacated. The matter was remanded to the trial court for resentencing.

State Farm Ins. Cos. v. Premier Manufactured Sys.


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-06-0338-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


217 Ariz. 222; 172 P.3d 410; 2007 Ariz. LEXIS 142



December 3, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Court of Appeals, Division One, No. 1 CA-CV 04-0465. Maricopa County Superior Court No. CV2001-016394. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Ruth Harris Hilliard, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, 213 Ariz. 419, 142 P.3d 1232 (App. 2006).
State Farm Ins. Cos. v. Premier Manufactured Sys., 213 Ariz. 419, 142 P.3d 1232, 2006 Ariz. App. LEXIS 103 (Ariz. Ct. App., 2006)



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County, AFFIRMED. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff insurer, as a subrogee, brought suit alleging that defendant manufacturer or defendant distributing company was strictly liable in tort for distributing a defective product. The distributing company failed to respond to the complaint, and a default judgment was entered. The superior court denied the insurer's motion to find joint and several liability. The Arizona Court of Appeals affirmed. The insurer sought review.


OVERVIEW: The insured discovered that a leak in his water filtration system had damaged his home and personal property. The insurer paid the homeowner for the loss. The insurer and the manufacturer entered into a stipulated judgment, which stated that the leak had been caused by either a design or manufacturing defect. The judgment provided that the distributing company was 75 percent at fault, and the manufacturer was 25 percent at fault. The distributing company had gone out of business; therefore, the insurer could recover only 25 percent of the damages. The case required the supreme court to decide whether liability among defendants in strict products liability actions was joint and several, or several only. The supreme court concluded that the legislature abolished, with its amendment in 1987 of Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 12-2506, joint and several liability for participants in a defective product's chain of distribution Further, this statute did not offend Ariz. Const. art. 18, § 6. The legislative abolition of joint and several liability extended to strict products liability actions. In such cases, liability was several only and fault had to be apportioned among defendants.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the judgment of the superior court and the opinion of the intermediate appellate court.

SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee wife sought a dissolution of marriage. The superior court ended the marriage by entering a decree that included the parties' settlement agreement. Appellant husband later sought, pursuant to Ariz. R. Civ. P. 60(c), to set aside provisions of the decree. The Arizona Court of Appeals vacated the superior court judgment. Petition for review was granted.


OVERVIEW: The decree required the husband to pay child support, attorneys' fees, and spousal maintenance. The husband failed to fulfill some of his obligations. The husband was declared disabled. Subsequently, the husband moved under Ariz. R. Civ. P. 60(c) to set aside provisions of the decree, alleging that his support and maintenance obligations were excessive in light of his reduced income. In vacating the trial court's decision, the intermediate appellate court had determined that the husband was entitled to an evidentiary hearing. At issue on review was whether a statutorily non-modifiable spousal maintenance provision in a decree of dissolution of marriage was subject to termination under Ariz. R. Civ. P. 60(c)(5). The husband claimed that despite any statute purporting to divest jurisdiction to modify or terminate the maintenance provision, a court nonetheless retained equitable power to grant relief by terminating the maintenance provision at issue. The spousal maintenance agreements made non-modifiable pursuant to Ariz. Rev. Stat. §§ 25-319(C) and 25-317(G) were not subject to modification or termination, nor was relief from such provisions available under Rule 60(c)(5).


OUTCOME: Paragraphs 17 to 27 of the intermediate appellate court's opinion was vacated, and the judgment of the trial court was affirmed.
