In re Gen. Adjudication of All Rights to Use Water


Arizona Supreme Court No. WC-07-0002-IR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


217 Ariz. 276; 173 P.3d 440; 2007 Ariz. LEXIS 138



November 30, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by, Request granted In re Gen. Adjudication of All Rights to Use Water, 217 Ariz. 276, 173 P.3d 440, 2007 Ariz. LEXIS 137 (Ariz., 2007)
Subsequent appeal at In re General Adjudication of All Rights To Use Water In the Gila River Sys. & Source, 2010 Ariz. LEXIS 12 (Ariz., Feb. 19, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Maricopa County Superior Court No. W-1, W-2, W-3, W-4 (Consolidated Cases) Contested Case No. W1-208. Interlocutory Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Eddward Ballinger, Jr., Judge.
In re General Adjudication of All Rights to Use Water in the Gila River System & Source, 212 Ariz. 64, 127 P.3d 882, 2006 Ariz. LEXIS 19 (Ariz., 2006)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a water rights dispute, the Pascua Yaqui Tribe filed objections to a settlement agreement among the various parties, including the Tohono O'odham Nation. The adjudication court summarily disposed of the objections. The Superior Court in Maricopa County (Arizona) denied the Tribe's motion for reconsideration. Following the adjudication court's rejection of the Tribe's objections, the settling parties and the Tribe sought interlocutory review.


OVERVIEW: The case arose from the ongoing adjudication of water rights and the impact of legislation facilitating the resolution of tribal water claims. A Special Procedural Order providing for the Approval of Federal Water Rights Settlements, Including Those of Indian Tribes (Special Order) was previously entered, establishing circumstances under which special settlement proceedings could be initiated; it set forth the process by which parties could apply to initiate special proceedings; it allowed the parties to object to court approval of the settlement; and it provided for resolution of objections and approval of the settlement adjudication by the court. The Tribe asked that the Special Order be found invalid insofar as it limited the adjudication court's obligation to consider objections that did not turn on the settlement agreement itself. The supreme court, however, determined that none of the Tribe's issues had merit because the settlement determined only the water rights of the Nation; it did not provide the Nation with any federal reserved rights; it restricted the amounts of groundwater the Nation could pump; and it expressly reserved all rights and claims of the Tribe.


OUTCOME: The supreme court accepted interlocutory review, and it affirmed the judgment and decree of the adjudication court in its entirety.

 

98 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


Kromko v. Ariz. Bd. of Regents


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-07-0018-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


216 Ariz. 190; 165 P.3d 168; 2007 Ariz. LEXIS 74; 511 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 48



August 9, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Court of Appeals, Division One, No. 1 CA-CV 04-0250. Maricopa County Superior Court No. CV03-021650. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Rebecca A. Albrecht, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, 213 Ariz. 607, 146 P.3d 1016 (2006).



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County, AFFIRMED. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, VACATED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff students sued defendant State and Board of Regents, alleging that a tuition increase violated Ariz. Const. art. XI, § 6. The Court of Appeals of Arizona, Division One, reversed the superior court's judgment dismissing the claim, holding that Ariz. Rev. Stat. 12-820.01(A) did not immunize the Board from suit and that the students' complaint stated a claim upon which relief could be granted. The Board's petition for review was granted.


OVERVIEW: The issue what whether there were judicially discoverable and manageable standards for determining when tuition was constitutionally excessive. The constitutional language did not provide a standards as case law interpreting Ariz. Const. art. XI, § 6 provided no guidance on how to measure whether tuition at some level above zero was "as nearly free as possible." Although the legislature had provided guidance in past statutory enactments, no current statutes provided standards by which a court could have measured whether tuition was too high. The Ariz. Rev. Stat. 15-1626(A)(13) requirement that the Board adopt an annual operating budget provided no guidance as to an appropriate operating budget or the proportions to be satisfied by tuition. Rather, the ultimate size of the budget was left to the Board's discretion. The court could not assess whether the cost of tuition was as nearly free as possible in the absence of an initial policy determination of a kind clearly reserved to the Legislature and the Board. Since there were no judicially discoverable and manageable standards, the funding decisions by the other branches of government were insulated from judicial review.


OUTCOME: The lower court's opinion was vacated insofar as it held that the complaint against the Board should not have been dismissed. The superior court's judgment was affirmed.

 

99 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


State v. Velazquez


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-04-0361-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


216 Ariz. 300; 166 P.3d 91; 2007 Ariz. LEXIS 75; 512 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 18



August 9, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Velazquez v. Arizona, 553 U.S. 1014, 128 S. Ct. 2078, 170 L. Ed. 2d 811, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 3478 (2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR2001-014970. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Jeffrey S. Cates, Judge.
Velazquez v. Schriro, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 55370 (D. Ariz., July 27, 2007)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a jury conviction from the Superior Court in Maricopa County (Arizona) for seven counts of child abuse and one count of first-degree murder, for which he received the death penalty plus 60 years. The jury found the existence of three aggravating factors in former Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(G)(2), (6), (9) (Supp. 2001): a previous conviction for a serious offense, especial cruelty, and the victim's being under 15 years of age.


OVERVIEW: Defendant physically abused his girlfriend's 3-year-old and 20-month old daughters, inflicting skull fractures and causing the death of the younger child, whose body he weighted with a cement block and dropped into a canal. Conducting an independent review, the court found that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in excusing for cause six venire persons who were biased in favor of the death penalty or by empaneling two others who were equivocal. In the aggravation phase, the jury was presumed to rely on the trial court's instructions not to use the victim's age as a basis for two separate aggravators. A "previously convicted of a serious offense" aggravator was properly imposed under former Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(G)(2) because defendant confessed to abusing the surviving sister separately from killing the younger sister. The trial court's penalty phase jury instructions did not mischaracterize the sentencing decisionmaking process or restrict the use of mitigation evidence. Although the prosecutor improperly inpugned a defense mental health expert's anticipated testimony, defendant was not prejudiced because the expert never testified.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions and sentences.

 

100 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


State v. Pandeli


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-06-0143-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


215 Ariz. 514; 161 P.3d 557; 2007 Ariz. LEXIS 71; 508 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 22



July 12, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Pandeli v. Arizona, 552 U.S. 1190, 128 S. Ct. 1228, 170 L. Ed. 2d 78, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 1397 (2008)
Post-conviction proceeding at State v. Pandeli, 2011 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 417 (2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR93-08116. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Robert L. Gottsfield, Judge.
State v. Pandeli, 204 Ariz. 569, 65 P.3d 950, 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 30 (Ariz., 2003)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant was convicted of first degree murder. On remand for a new sentencing hearing, a jury in the Superior Court in Maricopa County (Arizona) determined that appellant should have been sentenced to death. Appellant challenged the sentence.


OVERVIEW: The trial court did not fundamentally err by delaying a ruling on the scope of the State's penalty phase rebuttal as appellant was able to conduct voir dire. The Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(F)(2) aggravating factor was properly applied where the State offered only a certified copy of a prior murder conviction, nothing in the Sixth Amendment prohibited the submission of that factor to the jury, and use of the factor did not violate double jeopardy. The Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(F)(6) aggravating factor was not unconstitutional or overly broad as judicial opinions had narrowed its construction and the trial court's jury instructions sufficiently defined heinous and depraved. Photographs of the victim's body, a Confederate flag found in appellant's van, and appellant's tattoos were properly admitted to prove the § 13-703(F)(6) aggravating factor as they were relevant and not unduly prejudicial. The State did not improperly limit the type of mitigation evidence the jury could consider in the penalty phase as it merely suggested that the mitigation evidence was entitled to minimal weight and any potential error was cured by the jury instructions. The remaining claims also failed.


OUTCOME: The sentence was affirmed.

 

101 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


State v. Andriano


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-05-0005-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


215 Ariz. 497; 161 P.3d 540; 2007 Ariz. LEXIS 70; 508 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 3



July 9, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Andriano v. Ariz., 128 S. Ct. 297, 169 L. Ed. 2d 212, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 10610 (U.S., Oct. 1, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR2000-096032. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Brian K. Ishikawa, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court in Maricopa County (Arizona) convicting her of one count of first-degree murder and sentencing her to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for slitting her husband's throat after poisoning him. On appeal, the court held that the trial court did not err by admitting evidence of defendant's attempts to obtain insurance policies on her husband's life because it was admissible under Ariz. R. Evid. 404(b) to show defendant's plan, knowledge, and intent to kill her husband, and to show that she premeditated her husband's death. The significant probative value of the evidence was not substantially outweighed by the danger of unfair prejudice. The trial court did not err by admitting evidence of defendant's extramarital affair because it was admissible under Ariz. R. Evid. 404(b) as evidence of defendant's motive for killing her husband and to rebut the defense theory that defendant was a domestic violence victim who killed her husband in self-defense. The trial court did not err by failing to sua sponte instruct the jury on the lesser-included offenses of second-degree murder and manslaughter because defendant claimed that she acted in self-defense and the jury was instructed on self-defense. There was no evidence supporting the lesser-included offense instructions.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction and sentence were affirmed.

 

102 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


State v. Garza


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-04-0343-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


216 Ariz. 56; 163 P.3d 1006; 2007 Ariz. LEXIS 68



June 29, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Garza v. Arizona, 552 U.S. 1107, 128 S. Ct. 890, 169 L. Ed. 2d 743, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 215 (2008)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Motion denied by Garza v. Ryan, 2016 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 119019 (D. Ariz., Sept. 2, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR1999-017624. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Gregory H. Martin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury in the Superior Court of Maricopa County (Arizona) convicted appellant of two counts of first degree murder and determined that he should be sentenced to life imprisonment for one murder and death for the other. After the superior court imposed the recommended sentences, an automatic notice of appeal was filed pursuant to Ariz. R. Crim. P. 31.2(b).


OVERVIEW: The superior court did not abuse its discretion in allowing the prosecutor to speak to the panel first during voir dire. The prosecutor's comments during the State's voir dire simply described the role of the prosecutor in jury selection. It was not fundamental error to allow the State to inquire if jurors would follow the law. The superior court had cured any error arising from an initial time limit for voir dire. The claims as to three jurors lacked merit where they had stated that their opinion of the death penalty depended on the circumstances. Appellant had waived any Batson claims by failing to challenge the State's peremptory strikes and failed to show that the empanelled jury was not impartial. The State had not improperly withheld exculpatory evidence where there was no showing that any sheriff's office records were withheld and a state agency was not involved in the investigation of the murders. Admission of jailhouse telephone conversation was proper as it was probative of appellant's consciousness of guilt and was a party admission under Ariz. R. Evid. 801(d)(2)(A). Appellant's claims as to the sentencing phase and the propriety of the death sentence also lacked merit.


OUTCOME: The convictions and sentences were affirmed.

 

103 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


State v. Morris


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-05-0267-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


215 Ariz. 324; 160 P.3d 203; 2007 Ariz. LEXIS 65



June 18, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Morris v. Ariz., 2008 U.S. LEXIS 296 (U.S., Jan. 7, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR2003-011506. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Douglas L. Rayes, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court in Maricopa County (Arizona) convicting him of five counts of first-degree murder and sentencing him to death on each count.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for strangling the five female victims to death. On appeal, the court held that the trial court did not err by admitting defendant's statements concerning the deaths of two of the victims because sufficient evidence independent of defendant's incriminating statements established corpus delicti for the victims deaths, including the following: (1) both victims died under suspicious circumstances and were discovered naked in the same alley; (2) drag marks near both bodies indicated that they had been moved; (3) DNA on panties and overalls found in defendant's camper matched one victim's DNA, DNA on panties found in the camper matched the other victim's DNA, and the victim had defendant's DNA under her fingernails; (4) hair extensions similar to one victim was found in the camper; and (5) defendant was carrying one victim's driver's license, social security card, and check card when he was arrested. Defendant's absence during the jury commissioner's prescreening of prospective jurors to determine whether they could serve on a lengthy trial did not violate defendant's right to be present at all stages of the criminal proceeding.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions and sentences were affirmed.

 

104 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


State v. Smith


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-04-0208-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


215 Ariz. 221; 159 P.3d 531; 2007 Ariz. LEXIS 62; 505 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 36



May 31, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Smith v. Arizona, 552 U.S. 985, 128 S. Ct. 466, 169 L. Ed. 2d 326, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 11482 (2007)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Certificate of appealability granted, in part, Certificate of appealability denied, in part, Motion granted by, in part, Motion denied by, in part Smith v. Ryan, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 38511 (D. Ariz., Mar. 24, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR 95116. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Thomas W. O'Toole, Judge.
State v. Smith, 131 Ariz. 29, 638 P.2d 696, 1981 Ariz. LEXIS 274 (Ariz., 1981)



DISPOSITION: SENTENCES AFFIRMED AS MODIFIED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In 2004, in the Maricopa County Superior Court (Arizona), defendant was resentenced to death for two murders committed in 1975 and 1976. An automatic notice of appeal was filed under Ariz. R. Crim. P. 26.15 and 31.2(b) and Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-4031 (2001). The supreme court had jurisdiction under Ariz. Const. art. 6, § 5(3), and Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-4031.


OVERVIEW: The victims died of asphyxiation after having their noses and mouths filled with dirt and taped shut. That type of asphyxiation would undoubtedly cause mental anguish and physical pain. At a minimum, defendant should have known pain and anguish would occur. Thus, the State proved that the murders were cruel under former Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-454(E)(6). Also, the State proved the existence of two other aggravating factors, including that he was convicted of another offense that carried a sentence of life imprisonment or death and that his prior convictions involved the use or threat of violence under former Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-454(E)(1), (2). The focus of defendant's mitigation evidence related to his mental health. But, the role of his mental health in the commission of the murders, and thus the quality of the mitigation was called into serious question by testimony that he could have controlled his impulses and that he likely knew what he was doing and that it was wrong. After reviewing the record, under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703.04(B), the mitigation presented was not sufficiently substantial to warrant leniency given the nature of the crimes and the aggravating factors.


OUTCOME: The supreme court vacated the lethal injection portion of defendant's sentences and ordered that defendant be permitted to choose either lethal injection or lethal gas, as long as he did so at least 20 days before the execution date. If defendant failed to choose, the death penalty would be carried out by lethal injection. As modified, the supreme court affirmed defendant's death sentences.

 

105 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


State v. Tucker


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-05-0162-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


215 Ariz. 298; 160 P.3d 177; 2007 Ariz. LEXIS 64



May 13, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Tucker v. Arizona, 128 S. Ct. 296, 169 L. Ed. 2d 211, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 10884 (U.S., 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR1999-015293. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Jeffrey A. Hotham, Judge.
State v. Tucker, 205 Ariz. 157, 68 P.3d 110, 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 62 (Ariz., 2003)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Maricopa County Superior Court (Arizona) convicted defendant of, inter alia, three counts of first degree murder. The trial judge sentenced defendant to death for each of the murders. The supreme court reversed the sentence and remanded the case to the trial court for resentencing. On remand, a newly impaneled jury sentenced defendant to death for each of the murders. The case was before the supreme court on an automatic appeal.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty of sexual assault and sentenced to 25 years to life imprisonment. Thus, the aggravator under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(F)(1) was proven beyond a reasonable doubt. Also, the supreme court found in its independent review that the first victim was conscious when she was bound and strangled. Furthermore, her injuries indicated that she suffered extreme physical pain when she was bludgeoned, sexually assaulted, and strangled. Accordingly, the State established beyond a reasonable doubt that her murder was especially cruel under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(F)(6) (Supp. 1999). In addition, the record demonstrated that all three murders occurred on the same day and in the same apartment. Thus, the State proved the multiple murders aggravator in Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(F)(8) (Supp 1999) beyond a reasonable doubt. Finally, defendant's narcissistic personality disorder did not rise to the level of a statutory mitigating circumstance. Thus, defendant's mitigation evidence of his young age and his lack of a prior criminal record was not sufficiently substantial to warrant leniency in light of the aggravating factors. Thus, defendant's death sentences were upheld.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed defendant's three death sentences.

 

106 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


Forty-Seventh Legislature v. Napolitano


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-06-0079-SA


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


213 Ariz. 482; 143 P.3d 1023; 2006 Ariz. LEXIS 105; 486 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 36



September 12, 2006, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] JURISDICTION ACCEPTED; RELIEF GRANTED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a special action pursuant to Ariz. R.P. Spec. Actions 1, petitioners, the 47th Legislature, the State Senate President, and Speaker of the House of Representatives, challenged the validity of respondent Governor's item veto of a portion of H.B. 2661, § 5, 47th Leg., 2d Reg. Sess. (Ariz. 2006), that adopted a substantive change to Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 41-771 (2004) by adding a new class of employees to those exempt from the state merit system.


OVERVIEW: The Legislature passed H.B. 2661 as an emergency measure in order to grant state employees a pay raise under H.B. 2661, § 1, and appropriated money for the employee salary adjustments under H.B. 2661, § 6. H.B. 2661, § 5 amended Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 41-771 and exempted certain employees from the state merit system. The Governor item vetoed a portion of H.B. 2661, § 5. The Legislature challenged the validity of the veto, arguing that H.B. 2661, § 5 was not an "item of appropriation of money"; thus the Governor's item veto power under Ariz. Const. art. V, § 7 did not extend to H.B. 2661, § 5. The court held that it had jurisdiction because the issues presented were purely legal issues of public importance, likely to recur, and not political questions. The court also found that the Legislature had standing because it had alleged a direct institutional injury. The court concluded that H.B. 2661, § 5 was not an "item of appropriation of money" within the governor's veto power in Ariz. Const. art V, § 7 because H.B. 2661, § 5 failed to set aside a certain sum of public revenue from the general fund or from some other specific limited funding source; thus the Governor's veto was invalid.


OUTCOME: The court held that because H.B. 2661, § 5, was not an appropriation, the Governor's item veto of that provision exceeded her constitutional authority and was invalid. Accordingly, the court ordered that H.B. 2661, § 5 be given full force and effect.

 

107 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


State v. Hampton


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-03-0033-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


213 Ariz. 167; 140 P.3d 950; 2006 Ariz. LEXIS 104; 485 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 34



August 15, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Hampton v. Arizona, 549 U.S. 1132, 127 S. Ct. 972, 166 L. Ed. 2d 738, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 334 (2007)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Stay denied by Hampton v. Ryan, 2016 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 88676 (D. Ariz., July 8, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR2001-008991. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable John Foreman, Judge.
State v. Hampton, 208 Ariz. 241, 92 P.3d 871, 2004 Ariz. LEXIS 75 (Ariz., 2004)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Maricopa County Superior Court (Arizona) convicting him of two counts of first-degree murder and one count of manslaughter in violation of Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-1103(A)(5) (2001) and sentencing him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for shooting the two victims to death. One of those victims was pregnant, and as a result of her death her fetus also died. On appeal, the court held that defendant's Eighth Amendment rights were not violated as the jury for the guilt phase was death-qualified, even though the jury was allegedly incorrectly instructed that it would not decide aggravating factors or sentencing, because the guilt-phase jury played no role in sentencing, as a second sentencing-phase jury was selected, and therefore the guilt-phase jury could not have been misled into thinking that responsibility for the determination of the proper sentence lay elsewhere. The trial court did not err by admitting into evidence photographs of the victims because they showed the nature and placement of the victims' injuries and therefore were relevant to corroborate the testimony of the State's witnesses. Photographs of the fetus were relevant to both the manslaughter offense and the multiple homicides aggravator. Defendant's argument that the fetal manslaughter statute, § 13-1103(A)(5), applied only in cases in which the mother did not die was rejected.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions and sentences were affirmed.

 

108 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


State v. McGill


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-04-0405-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


213 Ariz. 147; 140 P.3d 930; 2006 Ariz. LEXIS 103; 485 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 59



August 14, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by McGill v. Arizona, 549 U.S. 1324, 127 S. Ct. 1914, 167 L. Ed. 2d 570, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 3839 (2007)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Motion denied by McGill v. Ryan, 2015 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 40363 (D. Ariz., Mar. 30, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR2003-005315. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Frank T. Galati, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Maricopa County Superior Court (Arizona) that convicted him of the first-degree premeditated murder of one victim, the attempted first-degree murder of the victim's girlfriend, arson, and the endangerment of other occupants in the building under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-1201.A; and sentenced him to death based on the aggravators in Ariz. Rev. Stat. §§ 13-703.F.2; 13-703.F.3; and 13-703.F.6 (20001).


OVERVIEW: Defendant asserted claims of error pertaining to jury selection, double jeopardy, and the sufficiency of the evidence to support his endangerment convictions and the cruel and heinous aggravator finding. The record showed that defendant poured gasoline on the victims and set them on fire because he was angry about their accusations about a stolen shotgun. The court held that defendant's conviction for endangerment was supported by sufficient evidence that showed he was aware that other people were in the small apartment building, and thus was aware of and disregarded a substantial risk that his actions would place others at risk of imminent death or physical injury. The court held the cruelty aggravator was supported by evidence that showed defendant included styrofoam in the gas so that it would stick to the victims and cause greater pain. The court found that defendant's sentence for endangerment and the use of the same conduct to establish his eligibility for the death penalty under the aggravator in Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703.F.3 did not violate the Double Jeopardy Clause because each offense contained an element not contained in the other, thus they were 2 separate offenses.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions and sentences, including the capital sentence. Defendant conceded that his arguments regarding the constitutionality of Arizona's death penalty scheme had been previously rejected by the court. The court noted that the mitigation evidence was not sufficient to call for leniency.

State v. Roque


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-03-0355-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


213 Ariz. 193; 141 P.3d 368; 2006 Ariz. LEXIS 102; 485 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 16



August 14, 2006, Filed

Post-conviction relief denied at, Petition granted by State v. Roque, 2014 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 1132 (Sept. 22, 2014)
Post-conviction relief denied at, Petition granted by State v. Roque, 2015 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 286 (Mar. 3, 2015)
Post-conviction relief dismissed at State v. Roque, 2015 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 543 (Apr. 21, 2015)
Post-conviction relief dismissed at State v. Roque, 2016 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 965 (July 29, 2016)
Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Roque, 2016 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 1432 (Nov. 15, 2016)
Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Roque, 2016 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 1434 (Nov. 15, 2016)
Post-conviction relief dismissed at, Petition granted by State v. Roque, 2017 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 1047 (Aug. 3, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR 2001-095385. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Mark F. Aceto, Judge.



DISPOSITION: CONVICTIONS AFFIRMED; SENTENCE REDUCED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction in the Maricopa County Superior Court (Arizona) of first degree murder, attempted first degree murder, reckless endangerment, and three counts of drive-by shooting. He was sentenced to death for the murder under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(F)(2), (3) (Supp. 2003).


OVERVIEW: The day after the 9-11 tragedy, defendant drove around and shot at people who appeared to be of middle-eastern descent. He killed one person. The court affirmed the convictions and reversed as to the death penalty and held: (1) excusing jurors for cause because of their uncertainty about the death penalty was proper; (2) while the trial court erred in ruling that Ariz. R. Crim. P. 15.1(a)(3) required only an expert "written report or statement," because the defense rejected the trial court's attempt to resolve the discovery dispute the court could not assess the potential prejudice; (4) the judge did not err in precluding defendant's sister's statement regarding what her grandmother said about defendant's mother's mental illness; (5) the trial judge did not err on the State's counterclaim in concluding that acts constituting attempted robbery under California law did not necessarily constitute an attempted robbery under Arizona law; and (6) given defendant's predisposition to mental illness and below-average IQ, balanced against a single Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(F)(3) (Supp. 2003) aggravating factor there was a question whether a sentence of death was warranted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions and non-capital sentences. The court vacated the sentence of death.

 

110 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


State v. Ellison


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-04-0073-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


213 Ariz. 116; 140 P.3d 899; 2006 Ariz. LEXIS 100; 485 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 44



August 9, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Ellison v. Arizona, 127 S. Ct. 506, 166 L. Ed. 2d 377, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 8126 (U.S., Oct. 30, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Mohave County Superior Court No. CR-99-0187. Appeal from the Superior Court in Mohave County. The Honorable Robert R. Moon, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court in Mohave County (Arizona) convicting him of two counts of first-degree murder and one count of first-degree burglary and sentencing him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted, along with his accomplice, of strangling the two victims to death while burglarizing their home. On appeal, the court held that defendant's right to counsel during his interrogation was not violated because defendant's statement "I think I might want an attorney" was an equivocal request for counsel and therefore the detectives were not required to stop questioning him. There was no basis to conclude that the trial court abused its discretion by determining that defendant's statements were voluntary because the detective testified that no express or implied promises of leniency were made, it did not appear that defendant's will was overborne by any promises of leniency, defendant maintained that his accomplice was the ringleader and that he acted only under duress, and he admitted being familiar with and understood his Miranda rights. Defendant's argument that the trial court judge was improperly biased was rejected because defense counsel failed to make a motion under Ariz. R. Crim. P. 10.1. The court further held that the aggravating circumstances that the jury found warranted a death sentence were supported by the evidence.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions and sentence were affirmed.

 

111 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


State v. Grell


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-01-0275-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


212 Ariz. 516; 135 P.3d 696; 2006 Ariz. LEXIS 75; 479 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 12



June 6, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Grell v. Arizona, 550 U.S. 937, 127 S. Ct. 2246, 167 L. Ed. 2d 1095, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 5226 (2007)
Appeal after remand at, Decision reached on appeal by State v. Grell, 2013 Ariz. LEXIS 3 (Ariz., Jan. 9, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR 1999-095294. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Barbara M. Jarrett, Judge.
State v. Grell, 205 Ariz. 57, 66 P.3d 1234, 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 28 (Ariz., 2003)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED IN PART; SENTENCE VACATED; REMANDED FOR RESENTENCING.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment from the Superior Court in Maricopa County, which convicted him of first degree murder in 2000, following a bench trial, on stipulated facts. After an aggravation and mitigation hearing, the judge sentenced appellant to death.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of first degree murder, in the death of his two-year old daughter. The trial court accepted the expert's diagnosis that appellant suffered from an anti-social personality disorder. However, finding no mitigation sufficiently substantial to call for leniency, the judge sentenced appellant to death. The trial could ruled that appellant failed to satisfy his burden of proving mental retardation by clear and convincing evidence. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the application of Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703.02 tiered procedure did not deprive appellant of his rights. The court ruled that requiring appellant to prove mental retardation by clear and convincing evidence in the initial retardation hearing did not violate constitutional standards. The trial court did not abuse its discretion by allowing the experts' testimony. However, the court concluded that the trial court erred in determining that a jury need not determine mental retardation as a bar to execution. The court held that appellant was entitled to a jury sentencing. Therefore, the sentence was vacated. The case was remanded for sentencing in accordance with §§ 13-703 and 13-703.01.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's finding that appellant did not prove mental retardation. The case was remanded for resentencing by a jury.

 

112 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


State v. Berger


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-05-0101-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


212 Ariz. 473; 134 P.3d 378; 2006 Ariz. LEXIS 58; 478 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 17



May 10, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Berger v. Arizona, 549 U.S. 1252, 127 S. Ct. 1370, 167 L. Ed. 2d 159, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 2676 (Feb. 26, 2007)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Magistrate's recommendation at Berger v. Ryan, 2011 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 74460 (D. Ariz., July 11, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals, Division One, No. 1 CA-CR 03-0243. Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR2002-013657. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Ruth H. Hilliard, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, 209 Ariz. 386, 103 P.3d 298 (2004).
State v. Berger, 209 Ariz. 386, 103 P.3d 298, 2004 Ariz. App. LEXIS 182 (Ariz. Ct. App., Dec. 14, 2004)



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County, AFFIRMED. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, VACATED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of 20 separate counts of sexual exploitation of a minor under the age of 15 and sentenced to 20 consecutive 10-year prison terms. A divided Arizona Court of Appeals panel affirmed his convictions and sentences. Defendant petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: Each count for which defendant was sentenced was based on a different video or photo image, the images involved some 15 different child victims, and defendant had accumulated the images over a six-year period. In comparing the gravity of defendant's crime and the severity of the punishment, the supreme court focused on whether a 10-year sentence was disproportionate for a conviction of possessing child pornography involving children younger than fifteen. The supreme court could not conclude that the sentence was grossly disproportionate. The 10-year sentence imposed for each offense was consistent with the State's penological goal of deterring the production and possession of child pornography. The supreme court also did not accept defendant's assertion that his crimes were victimless merely because he did not touch or even photograph any children himself. The supreme court noted the production of the images defendant possessed required the abuse of children.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the sentences.

 

113 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


State v. Johnson


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-03-0420-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


212 Ariz. 425; 133 P.3d 735; 2006 Ariz. LEXIS 60



May 9, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Johnson v. Arizona, 127 S. Ct. 559, 166 L. Ed. 2d 415, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 8432 (U.S., Nov. 6, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR 01-01604. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Thomas W. O'Toole, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed an automatic appeal from a judgment of the Maricopa County Superior Court (Arizona) that convicted him of first-degree murder, assisting a criminal syndicate or criminal street gang, burglary in the first degree, and armed robbery and that sentenced him to death for the murder and to consecutive, aggravated terms on the non-capital charges based on 3 aggravating factors in Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703.F(2), (3), and (6).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for killing a woman in order to keep her from testifying against his fellow gang member on armed robbery charges. Defendant claimed the trial court erred by failing to sever Count 2, the gang offense, from the other crimes charged, and he challenged the aggravating factors and the jury instructions as to those factors. The court held that the trial court did not err by refusing to sever Count 2 because the evidence of gang affiliation was admissible to establish motive and identity for defendant's other crimes and the trial court took appropriate steps to reduce any prejudice that could have resulted from joinder. The court found that strong and uncontroverted evidence supported witness elimination as a motive for the victim's murder and that the fact that the victim was a witness to a crime other than the murder established the heinous and depraved aggravating factor under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703.F(6). The court rejected defendant's claim that a trial court should be required to provide a list of specific mitigating factors to the jury and held that defendant failed to establish a causal nexus between the mitigating factors he alleged and his crime.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions and sentences, including the capital sentence.

 

114 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


State v. Brown


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-05-0263-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


212 Ariz. 225; 129 P.3d 947; 2006 Ariz. LEXIS 25; 473 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 39



March 16, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at State v. McMullen, 2008 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 1564 (2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals, Division Two, No. 2 CA-SA 05-0011. Santa Cruz County Superior Court No. CR 01-199. Appeal from the Superior Court in Santa Cruz County. The Honorable Michael Brown, Retired Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division Two, 210 Ariz. 534, 115 P.3d 128 (App. 2005).
State v. Brown, 210 Ariz. 534, 115 P.3d 128, 2005 Ariz. App. LEXIS 77 (Ariz. Ct. App., 2005)



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court in Santa Cruz County, AFFIRMED IN RELEVANT PART AND REMANDED. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division Two, AFFIRMED IN RELEVANT PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pleaded guilty to reckless manslaughter in violation of Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-1103(A)(1). The State sought review of a decision of the Superior Court in Santa Cruz County (Arizona) holding that defendant was entitled to a jury trial on alleged aggravating factors under Ariz. Rev. Stat. §§ 13-702, 13-702.01 pursuant to the Apprendi/Blakely rule.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of shooting his mother to death. After defendant pleaded guilty, the State filed a notice of aggravating factors and argued that defendant was not entitled to a jury trial on those factors due to his guilty plea. On appeal, the court held that defendant did not waive his right to a jury trial on the aggravating factors through his agreement to plead guilty because the plea agreement was silent as to any waiver of such a right and defendant was not informed of such a right at the change-of-plea hearing. The record made plain that defendant expressly preserved his jury trial claim; at the status conference, defense counsel explicitly told the trial court that defendant was not waiving any such right. During the plea colloquy, defendant did not agree to judicial factfinding concerning the aggravating factors and did not necessarily admit those facts by pleading guilty to an offense of which they were elements; therefore, his Sixth Amendment right to a jury trial remained intact.


OUTCOME: The decision was affirmed.

 

115 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


State v. Gomez


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-05-0062-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


212 Ariz. 55; 127 P.3d 873; 2006 Ariz. LEXIS 17; 475 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 6



February 8, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals Division One No. 1 CA-CR 03-1050, Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR2003-012719-001 DT. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Eileen S. Willett, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, 209 Ariz. 372, 102 P.3d 992.
State v. Gomez, 209 Ariz. 372, 102 P.3d 992, 2004 Ariz. App. LEXIS 186 (Ariz. Ct. App., 2004)



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County, VACATED; REMANDED. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, VACATED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted for possession of marijuana and methamphetamine. She stipulated to having been previously indicted for manslaughter, but the indictment was dismissed. The trial court ruled that she was ineligible for probation, pursuant to Proposition 200, Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-901.01 (2002). The Arizona Court of Appeals reversed the ruling. The State petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's manslaughter indictment was 10 years old. After defendant was deemed ineligible for probation because manslaughter was considered a violent crime, defendant faced a mandatory prison sentence because she committed the current offense while on parole. The issue presented was whether a 10-year-old, dismissed indictment disqualified a defendant from mandatory probation under Proposition 200. The supreme court held that a dismissed indictment, like a reversed conviction, did not disqualify a defendant from mandatory probation. Such a conviction typically would result in probation under Proposition 200. The State conceded that if a defendant were charged by information and convicted of a violent crime, and if the conviction were reversed later, it could not be used later to disqualify the defendant from probation under Proposition 200. The supreme court determined that imprisonment would not have served the purposes of Proposition 200, which was to provide treatment for drug offenders and to free prison space. The State's interpretation would have led to absurd results and constitutional concerns.


OUTCOME: The supreme court vacated defendant's sentence, and the matter was remanded for resentencing.

State v. Gomez


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-03-0199-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


211 Ariz. 494; 123 P.3d 1131; 2005 Ariz. LEXIS 129; 466 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 24



December 6, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at State v. Fabio Evelio Gomez, 2012 Ariz. LEXIS 259 (Ariz., Dec. 7, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR2000-090114. Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County. The Honorable James H. Keppel.



DISPOSITION: CONVICTIONS AND SEXUAL ASSAULT SENTENCE AFFIRMED, CAPITAL AND KIDNAPPING SENTENCES VACATED, REMANDED FOR FURTHER SENTENCING PROCEEDINGS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted by a jury, before the Superior Court of Maricopa County, Arizona, of kidnapping, sexual assault, and first degree murder. Defendant received a death sentence for first degree murder, and an automatic notice of appeal was filed pursuant to Ariz. R. Crim. P. 31.2(b).


OVERVIEW: Defendant lived across an apartment landing from the victim. Screams were heard, and a neighbor called 911, but when police arrived, they could not find where the screams were coming from, so they left. When defendant's girlfriend came home, she found blood in the apartment. The victim's body was eventually found inside a garbage bin. An examination of semen found inside the victim's body matched defendant's DNA. On appeal, defendant argued that the superior court erred in its premeditation instruction. The supreme court noted, however, that defendant presented a defense of total innocence; therefore, any error was not fundamental. Because the jury found defendant guilty of the highest offense, the rule that required instruction on lesser included offenses was not implicated. Defendant also claimed that the superior court violated his right to an impartial jury at sentencing by imposing an aggravated sentence. The supreme court noted that the aggravated factors were not found by the jury or admitted by defendant. The supreme court found fundamental error. Finally, defendant was shackled during the penalty phase; the State failed to prove that this error was harmless.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed defendant's convictions for first degree murder, sexual assault, and kidnapping, and the aggravated sentence for sexual assault. The supreme court vacated the sentence of death for first degree murder and the aggravated sentence for kidnapping, and the matter was remanded for further proceedings.

Scheehle v. Justices of the Ariz. Supreme Court


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-04-0103-CQ


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


211 Ariz. 282; 120 P.3d 1092; 2005 Ariz. LEXIS 106



October 5, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion denied by Scheehle v. Justices of the Supreme Court, 2005 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 45824 (D. Ariz., Oct. 11, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] United States District Court. No. CV-98-1095-PHX-SMM.
Scheehle v. Justices of the Supreme Court of Ariz., 120 P.3d 1092, 2005 Ariz. LEXIS 107 (Ariz., 2005)
Scheehle v. Justices of the Supreme Court, 203 Ariz. 520, 57 P.3d 379, 2002 Ariz. LEXIS 179 (Ariz., 2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The United States District Court for the District of Arizona granted summary judgment in favor of defendants, superior court judges, on plaintiff arbitrator's complaint attacking the arbitration program on both constitutional and state law grounds. The Ninth Circuit certified a question to the Arizona Supreme Court asking whether Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 12-133 authorized a system of compulsory participation of attorneys in the arbitration system.


OVERVIEW: After accepting jurisdiction of the certified question, all justices named in the complaint recused themselves. The arbitrator argued that the participation of the permanent members of the supreme court was prohibited by Ariz. Code Jud. Conduct Canon 3(E)(1)(d)(i) and 3(E)(1)(a). The supreme court noted that it was the court of last resort on the interpretation of the authority to regulate the practice of law. The rule of necessity obliged the supreme court to sit in answering the questions certified in the case even though its members were nominal parties to the action. If any Arizona court would be incapable of answering the certified question without bias, it would not be possible, by appointing temporary replacements, to cure the basis of the arbitrator's objection. The arbitrator's allegation that the four permanent justices already had a view about the question certified did not constitute a basis for disqualification even if it could be established. A complaint to the Commission on Judicial Conduct did not alone merit disqualification. Even when the canons did not require recusal, a judge could recuse from judicial duties.


OUTCOME: The arbitrator's motion to disqualify was denied.

Bennett v. Brownlow


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-04-0215-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


211 Ariz. 193; 119 P.3d 460; 2005 Ariz. LEXIS 85; 460 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 10



September 9, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals, Division One, No. 1 CA-CV 03-0233. Yavapai County Superior Court No. CV 01-0567. Appeal from the Superior Court in Yavapai County. No. CV-01-0567. The Honorable Kenneth L. Fields, Judge. Court of Appeals, Division One, 208 Ariz. 79, 90 P.3d 1245 (2004).
Bennett v. Brownlow, 208 Ariz. 79, 90 P.3d 1245, 2004 Ariz. App. LEXIS 72 (Ariz. Ct. App., 2004)



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court in Yavapai County, REMANDED WITH INSTRUCTIONS. Court of Appeals, Division One, VACATED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Arizona Court of Appeals, Division One, reversed and remanded the superior court's judgment granting appellant county summary judgment on appellee citizen's claims alleging various federal and state claims concerning the county court plaza. The county petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: The court of appeals determined that the plaza was a public forum and that Yavapai County, Ariz., Ordinance 2000-4 § 104(B) was a time, place and manner restriction. The court of appeals found that the ordinance was neither significantly related to the county's purpose nor narrowly tailored to achieve it. The supreme court noted that to establish standing, it required that petitioners show a particularized injury to themselves. The citizen could not have suffered personal harm from the non-profit requirement of the ordinance as the fraternal order of police application was not rejected on that ground. It was rejected because sponsorship by a volunteer fire department, a long-time backer of the festival, was preferable. The citizen was not a sponsor. The city partnership simply made its choice between two non-profit organizations. That choice could not be said to have caused First Amendment damage to the citizen. She could not establish standing to assert her claim.


OUTCOME: The opinion of the court of appeals was vacated and the case was remanded to the superior court with instructions to dismiss the complaint.

State v. Cromwell


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-03-0083-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


211 Ariz. 181; 119 P.3d 448; 2005 Ariz. LEXIS 84; 458 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 32



August 17, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Cromwell v. Ariz., 126 S. Ct. 2291, 164 L. Ed. 2d 819, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 4016 (U.S., May 22, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR2001-095438. Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County, No. CR2001-095438. Honorable Mark F. Aceto, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Maricopa County Superior Court (Arizona) that convicted him of first-degree murder and sexual assault of an 11-year-old girl and two counts of aggravated assault against the murder victim's mother and her friend and that sentenced him to death for the murder and to life imprisonment with no release for 35 years for the sexual assault and to 10 years each for the aggravated assaults.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that the trial court erred in denying his request for new counsel and in imposing his sentences and that the especially heinous, cruel, and depraved aggravator, Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(F)(6), was unconstitutionally vague. The court held that defendant was not entitled to new counsel based on mere personality conflicts or disagreements over trial strategy as they did not constitute irreconcilable conflicts. The court noted that defendant's right to counsel did not include counsel of his choice. The court rejected defendant's claim that § 13-703(F)(6) was unconstitutionally vague because the jury instructions given narrowed and defined the terms of especially heinous, cruel, and depraved as required by the Eight and Fourteenth Amendments. The court noted that § 13-703(F)(6) was stated in the disjunctive; thus the jury's finding of cruelty was sufficient without addressing heinousness or depravity. The court held that the death penalty was properly imposed on the murder conviction because the jury found two uncontested capital aggravators in that the murder was committed in an especially cruel manner and the victim was less that 15 years old.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed all of defendant's convictions and sentences.

State v. Anderson


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-02-0402-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


211 Ariz. 59; 116 P.3d 1219; 2005 Ariz. LEXIS 83; 458 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 19



August 12, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Habeas corpus proceeding at, Motion denied by Anderson v. Schriro, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 85736 (D. Ariz., Nov. 15, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Mohave County Superior Court NO. CR-96-865. Appeal from the Superior Court of Mohave County. Honorable James E. Chavez. Majority Opinion at 210 Ariz. 327, 111 P.3d 369, 2005 Ariz. LEXIS 50.
State v. Anderson, 210 Ariz. 327, 111 P.3d 369, 2005 Ariz. LEXIS 50 (Ariz., 2005)



DISPOSITION: SENTENCE AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an earlier opinion, defendant's convictions for conspiracy to commit first degree murder, armed robbery, and first degree murder were affirmed. The sentences of death and the sentence of life imprisonment were also affirmed. Resolution, however, was deferred on defendant's claim that the Mohave County Superior Court, Arizona, imposed an unconstitutional sentence in violation of the Sixth Amendment.


OVERVIEW: The superior court did not identify the statute under which the sentence was imposed; it appeared that the trial judge intended to impose the maximum super-aggravated sentence under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-702.01(A) (Supp. 1995). Defendant was eligible, however, for sentencing under the more punitive provisions of Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-702.01(C) (Supp. 1995) because he had a prior conviction. Defendant claimed that his right to a jury at sentencing was violated. The appellate court noted that jury found defendant guilty of first degree murder. The verdicts established beyond a reasonable doubt the existence of a serious physical injury aggravator. Also, the jury concluded beyond a reasonable doubt that each of the murders was motivated by pecuniary gain. Although the jury did not make a separate finding with respect to the motivation for the armed robbery, no reasonable jury could have failed to find the pecuniary gain aggravator for that offense. The failure of the superior court to submit to the jury the issue of motivation for the armed robbery was at worst harmless error.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the judgment.

State v. Glassel


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-03-0022-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


211 Ariz. 33; 116 P.3d 1193; 2005 Ariz. LEXIS 81; 458 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 20



August 10, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by State v. Glassel, 211 Ariz. 370, 121 P.3d 1240, 2005 Ariz. LEXIS 133 (Ariz., 2005)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Glassel v. Arizona, 547 U.S. 1024, 126 S. Ct. 1576, 164 L. Ed. 2d 308, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 2323 (2006)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Motion denied by Glassel v. Schriro, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 24946 (D. Ariz., Mar. 29, 2007)
Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Glassel, 2011 Ariz. App. LEXIS 24 (Ariz. Ct. App., Mar. 1, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR 2000-006872. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Peter C. Reinstein, Judge.
Lynn v. Reinstein, 205 Ariz. 186, 68 P.3d 412, 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 66 (Ariz., 2003)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Superior Court in Maricopa County (Arizona) of two counts of premeditated first degree murder and sentenced to death. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the trial court erred by denying him sufficient opportunity to voir dire the potential jurors about their understanding of the phrase "sufficiently substantial to call for leniency." The supreme court disagreed. The trial court fully complied with the Morgan requirements. The trial court required each potential juror to fill out a jury questionnaire, which contained six questions about predispositions on capital punishment. Two questions specifically addressed the Morgan issue. Moreover, the trial court conducted individual voir dire of every prospective juror whose responses to the questionnaire suggested an inability to deliberate impartially or a predisposition to impose the death penalty regardless of the mitigation evidence. Those potential jurors were either rehabilitated or dismissed. Defendant did not identify a single juror who deliberated notwithstanding an unwillingness to consider mitigation evidence. Every juror selected answered "no" to the question: "conversely, will you, for whatever reason, automatically vote for the death penalty without considering the evidence and the instructions of law that will be presented to you."


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

122 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


Grammatico v. Indus. Comm'n


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-04-0197-PR CONSOLIDATED WITH Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-04-0364-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


211 Ariz. 67; 117 P.3d 786; 2005 Ariz. LEXIS 82; 458 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 40; 22 A.L.R.6th 799



August 10, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] CV-04-0197-PR. Court of Appeals, Division One, No. 1 CA-IC 01-0117. ICA Claim No. 20001-390571. Carrier No. AZ 00008522. Industrial Commission Award. J. Matthew Powell, Administrative Law Judge. Court of Appeals, Division One, 208 Ariz. 10, 90 P.3d 211 (App. 2004). CV-04-0364-PR. Court of Appeals, Division One, No. 1 CA-IC 03-0041. ICA Claim No. 20013-370241. Carrier No. WC-905 367138. Industrial Commission Award. Stephen W. Pogson, Administrative Law Judge. Court of Appeals, Division One. 209 Ariz. 211, 99 P.3d 26 (App. 2004).
Komalestewa v. Indus. Comm'n, 209 Ariz. 211, 99 P.3d 26, 2004 Ariz. App. LEXIS 154 (Ariz. Ct. App., 2004)
Grammatico v. Indus. Comm'n, 208 Ariz. 10, 90 P.3d 211, 2004 Ariz. App. LEXIS 68 (Ariz. Ct. App., 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated appeals, respondents, first employer and the State Compensation Fund, and petitioner, second employee, challenged the judgments of the Court of Appeals, Arizona, setting aside the denial of workers' compensation benefits in the first case and affirming the denial of benefits in the second case, in the action with petitioner, first employee, and respondent, second employer.


OVERVIEW: Both employees were injured while at work. The first employee's post-accident urine test showed positive results for marijuana, amphetamine, and methamphetamine. The second employee's blood tests taken at the hospital shortly after the accident revealed alcohol in his blood. The issue in these consolidated matters required the court to determine whether Ariz. Const. art. 18, § 8, precluded the Arizona Legislature from requiring proof that the presence of alcohol or illegal drugs in an injured worker's system was not a contributing cause of the accident before workers' compensation benefits could be awarded. The court ruled that while alcohol consumption and illegal drug use shortly before work or during work undeniably increased the chances of being injured on the job, it could not be unequivocally said that employees with alcohol or drugs in their systems who sustained injuries had intentionally injured themselves. The Legislature could not abrogate claims for workers' compensation for injuries wholly or partially caused or contributed to by necessary employment risks or dangers solely because an employee failed to pass a drug or alcohol test.


OUTCOME: The judgment setting aside the denial of workers' compensation benefits in the first case was affirmed, and the judgment affirming the denial of benefits in the second case was reversed and set aside.

 

123 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


State v. Fell


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-04-0344-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


210 Ariz. 554; 115 P.3d 594; 2005 Ariz. LEXIS 73; 456 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 18



July 8, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals, Division Two, No. 2 CA-SA 04-0057. Pima County Superior Court No. CR20002900. Special Action from the Superior Court of Pima County. The Honorable Howard Fell, Judge Pro Tempore. No. CR20002900. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division Two, 209 Ariz. 77, 97 P.3d 902.
State v. Fell, 209 Ariz. 77, 97 P.3d 902, 2004 Ariz. App. LEXIS 137 (Ariz. Ct. App., 2004)



DISPOSITION: Special Action from the Superior Court of Pima County, REMANDED. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division Two, AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury found defendant guilty of first degree murder. The trial judge subsequently determined that a jury was necessary to find the existence of aggravating circumstance before a sentence of "natural life" could be imposed. The State sought special action review. The Arizona Court of Appeals held that the Blakely decision was inapplicable to the non-capital, first-degree murder scheme. The parties cross-petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted in 2000 for sexual assault and murder. Defendant argued that a life term was the presumptive sentence, and a natural life term would be an aggravated sentence. The supreme court was called on in this case to decide: (1) whether the Sixth Amendment required that a jury find specific aggravating circumstances before the superior court could impose a natural life sentence; and (2) whether the superior court could apply a law adopted in 2003 in deciding between a life and a natural life sentence for a first degree murder committed in 2000. The supreme court concluded that the Sixth Amendment did not require that a jury find an aggravating circumstance before a natural life sentence could be imposed. The remaining question was what factors the judge could consider in exercising his discretion to sentence a defendant to either life or natural life. The supreme court concluded that the legislature's enactment of Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703.01(Q) (Supp. 2004) was substantive, not procedural; therefore, this statute could not be applied retroactively. The supreme court's Viramontes decision was still applicable to cases arising before the effective date of the statute.


OUTCOME: The supreme court remanded the special action, and it affirmed the judgment.

 

124 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


Citizen Publ. Co. v. Miller


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-04-0280-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


210 Ariz. 513; 115 P.3d 107; 2005 Ariz. LEXIS 69; 455 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 25; 33 Media L. Rep. 1961



July 1, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals, Division Two, No. 2 CA-SA 04-0041. Pima County Superior Court No. C20040194. Special Action from the Superior Court of Pima County. The Honorable Leslie B. Miller, Judge. No. C20040194. Petition for Review from Order of the Court of Appeals, Division Two. No. 2 CA-SA 04-0041.



DISPOSITION: Special Action from the Superior Court of Pima County, REVERSED AND REMANDED WITH INSTRUCTIONS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner newspaper publisher sought review of an order of the Court of Appeals, Division Two (Arizona), denying the newspaper publisher's motion to dismiss, under Ariz. R. Civ. P. 12(b)(6), an intentional infliction of emotional distress claim filed by plaintiffs, two Islamic-Americans.


OVERVIEW: After the newspaper printed a letter to the editor about the war in Iraq, in which the author stated that "whenever there is an assassination or another atrocity we should proceed to the closest mosque and execute five of the first Muslims we encounter," plaintiffs sued the newspaper publisher alleging intentional infliction of emotional distress. On appeal of the appellate court's order denying the newspaper publisher's motion to dismiss, the court reversed, holding that because the letter did not fall within one of the well-recognized narrow exceptions to the general rule of First Amendment protection for political speech, the newspaper publisher could not be held liable under Arizona tort law for publishing the letter. The letter did not fall under the "incitement" exception because it did not advocate imminent lawless action, because the suggestions that Muslims be executed was premised on the occurrence of some future assassination or another atrocity. The letter did not fall under the "fighting words" exception because the statements were not made in a face-to-face confrontation with the target of the remarks. Nor did the letter fall under the "true threat" exception.


OUTCOME: The order denying the newspaper publisher's motion to dismiss was reversed and the case was remanded with instructions to dismiss plaintiffs' intentional infliction of emotional distress claim.

State v. Roseberry


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-03-0247-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


210 Ariz. 360; 111 P.3d 402; 2005 Ariz. LEXIS 52; 451 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 61



May 11, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Roseberry v. Arizona, 546 U.S. 945, 126 S. Ct. 444, 163 L. Ed. 2d 338, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 7459 (2005)
Related proceeding at State ex rel. Ariz. Dep't of Corr. v. Kiger, 224 Ariz. 252, 229 P.3d 264, 2010 Ariz. App. LEXIS 62 (Ariz. Ct. App., 2010)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Petition granted by, Request denied by State v. Roseberry, 2015 Ariz. LEXIS 158 (Ariz., May 26, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Yavapai County Superior Court No. CR 2001-0177. Appeal from the Superior Court in Yavapai County. No. CR 2001-0177. The Honorable William T. Kiger, Judge.



DISPOSITION: CONVICTIONS AND SENTENCES AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court in Yavapai County (Arizona) sentenced defendant to death for first-degree murder, to an aggravated term of 10 years' imprisonment for conspiracy to transport two pounds or more of marijuana for sale, and to a consecutive aggravated term of 10 years' imprisonment for transportation of marijuana for sale. Defendant appealed his convictions and sentences.


OVERVIEW: During a drug run, defendant killed his traveling companion so that he could steal the marijuana he was transporting and sell the drugs for himself. Although the State notified defendant that it would seek the death penalty, the State did not formally specify the aggravating factors on which it would rely. The jury found that defendant was guilty of the charged offenses and that defendant committed the murder for pecuniary gain. Because defendant had actual notice of the aggravating circumstance before trial and conceded that he suffered no prejudice from the lack of formal notice, defendant was not denied due process. On appeal, the court held that the trial court did not err by dismissing for cause potential jurors who stated that they would not follow the law if following the law meant that defendant would be sentenced to death. The finding of the pecuniary gain aggravating factor under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(F)(5) (Supp. 2004) was sufficiently supported by evidence showing that on the day of the murder, defendant began to set up a deal to sell the marijuana and that defendant told his wife that he was going to make a lot of money selling the marijuana for himself.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions and sentences.

State v. Carreon


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-03-0160-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


210 Ariz. 54; 107 P.3d 900; 2005 Ariz. LEXIS 20; 446 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 57



February 24, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Supplemental opinion at State v. Carreon, 116 P.3d 1192, 2005 Ariz. LEXIS 80 (Ariz., 2005)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Martinez Carreon v. Arizona, 126 S. Ct. 122, 163 L. Ed. 2d 129, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 6944 (U.S., Oct. 3, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR 2001-090195. Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County. Honorable James H. Keppel.



DISPOSITION: Convictions affirmed; sentences reversed in part and affirmed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury for the Maricopa County Superior Court (Arizona) found defendant guilty of first-degree murder, attempted first-degree murder, burglary in the first degree, two counts of endangerment, and misconduct involving weapons. The jury sentenced defendant to death on the murder in the first degree count. The trial court entered the sentence of death on that count and sentenced him to aggravated prison terms on the non-capital charges. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: All charges arose out of the murder for hire of the deceased and the attempted murder of the victim, the deceased's girlfriend. The supreme court found that because Ring II announced a new procedural rule, application of Arizona's new death penalty statute to defendant did not violate either the federal or state Ex Post Facto Clause. Additionally, the supreme court found that the the trial court did not abuse its discretion in precluding evidence about two unrelated murders because they could have confused the jury. However, the supreme court found that the jury improperly found that the aggravating factors of pecuniary gain and endangerment were applicable. The supreme court engaged in an independent reweighing of the aggravating and mitigating factors. In balancing the mitigating evidence of a troubled childhood and unsubstantiated allegations of drug abuse, which had no more than de minimus weight, against two statutory aggravating factors that defendant had previously been convicted of a serious offense and that he committed the offenses while on authorized release from the department of corrections, the supreme court found that defendant's death sentence was appropriate.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed defendant's convictions and the sentence of death. However, the supreme court ordered that defendant's sentence on the misconduct involving weapons count run concurrent with that imposed for the attempted murder count.

City of Tucson v. Clear Channel Outdoor, Inc.


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-04-0033-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


209 Ariz. 544; 105 P.3d 1163; 2005 Ariz. LEXIS 14; 445 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 21



February 10, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at, Remanded by City of Tucson v. Clear Channel Outdoor, Inc., 2008 Ariz. App. LEXIS 53 (Ariz. Ct. App., Apr. 2, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1] Court of Appeals Division Two No. 2 CA-CV 02-0183. Pima County Superior Court No. C-20003722. Appeal from Pima County Superior Court. The Honorable Charles V. Harrington, Judge. The Honorable Carmine Cornelio, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division Two.
City of Tucson v. Clear Channel Outdoor, Inc., 206 Ariz. 335, 78 P.3d 1056, 2003 Ariz. App. LEXIS 179 (Ariz. Ct. App., 2003)



DISPOSITION: Appeal from Pima County Superior Court, vacated and remanded. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division Two, vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sued appellee billboard company in the trial court to remove billboards which did not conform to the city's ordinance. The trial court dismissed 89 of the 173 counts in the city's original and amended complaints, under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 9-462.02(C). The Court of Appeals, Division Two (Arizona), affirmed, and the city sought further review.


OVERVIEW: The city's claims were those in its first complaint, filed before Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 9-462.02(C), imposing a limitations period on such claims, became effective, and those in its amended complaint, filed a day less than a year after § 9-462.02(C)'s effective date. The supreme court held, as to claims filed before § 9-462.02(C)'s effective date, no limitations period applied before the enactment of § 9-462.02(C), and filing the claims tolled the limitations period in § 9-462.02(C), so the claims were timely. As to claims filed after § 9-462.02(C)'s effective date, Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 12-505 governed the retroactivity of new limitation statutes. Under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 12-505(B), Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 9-462.02(C) governed claims not barred by a prior limitations period, except that if, under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 12-505(B), a claim was barred by Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 9-462.02(C), the city had one year after § 9-462.02(C)'s effective date to file suit. Section 9-462.02(C) shortened the city's time to bring its claims against the billboard company, and it had to bring any such claims within one year of that statute's effective date, which it did, so its claims were timely.


OUTCOME: The opinion of the lower court and the judgment of the trial court dismissing the city's claims and awarding attorneys' fees and costs were vacated. The company's request for attorney's fees incurred in the supreme court was denied. The case was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.

McKaney v. Foreman


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-04-0032-SA


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


209 Ariz. 268; 100 P.3d 18; 2004 Ariz. LEXIS 118; 441 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 19



November 3, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at, Petition denied by McKaney v. Foreman, 2006 Ariz. LEXIS 48 (Ariz., Apr. 20, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR 2001-011445. Special Action from the Superior Court of Maricopa County. The Honorable John Foreman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Jurisdiction accepted. relief denied; remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was indicted on counts of first degree murder, first degree burglary, sexual assault, and kidnapping. Defendant moved to dismiss the State's notice of intent and notice of aggravating factors. The Superior Court of Maricopa County, Arizona, denied defendant's motion. Defendant filed a petition for special action.


OVERVIEW: On review, defendant argued that under the Due Process Clause of U.S. Const. amend XIV and Ariz. Const. art. II, § 30, the aggravating factors had to be alleged in the indictment and supported by evidence of probable cause. The court disagreed, holding that aggravating factors essential to the imposition of a capital sentence did not need to be alleged in the grand jury indictment or the information in order to satisfy constitutional due process. Even though aggravating factors did not need to be specified in the charging document, an accused in the State of Arizona was accorded notice under the rules of criminal procedure that complied with constitutional requirements. Defendant was notified of the State's intent to seek the death penalty and of the specific aggravating factors to be proved with ample time to prepare.


OUTCOME: The court accepted jurisdiction of defendant's petition for special action, but denied relief. The case was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.

State v. Brown


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-03-0255-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


209 Ariz. 200; 99 P.3d 15; 2004 Ariz. LEXIS 114; 442 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 21



October 27, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals, Division Two, No. 2 CA-SA 2003-0003. Santa Cruz County Superior Court No. CR-01-199. Special Action from the Superior Court in Santa Cruz County. The Honorable Michael J. Brown, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division Two, 205 Ariz. 325, 70 P.3d 454.
State v. Brown, 205 Ariz. 325, 70 P.3d 454, 2003 Ariz. App. LEXIS 106 (Ariz. Ct. App., 2003)



DISPOSITION: Opinion of the Court of Appeals vacated; remanded to superior court for further proceedings.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pled guilty to reckless manslaughter. Defendant appealed the judgment of the Arizona Court of Appeals, Division Two, finding that the trial judge could impose a sentence above five years absent a jury finding of aggravators beyond a reasonable doubt without running afoul of the United States or Arizona Constitutions.


OVERVIEW: The presumptive sentence for reckless manslaughter was five years, Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-701(C)(1) (2001). The plea agreement provided that defendant could receive a sentence between three years and twelve and one-half years. A three-year sentence was the minimum allowed for a class two felony and required a finding of at least two substantial mitigating factors, Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-702.01(B)(1) (2001). A sentence of twelve and one-half years could be imposed for a class two felony after a finding of at least two substantial aggravating factors, Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-702.01(A)(1). The reviewing court held that the "maximum sentence" for Apprendi analysis in the instant case was the five-year presumptive sentence in Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-701(C)(1). Because a sentence in excess of five years could be imposed on defendant only after a finding of one or more of the aggravating circumstances in Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-702(C), the Sixth Amendment guarantee of jury trial extended to the finding of these facts and required proof beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: The appellate court's opinion was vacated, and the case was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.
