Clean Elections Inst. Inc. v. Brewer


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-04-0263-AP-EL


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


209 Ariz. 241; 99 P.3d 570; 2004 Ariz. LEXIS 112; 442 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 28



October 7, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended October 11, 2004.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County Superior Court No. CV2004-012699. Appeal from Superior Court of Maricopa County. CV-2004-012699. The Honorable Margaret H. Downie.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In Maricopa County Superior Court (Arizona), appellee opponents sued to enjoin appellant Arizona Secretary of State from certifying Proposition 106, which proposed state constitutional amendments. Appellants, the Secretary and proponents, challenged the lower court's decision that (1) held the proposition violated Ariz. Const. art. 21, § 1's "separate amendment rule," and (2) ordered that the proposition not be certified or placed on the ballot.


OVERVIEW: The trial court held that Proposition 106, which would have affected the Arizona Citizens Clean Elections Act (CCEA), Ariz. Rev. Stat. §§ 16-940 to 16-961 (Supp. 2003), violated the "separate amendment rule" of Ariz. Const. art. 21, § 1. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's decision. The court noted that Ariz. Const. art. 21 established a stricter standard than Ariz. Const. art. 4, which applied to statutes enacted by the legislature, for determining whether a proposal involved more than one constitutional amendment. The court found that Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 16-955 of the CCEA created a Citizens Clean Election Commission (CCEC), which independently administered public funding for political candidates and enforced the CCEA. Proposition 106 § A proposed to prohibit public funding while § C proposed to change the funding source for the CCEC and make it dependent upon a legislative appropriation to carry out it duties under the CCEA. The court found that there was no common principle between §§ A and C that would make it likely that one voting to abolish public funding of political campaigns would also vote to strip the CCEC of its independence from legislative control.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court judgment.

State v. Murdaugh


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-01-0421-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


209 Ariz. 19; 97 P.3d 844; 2004 Ariz. LEXIS 97; 442 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 3



September 15, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Murdaugh v. Arizona, 543 U.S. 1155, 125 S. Ct. 1298, 161 L. Ed. 2d 121, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 1589 (2005)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Motion denied by, Stay vacated by, Certificate of appealability granted Murdaugh v. Ryan, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 100208 (D. Ariz., Sept. 2, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR1995-006472. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. No. CR1995-006472. The Honorable Sherry Hutt, Judge.

DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Maricopa County Superior Court (Arizona) convicted defendant for kidnapping, robbery, and first degree murder. Defendant was sentenced to death for the murder. An appeal was automatic and direct, pursuant to Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703.04 (Supp. 2003).


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued on appeal that his plea was not knowingly made because he was not informed that he had a Sixth Amendment right to have a jury determine his sentence; and a jury could determine that the mitigating circumstances were sufficiently substantial to call for leniency. The appellate court held: defendant was competent to enter a plea of guilty and he entered the plea knowingly, intelligently, and voluntarily and nothing indicated his guilty plea was influenced by whether a judge or a jury would decide if he deserved the death sentence; he had a prior conviction for an offense in which a sentence of life or death could be imposed; the State established the Ariz. OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Moody


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-02-0044-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


2004 Ariz. LEXIS 100



August 8, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [*1] As Corrected September 17, 2004.


PRIOR HISTORY: Pima County Superior Court No. CR-43804. Appeal from the Superior Court in Pima County. The Honorable Michael J. Cruikshank, Judge.
State v. Moody, 208 Ariz. 424, 94 P.3d 1119, 2004 Ariz. LEXIS 94 (Ariz., 2004)



DISPOSITION: Convictions affirmed; sentence vacated and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court in Pima County (Arizona), which convicted him of the first-degree murder of two victims, and which sentenced him to death pursuant to Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703 (Supp. 1993). Defendant had previously been convicted of the same charge, but that conviction was reversed by the court based on a denial of the right to counsel, and the case was remanded for a new trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the second trial should have been barred by the state and federal constitutions' Double Jeopardy Clauses. In affirming defendant's convictions, the court held that double jeopardy did not bar retrial. The trial court's review of the record on competency did not constitute an impermissible competency determination. A correction officer's actions in overhearing defendant's conversation with his attorney did not interfere with his relationship with counsel, because he knew that his conversation could be overheard and made no effort to safeguard against interception. Because a reasonable jury could differently assess the evidence upon which the trial court based its pecuniary gain finding during sentencing, the court could not conclude that the trial court's finding of the Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(F)(5) factor was harmless. Furthermore, because evidence was presented that defendant was in a dissociated state due to psychosis, drug impairment, or both, it could not be concluded beyond a reasonable doubt that a reasonable jury could not find other than the trial court did on the issue of special cruelty as an aggravating factor.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions, but vacated his death sentence and remanded for jury resentencing.

State v. Moody


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-02-0044-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


208 Ariz. 424; 94 P.3d 1119; 2004 Ariz. LEXIS 94; 433 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 31



August 8, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Corrected by, Reprinted as amended at State v. Moody, 2004 Ariz. LEXIS 100 (Ariz., Aug. 8, 2004)
Appeal after remand at State v. Moody, 2012 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 1279 (2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Pima County Superior Court No. CR-43804. Appeal from the Superior Court in Pima County. The Honorable Michael J. Cruikshank, Judge.
State v. Moody, 192 Ariz. 505, 968 P.2d 578, 1998 Ariz. LEXIS 641 (Ariz., 1998)
State v. Ring, 204 Ariz. 534, 65 P.3d 915, 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 29 (Ariz., 2003)



DISPOSITION: Convictions affirmed; sentence vacated and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Pima County Superior Court (Arizona) that convicted defendant of two counts of first-degree murder for the deaths of two victims and sentenced him to death pursuant to Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703 (Supp. 1993). Defendant's convictions in his first trial were reversed for deprivation of counsel after defendant refused counsel and represented himself.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed many errors on appeal, including double jeopardy violations. The court rejected the double jeopardy claims because defendant's convictions in the first trial were reversed for deprivation of counsel, not prosecutorial misconduct. The court held that the State's addition of felony murder theories on retrial was not error. The court rejected defendant's claims regarding his competency to stand trial because the evidence showed that despite his personality disorders, defendant was likely malingering or faking mental illness and was capable of assisting defense counsel. The court affirmed defendant's convictions but vacated his death sentence because defendant was sentenced to death under a procedure found to be unconstitutional in Ring II. Under the new capital sentencing statute, Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703.01, defendants were entitled to a jury determination as to aggravating factors under Ariz. Rev. Stat. §§ 13-703(F)(5) and 13-703(F)(6). However, in this case, the trial court made the determination and the court concluded that the evidence pertaining to those factors could have been interpreted differently by a reasonable jury, thus the error was not harmless.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for the first-degree murders of the two victims but vacated defendant's death sentence and remanded the case for jury resentencing.

State v. Armstrong


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-00-0595-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


208 Ariz. 360; 93 P.3d 1076; 2004 Ariz. LEXIS 91; 430 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 59



July 16, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at State v. Doogan, 2007 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 1172 (2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Pima County Superior Court No. CR-61846. Appeal from the Superior Court in Pima County. The Honorable Howard Hantman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Death sentences vacated; remanded for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's death sentences were reviewed in light of a United States Supreme Court decision, which held that defendants were entitled to a jury determination on any fact on which a state legislature conditions an increase in a maximum punishment.


OVERVIEW: A jury found defendant guilty of two counts of first degree murder and one count of conspiracy to commit murder for the murders of his sister and her fiance. In imposing death sentences, the trial court, rather than the jury, found aggravating factors under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-701(F)(5), (8). The trial court also found that while seven mitigating factors existed, those factors were insufficient to call for leniency. Reviewing the trial court's decision for harmless error, the Arizona Supreme Court vacated defendant's sentences, holding that (1) the § 13-701(F)(8) finding regarding the commission of multiple homicides was harmless error because defendant was found guilty of multiple homicides and the homicides were temporally, spatially, and motivationally related; but, (2) the finding that defendant committed the murders for pecuniary gain, an aggravating factor under § 13-701(F)(5), was not harmless error because a jury could have assessed the evidence differently and found no pecuniary gain factor; and (3) the finding on the mitigating factors was not harmless error because the jury could have differed with the trial court in finding and weighing the factors.


OUTCOME: Defendant's death sentences were vacated and the case was remanded so that defendant could be resentenced.

Aileen H. Char Life Interest v. Maricopa County


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-03-0348-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


208 Ariz. 286; 93 P.3d 486; 2004 Ariz. LEXIS 86; 430 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 33



July 13, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Decision reached on appeal by, Remanded by, in part Aida Renta Trust v. Maricopa County, 2009 Ariz. App. LEXIS 630 (Ariz. Ct. App., June 11, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals, Division One, Nos. 1 CA-TX 02-0003, 1 CA-TX 02-0013. Maricopa County Superior Court Nos. TX 98-00413, TX 98-00419, TX 98-00422. Appeal from the Arizona Tax Court. Nos. TX 98-00413, TX 98-00419, TX 98-00422. The Honorable Jeffrey S. Cates. Memorandum Decision of Court of Appeals, Division One, 1 CA-TX 02-0003, 1 CA-TX 02-0013, Filed September 2, 2003.
Aida Renta Trust v. Department of Revenue, 197 Ariz. 222, 3 P.3d 1142, 2000 Ariz. App. LEXIS 147 (Ariz. Ct. App., 2000)



DISPOSITION: Court of Appeals Memorandum Decision vacated; judgment of the Tax Court affirmed in part and reversed in part; and remanded to the Tax Court with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers appealed a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Arizona) that reversed a tax court decision that held the taxpayers were entitled to a refund of property taxes under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 42-204.C (Supp. 1997) (repealed) from defendants, county and state revenue department, because the county valued their property in a discriminatory manner in violation of the Uniformity Clause, Ariz. Const. art. IX, § 1 and U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OVERVIEW: The county valued the taxpayers' multi-family apartment properties by using a cost approach, but programmed its valuation program to "roll over" the value of other multi-family properties, which left the valuation unchanged. The taxpayers argued that the undervaluation of the "roll-over" properties resulted in the taxpayers being forced to bear a disproportionate share of the property tax in violation of the Uniformity Clause. On appeal, the court held that the taxpayers met their burden of establishing that the county intentionally and systematically used a method of valuing their property different from that used to value the roll-over properties, which resulted in the roll-over properties being valued at 66 percent of their full cash value while the taxpayers' properties were valued at 100 percent of full cash value. The court held that a refund was the appropriate remedy for such discrimination in order to make the taxpayers' tax burden uniform with that of the roll-over properties. The court reversed as to the award of attorney fees, holding that Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 12-348.E permitted each party to receive an award of attorney fees rather than a single award for each action.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the court of appeals' memorandum decision. The court affirmed the part of the tax court decision that held the taxpayers were entitled to a refund because of the greatly disproportionate valuation of their properties by the county, but reversed the tax court's decision as to the single award of attorney fees. The case was remanded to the tax court to determine the appropriate amount of refund and attorney fees.

State v. Davolt


Supreme Court No. CR-00-0508-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


207 Ariz. 191; 84 P.3d 456; 2004 Ariz. LEXIS 25; 419 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 3



February 17, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by State v. Davolt, 2004 Ariz. LEXIS 30 (Ariz., Mar. 16, 2004)
Appeal after remand at State v. Davolt, 2010 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 558 (2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Mohave County, Superior Court No. CR-98-1243. Appeal from the Superior Court in Mohave County. The Honorable Steven F. Conn, Judge.
State v. Ring, 204 Ariz. 534, 65 P.3d 915, 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 29 (Ariz., 2003)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, vacated in part and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court in Mohave County (Arizona), which convicted him of two counts of first-degree murder, one count of first-degree burglary, one count of theft of property valued at $ 1,000 or more, one count of arson of an occupied structure, and one count of theft of means of transportation. Defendant was sentenced to death for each of the murder counts.


OVERVIEW: Two days before the victims' bodies were discovered, bank video cameras captured defendant, who was 16 years old, attempting to withdraw money from the victims' bank account. DNA and fingerprint evidence placed defendant inside the victims' home. Several days after the bodies were discovered, defendant was found in California, in possession of the victims' car and other items. The court agreed with the trial court that Fourth Amendment and Fifth Amendment violations occurred during defendant's arrest and initial detention. Although the State proved that the location of defendant's motel room inevitably would have been discovered, the evidence in the room may never have been obtained lawfully. However, the introduction of the illegally seized evidence was harmless. The court found that substantial evidence supported the verdicts. The State could not seek the death penalty against a juvenile pursuant to Arizona's Automatic Filing Statute without an individual assessment of his maturity and moral responsibility at the time of the offense. The trial court also erred by refusing to consider defendant's age as a statutory mitigating circumstance when sentencing on the non-capital counts.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions, but remanded to the trial court to determine whether, at the time of the offense, defendant possessed moral responsibility and culpability sufficient to render him eligible for the death penalty. The court also vacated the sentences imposed on defendant's non-capital convictions and remanded the non-capital counts for resentencing.

Kerr v. Killian


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-03-0110-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


207 Ariz. 181; 84 P.3d 446; 2004 Ariz. LEXIS 24; 419 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 19



February 13, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
As Corrected March 31, 2004.
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Moran v. Hibbs, 160 L. Ed. 2d 13, 125 S. Ct. 39, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 5536 (U.S., Oct. 4, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Court of Appeals, Division One. No. 1 CA-TX 00-0023. Arizona Tax Court Nos. TX 97-00119, TX 97-00131, TX 97-00150. Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County. Arizona Tax Court. The Honorable William J. Schafer, III, Judge. The Honorable Susan R. Bolton, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One.
Kerr v. Killian, 204 Ariz. 485, 65 P.3d 434, 2001 Ariz. App. LEXIS 200 (Ariz. Ct. App., 2001)



DISPOSITION: REVERSED IN PART AND REMANDED, VACATED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff federal employees filed a class action against defendant Arizona Department of Revenue (ADOR), alleging that Arizona's income tax scheme violated intergovernmental tax immunity by treating employee contributions of federal employees differently than those of state employees. The Court of Appeals, Division One (Arizona), held that Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 43-1001(2) violated the intergovernmental tax immunity doctrine; ADOR appealed.


OVERVIEW: The employees challenged the use in Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 43-1001(2) of federal adjusted gross income (AGI) as the base for Arizona taxable income, because it allowed state employees whose contributions were picked up to avoid current taxation. The court of appeals, reversing its earlier ruling, ultimately held that, as the effect of Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 43-1001(2) for tax years after 1990 was to require federal employees to pay current Arizona income tax on their mandatory contributions, while deferring such taxation for state and local employees, the intergovernmental tax immunity doctrine, as codified in 4 U.S.C.S. § 111(a), mandated that the Arizona scheme be struck, because it was not justified by "'significant differences" between these classes. The supreme court disagreed. As Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 43-1001(2) contained no overt discrimination against any taxpayer because of the source of pay, and in practical terms did not require that federal employees be taxed differently than state employees with respect to mandatory retirement contributions, the use of federal AGI as the base for Arizona taxable income did not violate the intergovernmental tax immunity doctrine.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals' decision was vacated insofar as it held that the Arizona tax scheme violated the intergovernmental tax immunity doctrine, and the judgment of the tax court was reversed to the same extent. The case was remanded to the tax court for further proceedings.

Petersen v. City of Mesa


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-03-0100-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


207 Ariz. 35; 83 P.3d 35; 2004 Ariz. LEXIS 12; 418 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 75; 20 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 1586



January 27, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Mesa v. Petersen, 160 L. Ed. 2d 20, 125 S. Ct. 51, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 5593 (U.S., Oct. 4, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals, Division One, No. 1 CA-CV 02-0016. Maricopa County Superior Court No. CV 2001-090218. Appeal from Superior Court in Maricopa County. No. CV 2001-090218. The Honorable Robert D. Myers. Opinion of Court of Appeals, Division One, 204 Ariz. 278, 63 P.3d 309 (App. 2003).
Petersen v. City of Mesa, 204 Ariz. 278, 63 P.3d 309, 2003 Ariz. App. LEXIS 30 (Ariz. Ct. App., 2003)



DISPOSITION: Court of appeals' opinion vacated and trial court's judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A firefighter filed a complaint in the trial court, seeking declaratory and injunctive relief, alleging that random testing violated his rights under both the Arizona and federal constitutions. The trial court held that random drug testing violated the Arizona constitution. The Court of Appeals, Division One (Arizona) reversed. The firefighter appealed.


OVERVIEW: The firefighter maintained that the city could not enforce the random drug testing component because the city's alleged "special needs" offered in support of the program were insufficient to overcome the privacy intrusion occasioned by the search. The city conceded the record was devoid of any indication that the city had ever encountered any drug problem involving its firefighters. The city failed to define any real and substantial risk that random, suspicionless testing was designed to address. Balancing the firefighter's privacy interests against the interests the city advanced in favor of its program's random component, the city's generalized and unsubstantiated interest in deterring and detecting alcohol and drug use among the city's firefighters by conducting random drug tests was insufficient to overcome even the lessened privacy interests of the firefighters. The program's random testing component was unreasonable and therefore violated the Fourth Amendment to the United States Constitution.


OUTCOME: The Arizona Supreme Court vacated the court of appeals' opinion and affirmed the trial court's judgment enjoining the city from enforcing the random, suspicionless component of the program.

Weatherford v. State


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-02-0369-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


206 Ariz. 529; 81 P.3d 320; 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 143; 415 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 5



December 17, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals, Division One, No. 1 CA-CV 01-0289, 1 CA-CV 01-0496 (Consolidated). Maricopa County, Superior Court, Nos. CV 98-021291, CV 98-021291-2. Appeal from Superior Court of Maricopa County. Nos. CV 98-021291 and CV 98-021291-2. The Honorable Edward O. Burke, The Honorable Johnathan H. Schwartz. Opinion of Court of Appeals Division One. 203 Ariz. 313, 54 P.3d 342 (App. 2002).
Weatherford v. State, 203 Ariz. 313, 54 P.3d 342, 2002 Ariz. App. LEXIS 136 (Ariz. Ct. App., 2002)



DISPOSITION: Appeal from Superior Court of Maricopa County affirmed in part; reversed in part; remanded. Opinion of Court of Appeals Division One vacated in part; approved in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff guardian ad litem sued defendants, State, social workers, and supervisor, on behalf of a child who was sexually assaulted at a foster care facility. Defendants were granted summary judgment based upon protective services immunity, Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 8-805.A (2001), and qualified immunity, 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. The Court of Appeals of Arizona reversed the order dismissing the negligence and § 1983 claims. The instant court granted review.


OVERVIEW: The child was placed in a foster care facility and was sexually assaulted by two residents while there. Defendants admitted that, acting in their capacity as social workers, they failed to comply with various agency requirements, including failures to timely complete an initial case plan, to assess the child's needs and his compatibility with other residents, and to visit the facility within 24 hours of his placement. In addition, they did not dispute that they made only two of the 16 required weekly supervised visits to the facility between November 1996 and the date of the alleged assault four months later. The state supreme court vacated the court of appeals decision and reversed the trial court's grant of summary judgment and ordered that the trial court apply the standard it established. The court held that a foster child could establish § 1983 liability against a state official by showing that the official, without justification, acted with deliberate indifference by placing a child in foster care or by maintaining a placement when the official knew that the placement exposed the child to danger or would have known of the danger but for the official's deliberate indifference.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court vacated a portion of the court of appeals decision and affirmed the rest. The state supreme court reversed the trial court's grant of summary judgment as to the § 1983 claim and remanded the matter to have the trial court apply the new standard it established for imposing § 1983 liability in foster care child cases.

Facilitec, Inc. v. Hibbs


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-02-0412-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


206 Ariz. 486; 80 P.3d 765; 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 141; 414 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 25



December 10, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals, Division One, No. 1 CA-CV 01-0139. Maricopa County Superior Court No. CV 99-22372. Appeal from Superior Court of Maricopa County No. CV 99-22372. The Honorable Susan R. Bolton. The Honorable Kenneth L. Fields. The Honorable Edward O. Burke. Opinion of Court of Appeals, Division One, 204 Ariz. 39, 59 P.3d 803 (App. 2003).
Facilitec, Inc. v. Hibbs, 204 Ariz. 39, 59 P.3d 803, 2002 Ariz. App. LEXIS 172 (Ariz. Ct. App., 2002)



DISPOSITION: Appeal from Superior Court of Maricopa County REVERSED AND REMANDED. Opinion of Court of Appeals, Division One, VACATED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of Maricopa County (Arizona) granted partial summary judgment to plaintiff contract bidder in a procurement dispute. Defendant Director (director) of the Department of Administration (department) for the State of Arizona appealed. The (Arizona) Court of Appeals, Division One reversed. The contract bidder sought review.


OVERVIEW: The department solicited bids for office furniture and awarded the contract to a bidder other than the contract bidder. The contract bidder filed a protest. When it was denied, the contract bidder appealed to the director, who delegated the matter to his deputy director, who referred the matter for a hearing. The deputy director issued a decision rejecting the administrative law judge's recommendation, denying the contract bidder's protest. The deputy director denied the contract bidder's motion for review. The contract bidder's request that the deputy's decision be certified as final was denied. The contract bidder complained in the trial court. The trial court found the director did not have the power to delegate his authority. The appellate court reversed, finding the deputy director had authority to make the decision. The instant court granted the contract bidder's petition for review. It determined the director could delegate to the deputy the authority to make the final decision on the appeal of a procurement protest. The director acted within his statutory authority under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 38-462 (2001) in delegating review of procurement protests.


OUTCOME: On the appeal from the trial court, the judgment was reversed and the case was remanded. The opinion of court of appeals was vacated.

Bennett v. Napolitano


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-03-0245-SA


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


206 Ariz. 520; 81 P.3d 311; 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 138; 414 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 3



December 4, 2003, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Jurisdiction accepted; relief denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners -- state legislators -- brought this special action challenging the Arizona governor's use of the item veto in 11 specified instances and alleging, as to each, that the governor exceeded her veto authority under the Arizona Constitution.


OVERVIEW: Of the 11 vetoes challenged, nine involved provisions in the general appropriations bill, and two pertained, respectively, to provisions in the Education and the Health and Welfare omnibus reconciliation bills (ORBs). The legislators urged that the court hold the vetoes unconstitutional and order the governor and all affected state officers and departments to implement the legislature's budget package without regard to the vetoes. The court accepted jurisdiction of the petition and held the legislators lacked standing to challenge the governor's vetoes made in connection with the legislative budget package. Although it could waive the standing requirement in an exceptional case, it declined to do so here. Even where instances of misuse of the governor's veto power could be present as alleged, the record also reflected what appeared to be non-recurring instances of unconventional budget structuring, failure to attempt legislative override or to obtain authorization to maintain the action, and numerous apparent violations of the single subject rule in the ORBs. Accordingly, notions of restraint prompted the court to abstain from further consideration of this matter.


OUTCOME: Relief was denied.

State v. Davis


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-01-0423-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


206 Ariz. 377; 79 P.3d 64; 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 132; 415 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 48



October 30, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Arizona v. Davis, 158 L. Ed. 2d 723, 124 S. Ct. 2097, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 3353 (U.S., May 3, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals Division One No. 1 CA-CR 99-1028. Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR 99-90084(B). Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Dennis W. Dairman, Judge. Court of Appeals, Division One. Memorandum Decision (filed Oct. 16, 2001).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part; vacated in part, and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a 20-year-old man, sought review of an order from the Maricopa County Superior Court (Arizona), which sentenced him to a mandatory term of 52 years in prison without the possibility of parole after he was convicted of four counts of sexual misconduct with a minor. The victims were two post-pubescent teenage girls.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of four counts of sexual misconduct with a minor. On appeal, he contended that his four flat, consecutive, 13-year sentences violated the prohibitions against cruel and unusual punishment found in the Eight Amendment to the United States Constitution and Ariz. Const. art. II, § 15. The court agreed. The sentence struck the jurors, the trial judge, and the victims' mothers as an extraordinarily long sentence. Defendant's sexual relations with the victims involved neither actual nor threatened violence; the victims were willing participants. The victims sought out defendant; all acts occurred after the victims went voluntarily to defendant's home. Defendant did not have an adult criminal record. There was evidence in the record that defendant's intelligence and maturity level fell far below that of a normal young adult. The court held that the mandatory 52-year sentence was grossly disproportionate to defendant's crimes. The court further held that, because the State offered evidence of more than one offense to support its first charge against defendant and the events were not part of a single transaction, the charge was duplicitous.


OUTCOME: The court vacated defendant's conviction as to the first count and remanded for a new trial on that count. The court affirmed the remaining convictions. The court vacated the sentence imposed and remanded the case for resentencing.

State v. Dann


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-02-0042-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


206 Ariz. 371; 79 P.3d 58; 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 131; 412 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 3



October 29, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Decision reached on appeal by, Appeal after remand at State v. Dann, 220 Ariz. 351, 207 P.3d 604, 2009 Ariz. LEXIS 103 (Ariz., 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR 1999-003536. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable H. Jeffrey Coker, Judge.
State v. Dann, 205 Ariz. 557, 74 P.3d 231, 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 106 (Ariz., 2003)



DISPOSITION: Death sentence vacated; remanded for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court in Maricopa County (Arizona), which convicted him on three counts of first degree murder and one count of first degree burglary. Two of the murder convictions were reversed but the court affirmed one conviction for premeditated murder, three convictions for felony murder, and the conviction and sentence on the burglary charge. Review was then conducted on a death penalty sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was sentenced to death under an unconstitutional procedure that allowed the trial court, not the jury, to decide whether the death penalty should be imposed. Defendant's case was reviewed for harmless error in the sentencing procedure. Harmless error occurred only in cases where the record supported a finding beyond a reasonable doubt that no rational jury would have decided the issues of aggravating and mitigating circumstances differently than the trial court did. The court vacated the death penalty sentence, holding that (1) the judicial finding of an Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(F)(8) aggravating factor was harmless error because the jury had convicted defendant of three murders and the murders were temporally, spatially, and motivationally related; (2) the judicial finding of insufficient mitigating factors for leniency was not harmless error because the court could not conclude beyond a reasonable doubt that a reasonable jury might not have looked at the 11 mitigating factors presented differently than the trial court did; and (3) defendant had not raised evidence that would trigger a mental retardation review under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703.02.


OUTCOME: Defendant's death penalty sentence was vacated and the case was remanded for jury resentencing. Defendant's request for a mental retardation hearing was denied.

State v. Montano


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-99-0439-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


206 Ariz. 296; 77 P.3d 1246; 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 128; 412 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 11



October 21, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1] Pima County Superior Court No. CR-58016. Appeal from the Superior Court of Pima County. The Honorable Deborah J.S. Ward, Judge Pro Tempore.
State v. Montano, 204 Ariz. 413, 65 P.3d 61, 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 22 (Ariz., 2003).



DISPOSITION: Death sentence vacated; remanded for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Pima County Superior Court (Arizona), which sentenced him to death. He had been convicted of first-degree murder and conspiracy to commit first-degree murder.


OVERVIEW: The only issue before the supreme court was whether reversible error occurred when a trial judge sentenced defendant to death under a procedure that violated the right to a jury trial under U.S. Const. amend. VI. He had been convicted of first-degree murder and conspiracy to commit first-degree murder. The supreme court stated that it could not conclude that the Sixth Amendment violation constituted harmless error. Thus, it vacated the death sentence and remanded for resentencing under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703 and 13-703.1 (Supp. 2002). Given the evidence, the supreme court could not conclude that all reasonable juries would have found the death especially cruel. Whether the victim lost consciousness before the stabbing began or thereafter presented a question of fact and defendant was entitled to a jury determination of that issue. Further, the supreme court could not say that no reasonable jury would have weighed differently the established aggravating and mitigating circumstances or determined that the mitigating circumstances were sufficiently substantial to call for leniency, Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703E. Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703.02 was to apply at resentencing.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the death sentence and remanded for resentencing.

State v. Sansing


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-99-0438-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


206 Ariz. 232; 77 P.3d 30; 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 125; 409 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 23



September 24, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Sansing v. Arizona, 542 U.S. 939, 124 S. Ct. 2906, 159 L. Ed. 2d 816, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 4642 (2004)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Motion denied by, Stay vacated by, Certificate of appealability granted, in part, Certificate of appealability denied, in part Sansing v. Ryan, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 16597 (D. Ariz., Feb. 7, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR-98-003520. Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County. No. CR-98-003520. The Honorable Ronald S. Reinstein, Judge.
Sansing v. Arizona, 536 U.S. 954, 122 S. Ct. 2654, 153 L. Ed. 2d 830, 2002 U.S. LEXIS 4895 (2002)



DISPOSITION: Death sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The United States Supreme Court vacated defendant's death sentence and remanded for consideration as to whether it was reversible error when the trial judge sentenced the defendant to death under a procedure that violated a prior holding that Arizona's enumerated aggravating factors, under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703 (2001), operated as the functional equivalent of an element of a greater offense, which required that they be found by a jury.


OVERVIEW: A harmless error standard was used. There was evidence of defensive wounds, pleas for help, and attempts to resist, leaving no doubt of mental anguish. Defendant admitted that he raped the victim while she was bound. Defendant had ground a knife into the victim. Admissions showed the victim was conscious during the attack. Defendant rendered the victim helpless by binding her. The rape, facial wounds, neck ligatures, gagging, blindfolding, and grinding of the knife constituted violence beyond that necessary to kill. Any reasonable jury would have concluded that gratuitous violence was inflicted on the helpless victim and that the murder was especially heinous. There was no expert testimony that cocaine use impaired either defendant's capacity to control his conduct or to appreciate the wrongfulness of his actions. Defendant's wife's testimony that defendant was not acting himself was insufficient to establish impairment. Steps taken to avoid detection negated impairment. Defendant's age of 31 years had no mitigating weight. There was no showing of a causal link between the crimes and a lack of education. The victim's family's sentencing request was not proper mitigation evidence.


OUTCOME: The death sentence was affirmed.

Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-98-0278-AP

September 24, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at Nordstrom v. Cruikshank, 213 Ariz. 434, 142 P.3d 1247, 2006 Ariz. App. LEXIS 109 (Ariz. Ct. App., 2006)
Subsequent appeal at Nordstrom v. Leonardo, 214 Ariz. 545, 155 P.3d 1069, 2007 Ariz. App. LEXIS 60 (Ariz. Ct. App., 2007)
Subsequent appeal at State v. Nichols, 219 Ariz. 170, 195 P.3d 207, 2008 Ariz. App. LEXIS 148 (Ariz. Ct. App., 2008)
Appeal after remand at, Decision reached on appeal by State v. Nordstrom, 2012 Ariz. LEXIS 166 (Ariz., July 26, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Pima County Superior Court No. CR-55947. Appeal from the Superior Court in Pima County. No. CR-55947. The Honorable Michael Cruikshank, Judge Pro Tempore.
State v. Nordstrom, 200 Ariz. 229, 25 P.3d 717, 2001 Ariz. LEXIS 89 (Ariz., 2001)



DISPOSITION: Remanded for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court in Pima County, Arizona, convicted defendant of first degree murder and sentenced defendant to death. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that reversible error occurred when the trial judge sentenced him to death under a procedure the United States Supreme Court held unconstitutional. The appellate court had to examine a death sentence imposed under Arizona's suspended capital sentencing statutes for harmless error, and found that each of defendant's murders satisfied the Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(F)(1) aggravating factor. The murders occurred simultaneously with the robberies of each establishment and therefore facilitated defendant's ability to secure a pecuniary gain, an aggravating circumstance pursuant to Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(F)(5). Under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(F)(8), because the armed robberies and murders were committed during a continuous course of criminal conduct at each place, they were temporally, spatially, and motivationally related. Any error with respect to the aggravating factors was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt. A jury, hearing the same mitigation evidence as the trial court, could have found one or more of the statutory mitigating factors to be proven. Although the evidence was not compelling, defendant did present evidence from an expert on the issue.


OUTCOME: Defendant's death sentences were vacated and remanded for resentencing by a jury.

DISPOSITION: Convictions affirmed, sentences for armed robberies and life sentence for the death of Curry affirmed. Death sentences for the murders of Moniz and Bloxham to be addressed in a supplemental opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Pima County Superior Court (Arizona), which, in a jury trial, convicted defendant of three counts of first-degree felony murder and three counts of armed robbery.


OVERVIEW: After being taken into custody, defendant and an accomplice separately confessed that they had robbed a restaurant and shot three victims. The court, in affirming defendant's conviction, found no error in the trial court's admission of evidence obtained during a warrantless search of defendant's truck. Although the truck had already been impounded, the police had probable cause, based upon defendant's confession, to believe that a weapon would be located in the truck. Defendant was not entitled to suppression of statements obtained in violation of the Vienna Convention on Consular Relations, Apr. 24, 1963, art. 36, 21 U.S.T. 77, 596 U.N.T.S. 261. Defendant's confession was not coerced. A juror questionnaire stating that defendant was not a U.S. citizen was permissible under Ariz. R. Crim. P. 18.5(e). A peremptory strike based upon a juror's apparent reluctance to serve was non-discriminatory. Defendant was not entitled to a lesser included offense instruction. Use of dual juries was not prejudicial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction.

Proksa v. Ariz. State Schs. for the Deaf


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-02-0388-CQ


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


205 Ariz. 627; 74 P.3d 939; 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 107; 415 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 60; 20 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 621



August 21, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] United States District Court No. CIV 02-412-TUC-WDB. Certified Questions from the United States District Court for the District of Arizona. The Honorable William D. Browning, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Certified question answered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants were two long-time employees of appellee Arizona State Schools for the Deaf and the Blind. After their employment was terminated in 2002, the employees filed suit against the schools alleging that they had been wrongfully terminated. The schools removed the suit to federal court. The United States District Court for the District of Arizona certified two questions of Arizona law to the Supreme Court of Arizona.


OVERVIEW: Until 1993, the statutes created a property interest in continued employment for permanent employees of the schools that was protected by the Due Process Clause. Employees could be terminated only after a hearing establishing appropriate cause. The employees contended that, after completing their periods of probation, they each effectively entered into employment contracts with the schools that provided that they could not be discharged without cause and that the 1993 amendments to Ariz. Rev. Stat. §§ 15-1325 and -1326 did not apply to them without their assent or acceptance and consideration. The Supreme Court of Arizona held that generally statutes did not create contract rights. The legislature had the unquestioned right to create and abolish offices in the public interest, and that right necessarily included the power to fix or alter the term, the mode of appointment, and compensation. That was inconsistent with the assertion that statutes governing public employment generally created enforceable contract rights. To the extent that the certified question asked whether the employees had a property interest in continued employment by the State, the answer was that they did not.


OUTCOME: The first certified question from the district court was answered in the affirmative. Given its answer to the first certified question, it was not necessary for the supreme court to address the second certified question.

 

150 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


State v. Dann


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-02-0042-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


205 Ariz. 557; 74 P.3d 231; 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 106; 416 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 24



August 14, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at, Remanded by State v. Dann, 79 P.3d 58, 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 131 (Ariz., Oct. 29, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County, Superior Court No. CR-1999-003536. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable H. Jeffrey Coker, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Convictions affirmed in part; reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review from a judgment of the Superior Court in Maricopa County (Arizona), wherein he was convicted of three counts of first-degree murder and one count of first-degree burglary. The trial court sentenced appellant to death and to a concurrent term of imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: Appellant entered his girlfriend's brother's apartment and fatally shot him, his friend, and appellant's girlfriend. He borrowed a gun, and various witnesses had spoken with appellant about his intention to kill someone. He was arrested, charged, and convicted. On appeal, the court found that Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-1101(1) (Supp. 1999) was constitutional and not vague as applied. The court found the jury instruction on premeditation was error, which was harmless as to the girlfriend's brother, due to appellant's stated intent to kill him, but required reversal as to the other two victims, because there was no overwhelming showing of appellant's premeditation. The evidence supported the felony murder convictions, predicated on burglary based on an intent to commit assault, and there was no violation of the merger doctrine. Exclusion of appellant's evidence regarding third-party culpability and the victims' use of drugs was proper, as it was a "reach," and denial of appellant's motion for a mistrial was also proper. Appellant's right to be present at trial was not violated to the extent of requiring a new trial, as it was either waived or fundamental error was not shown.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions of appellant for felony murder of the three victims, for premeditated murder of appellant's girlfriend's brother, and for first-degree burglary. The court reversed the premeditated murder convictions, as to the other two victims.

 

151 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


State v. Jones


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-99-0536-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


205 Ariz. 445; 72 P.3d 1264; 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 104; 404 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 13



July 7, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by, Motion granted by Arizona v. Jones, 540 U.S. 1141, 124 S. Ct. 1043, 157 L. Ed. 2d 952, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 590 (2004)
Appeal after remand at State v. Jones, 2008 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 343 (2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR-96-04691. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. No. CR-96-04691. The Honorable Stephen A. Gerst, Judge.
State v. Jones, 203 Ariz. 1, 49 P.3d 273, 2002 Ariz. LEXIS 112 (Ariz., 2002)



DISPOSITION: Death sentence vacated; remanded for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury found defendant guilty of first-degree premeditated murder, kidnapping of a minor under the age of 15, and 2 counts of sexual assault of a minor under the age of 15. The judge in the Superior Court in Maricopa County (Arizona) conducted a sentencing hearing on the first-degree murder conviction under former Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(B) (Supp. 2002) and sentenced defendant to death. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: In State v. Ring, (Ring III), the Supreme Court of Arizona concluded that it would examine a death sentence imposed under Arizona's superseded capital sentencing statute for harmless error. The sole issue in the case at bar was whether reversible error occurred when a trial judge sentenced defendant to death under a procedure found unconstitutional in Ring v. Arizona (Ring II). The record established beyond any reasonable doubt that the aggravating circumstance of the age of the victim was proved. Although the physical evidence clearly established beyond a reasonable doubt that the amount of violence used was greater than that necessary to kill the victim, the supreme court could not conclude beyond a reasonable doubt that a jury would have found the murder to have been especially cruel. A jury could have found that the victim lost consciousness at the beginning of the assault and thus did not suffer physical pain or mental anguish sufficient to support a finding that the murder was cruel. All of the non-statutory mitigating factors depended upon an assessment of witness credibility. The supreme court could not conclude that the error was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: The death sentence was vacated, and the case was remanded for resentencing.

State v. Canez


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-98-0488-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


205 Ariz. 620; 74 P.3d 932; 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 96; 403 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 24



June 30, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by, Motion granted by Arizona v. Canez, 540 U.S. 1141, 124 S. Ct. 1043, 157 L. Ed. 2d 953, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 591 (2004)
Appeal after remand at State v. Canez, 2007 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 2 (2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Pinal County, Superior Court No. CR-96-021235. Appeal from the Superior Court in Pinal County. The Honorable Boyd T. Johnson, Judge.
State v. Canez, 202 Ariz. 133, 42 P.3d 564, 2002 Ariz. LEXIS 20 (Ariz., 2002)



DISPOSITION: Vacated and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was sentenced to death in the Superior Court in Pinal County. He had an intelligence quotient score of 70. After it was determined that Arizona's capital sentencing scheme was unconstitutional, the present court consolidated all the cases, including defendant's case, in which the death penalty had been imposed, but not mandated, to determine if reversal or vacatur of the death sentences was required.


OVERVIEW: The court examined the aggravating circumstances found during the trial. It affirmed the trial judge's findings regarding the aggravating circumstances of defendant's 4 prior convictions for serious offenses and that the victim was 70 years older or over. It could not be said that the trial judge's findings regarding the other 2 aggravating circumstances, that the murder was done for pecuniary gain or that the murder was committed in an especially heinous, cruel, or depraved manner, were harmless error. In the context of mitigating factors, the present court could not say beyond a reasonable doubt that a reasonable jury would necessarily conclude that defendant failed to establish mental impairment by a preponderance of the evidence. Additionally, the state legislature passed a statute barring the execution of a mentally retarded person that was applicable on resentencing. The evidence concerning defendant's mental abilities was considered only from the viewpoint of establishing mitigation, not as a potential bar to execution.


OUTCOME: Defendant's death sentence was vacated, and the case was remanded for resentencing.

State v. Casey


No. 1 CA-CR 00-0476


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


205 Ariz. 359; 71 P.3d 351; 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 84



June 25, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected July 9, 2003.
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Casey v. Arizona, 540 U.S. 1079, 124 S. Ct. 932, 157 L. Ed. 2d 752, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 9084 (2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-01-0223-PR. Court of Appeals, Division One. Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR 00-000653. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Bernard J. Dougherty, Judge. Memorandum Decision of the Court of Appeals, Division One. Filed April 12, 2001.
State v. Casey, 2002 Ariz. LEXIS 51 (Ariz., Apr. 5, 2002)



DISPOSITION: Superior court judgement affirmed; decision of Court of Appeals affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Arizona Court of Appeals affirmed defendant's conviction for aggravated assault and approved a jury instruction given by the trial judge that the State had to prove the elements of the offense beyond a reasonable doubt but that defendant had to prove self-defense by a preponderance of the evidence. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that once self-defense was raised, case law treated lack of justification as an element of the offense that due process required the State to prove beyond a reasonable doubt. Defendant was required to prove by a preponderance of the evidence that the reason he shot the victim was to prevent the victim from causing serious physical injury to him or his pregnant girlfriend, Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-205. The appellate court noted that defendant was not required to disprove any element of the offense. Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-205 did not violate Ariz. Const. art. II, § 4. Defendant's reliance on prior cases was without merit, as they were based on statutory interpretation rather than Arizona's due process clause. The appellate court rejected as a matter of interpretation defendant's attempt to combine Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-404, which defined self-defense, with the introductory language in Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-403. Lack of self-defense was not an element of assault but an affirmative defense under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-205 that defendant had to prove by a preponderance of the evidence. The legislature had the constitutional authority to shift the burden of proof.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Duncan v. Scottsdale Med. Imaging, LTD.


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-02-0191-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


205 Ariz. 306; 70 P.3d 435; 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 82; 415 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 43



June 16, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals Division One No. 1 CA-CV 01-0536. Maricopa County Superior Court No. CV 99-019784. Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County No. CV 99-019784. The Honorable Sherry Hutt, Judge. Court of Appeals, Division One, Memorandum Decision, Filed April 30, 2002.
Duncan v. Scottsdale Med. Imaging, 2002 Ariz. LEXIS 186 (Ariz., Oct. 31, 2002)



DISPOSITION: Court of Appeals, Division One vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant patient challenged a decision from the Arizona Court of Appeals, Division One, which affirmed a trial court's decision to grant summary judgment in favor of respondent health care provider in a battery case.


OVERVIEW: The health care provider performed a magnetic resonance imaging examination on the patient. Due to a back condition, sedation of the patient was required. The patient refused to consent to the sedation, unless she was given certain drugs. She informed the nurse of her wishes. After the nurse incorrectly told the patient that the medicine was one that she requested, the patient consented to the injection. Later, the patient suffered a severe allergic reaction. She then filed a battery action against the health care provider. After the appellate court affirmed the trial court's decision to grant summary judgment in favor of the health care provider, the patient sought review. In vacating the appellate court's decision, the court determined that the patient stated a cause of action for battery, because her consent to the procedure was ineffective. The evidence showed that the nurse acted in willful disregard of the consent given. Further, the patient was allowed to bring a battery claim, despite the prohibitive language in Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 12-562(B). Section 12-562(B) violated Ariz. Const. art. 18, § 6, because the common law claim of battery was totally abrogated.


OUTCOME: The decision of the appellate court was vacated. The trial court's decision to grant summary judgment in favor of the health care provider was reversed. The case was remanded for further proceedings.

Lynn v. Reinstein


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-02-0435-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


205 Ariz. 186; 68 P.3d 412; 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 66; 400 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 34



May 19, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by, Motion granted by Lynn v. Reinstein, 540 U.S. 1141, 157 L. Ed. 2d 952, 124 S. Ct. 1037, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 586 (2004)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Sub nomine at State v. Glassel, 2005 Ariz. LEXIS 81 (Ariz., Aug. 10, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals, Division One, No. 1 CA-SA 02-0309. Maricopa County, Superior Court No. CR 2000-006872. Special Action from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Peter C. Reinstein, Judge. Court of Appeals, Division One, Decision Order, filed December 23, 2002.



DISPOSITION: Special Action from the Superior Court in Maricopa County AFFIRMED. Court of Appeals, Division One, Decision Order, AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant opened fire at a homeowners' association meeting, killing the victim, petitioner's wife. In the murder trial, the husband asserted a right under Arizona's Victims' Bill of Rights, Ariz. Const. art. 2, § 2.1, to tell the jurors what sentence he thought they should impose on defendant. The trial court and the Court of Appeals of Arizona, Division One, held that he could not state his opinion on that subject. The husband appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trial judge allowed the victim impact statements, but denied the motion as to sentencing recommendations, concluding that a victim's sentencing recommendations were not relevant to either the mitigating or aggravating factors involved in capital sentencing and that precluding the husband's opinion on sentencing did not violate his rights as a crime victim. The court also found Arizona case law clear that sentencing recommendations offered by a deceased's survivors had no relevance in a capital case. Following binding precedent from the United States Supreme Court, the Supreme Court of Arizona held that the Eighth Amendment to the United States Constitution prohibited a victim from making a sentencing recommendation to the jury in a capital case. The victims' right to speak was not unlimited. Statements relevant to the harm caused by the defendant's criminal acts were no longer barred by the Eighth Amendment. But statements regarding sentencing exceeded those bounds, violated the Eighth Amendment, and therefore were prohibited.


OUTCOME: The special action from the trial court was affirmed. The decision order of the court of appeals was affirmed.

State v. Hickman


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-01-0424-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


205 Ariz. 192; 68 P.3d 418; 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 65; 400 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 19



May 19, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by State v. Hickman, 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 87 (Ariz., June 30, 2003)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Hickman v. Ryan, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 111206 (D. Ariz., Aug. 28, 2009)
Post-conviction relief denied at, Petition granted by State v. Hickman, 2017 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 1557 (Oct. 19, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals, Division One, Nos. 1 CA-CR 00-0215, 1 CA-CR 00-0542 (Consolidated). Maricopa County, Superior Court No. CR 98-009183. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Linda A. Akers, Judge. Memorandum Decision of the Court of Appeals, Division One, filed October 25, 2001.



DISPOSITION: Court of Appeals' decision vacated in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Arizona Court of Appeals, Division One, remanded the case back to the superior court for a new trial, after defendant was convicted of three counts of sexual exploitation of a minor for downloading child pornography from the Internet, finding that the trial court erred in refusing to strike a venireperson for cause. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the courts should continue to follow a prior case's automatic reversal rule, where reversal was the only feasible way to vindicate a party's substantial right to peremptory challenges. A majority of state courts, following a U.S. Supreme Court decision, held that the curative use of a peremptory challenge violated neither a constitutional nor a rule-based or statute-based right. A showing of prejudice was required before a case would be reversed when a defendant used a peremptory challenge to remove a juror the trial court should have excused for cause. The appellate court concluded that a defendant in a criminal case had to show prejudice and the Arizona Constitution and harmless error statute obligated the appellate court to review trial error in criminal cases under a harmless error standard. Sufficiently compelling reasons existed for overruling State v. Huerta, 175 Ariz. 262, 855 P.2d 776 (1993), where the right of a peremptory challenge was not given the same weight as the right to a fair and impartial jury. Defendant's case was fair and impartial, and he did not exhaust his peremptory challenges. An objectionable juror was not forced upon him.


OUTCOME: The judgment was vacated as to the court of appeals' decision that reversed based on the prior case, and affirmed as to defendant's convictions and sentences.

State v. Phillips

Post-conviction proceeding at, Remanded by State v. Phillips, 2011 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 1166 (2011)

State v. Ring, 2002 Ariz. LEXIS 102 (Ariz., June 27, 2002)



DISPOSITION: Death sentence vacated; remanded for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision from the Superior Court in Pima County (Arizona), which sentenced him to death after convicting him of felony murder and other offenses. The sentence was upheld on appeal, but after a United States Supreme Court decision in another case, the court conducted a harmless error review of defendant's sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty of attempted murder, armed robbery, kidnapping, aggravated assault, premeditated murder, and felony murder. During the sentencing hearing, the trial court found that two aggravating circumstances outweighed the two mitigating circumstances. Defendant's conviction for premeditated murder was reversed, but all of the other convictions and the sentences were affirmed. After a decision was rendered by the United States Supreme Court in another case, the court reviewed defendant's death sentence for harmless error. In vacating the sentence, the court determined that there was sufficient evidence presented to prove the aggravating circumstance for prior convictions under Ariz. Rev. However, the jury could have made a different determination about whether or not sufficient evidence of pecuniary gain had been presented under § 13-703(F)(5). There was little evidence as to defendant's intent and motivation regarding the murder of the witness. Finally, the court noted that a jury might have found additional mitigating factors or weighed them differently during sentencing.


OUTCOME: The court vacated defendant's death sentence. The case was remanded for resentencing.

Post-conviction relief denied at, Petition granted by State v. Finch, 2016 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 599 (2016)
Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Finch, 2016 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 1258 (2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Pima County Superior Court No. CR-61452. Appeal from the Superior Court in Pima County. The Honorable Bernardo P. Velasco, Judge.
State v. Finch, 202 Ariz. 410, 46 P.3d 421, 2002 Ariz. LEXIS 84 (Ariz., 2002)
State v. Ring, 2002 Ariz. LEXIS 102 (Ariz., June 27, 2002)



DISPOSITION: Death sentence vacated, and case remanded for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was previously convicted in the Pima County Superior Court, Arizona, for a litany of crimes, including several robberies and first-degree felony murder, and received the death penalty. After Arizona's capital sentencing scheme was found to have violated defendant's Sixth Amendment right to a jury trial, the matter was remanded to review his sentence.


OVERVIEW: At defendant's sentencing hearing, the trial judge found two aggravating circumstances; namely, that defendant committed the murder with the expectation of pecuniary gain and that he was convicted of prior serious offenses. After reviewing the mitigating circumstances, the trial court found either aggravating circumstance was sufficient to outweigh the mitigating factors presented. The supreme court held, in light of defendant's admissions of pecuniary motive, that no reasonable jury could have found that defendant did not shoot the victim for pecuniary gain; therefore, it declined to disturb the trial judge's finding that the aggravating circumstance of pecuniary gain rendered him eligible for the death penalty. However, the supreme court could not say that a reasonable jury would not have found additional mitigating factors or weighed the mitigating factors differently. The supreme court concluded that it could not say beyond a reasonable doubt that, if a jury found additional mitigating circumstances or weighed the mitigating circumstances differently, the jury would not have found them sufficiently substantial to call for leniency.


OUTCOME: The erroneous sentencing procedure used by the trial court did not result in harmless error; therefore, defendant's death sentence was vacated, and the matter remanded for resentencing.

State v. Cropper


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-00-0544-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


205 Ariz. 181; 68 P.3d 407; 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 61; 399 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 15



May 5, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Supplemental opinion at, Remanded by State v. Cropper, 76 P.3d 424, 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 114 (Ariz., 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR97-03949. Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County. The Honorable David R. Cole, Judge.
State v. Ring, 2002 Ariz. LEXIS 102 (Ariz., June 27, 2002)



DISPOSITION: Convictions affirmed; sentencing issues to be determined in a subsequent opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pleaded guilty to first-degree murder, conspiracy to commit first-degree murder, and three counts of promoting prison contraband in connection with the murder of an Arizona Department of Corrections (ADOC) officer. After the aggravation/mitigation hearing, the Superior Court for Maricopa County (Arizona) found that the State had established three aggravating circumstances and sentenced defendant to death. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the prosecutor failed to give notice of the State's intent to prove the third aggravating circumstance prior serious conviction within the time period prescribed by former Ariz. R. Crim. P. 15.1.g(2). As a result, he unknowingly prejudiced himself by entering the guilty plea in the assault case. The State had not filed notice of the prior serious conviction aggravating circumstance within 10 days of defendant's first-degree murder conviction, as required by superseded Rule 15.1.g(2). However, the State could not give notice of the prior serious conviction aggravating circumstance until defendant had committed that crime. The Supreme Court of Arizona held that because defendant had actual, although oral, notice of the prosecutor's intent to use the aggravated assault conviction as a prior serious offense aggravating circumstance and the delay caused him no prejudice, he was adequately noticed of the prior serious conviction aggravating circumstance. The aggravated assault plea was entered at a proceeding unrelated to the first-degree murder trial and because defendant pleaded guilty to aggravated assault, he waived any right to direct appeal in that action.


OUTCOME: The convictions were affirmed. By separate order, the supreme court directed the parties to submit supplemental briefing in accordance with Ring III. Sentencing issues were to be addressed in a supplemental opinion.

State v. Lehr


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-97-0317-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


205 Ariz. 107; 67 P.3d 703; 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 60; 403 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 16



April 30, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected April 30, 2003.
Decision reached on appeal by, Appeal after remand at State v. Lehr, 2011 Ariz. LEXIS 37 (Ariz., July 13, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR 92-05731. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Stephen A. Gerst, Judge.
State v. Lehr, 201 Ariz. 509, 38 P.3d 1172, 2002 Ariz. LEXIS 15 (Ariz., 2002)



DISPOSITION: REMANDED FOR RESENTENCING.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder and received the death penalty. Following the United States Supreme Court's ruling that the procedure used by the Maricopa County Superior Court (Arizona) to sentence defendant to death was unconstitutional, the Arizona Supreme Court examined defendant's sentencing for reversible error.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court could not conclude that no reasonable probability existed that a jury would not have imposed the death penalty. It was impossible for the court to say just how a jury may have balanced the aggravating and mitigating circumstances in defendant's case. To find the error harmless, the appellate court had to determine that, if a jury had made the sentencing determination, and, if that jury had been presented with a single murder conviction rather than three, and, if it had considered only the Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(F)(1) and (F)(2) aggravators rather than also an (F)(6) aggravator, and, if it had considered the (F)(1) aggravator on the basis of kidnapping and sexual assault charges rather than multiple homicides, that jury would have found that the mitigating circumstances were not sufficiently substantial to call for leniency. Under these circumstances, the court held that the finding of error to be harmless was too speculative. Thus, defendant's death sentence was vacated and remanded for resentencing.


OUTCOME: Defendant's death sentence was vacated and remanded for resentencing.

State v. Rutledge


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-01-0129-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


205 Ariz. 7; 66 P.3d 50; 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 33; 397 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 21



April 7, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Supplemental opinion at, Remanded by State v. Rutledge, 76 P.3d 443, 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 124 (Ariz., 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR1997-05555. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Frank T. Galati, Judge.
State v. Ring, 2002 Ariz. LEXIS 102 (Ariz., June 27, 2002)



DISPOSITION: CONVICTIONS AND SENTENCES EXCEPT SENTENCE OF DEATH AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Superior Court in Maricopa County (Arizona) of armed robbery, first-degree murder, and attempted second-degree murder. Defendant was sentenced to the maximum of 21 years for armed robbery and 21 years for attempted murder. He was sentenced to death for the murder. Direct appeal to the Supreme Court of Arizona was mandatory when the trial court imposed a sentence of death. Ariz. R. Crim. P. 26.15 and 31.2(b).


OVERVIEW: Inter alia, defendant argued that he was denied a fair trial, because the trial court did not comply with Ariz. R. Evid. 613(b), which required that his accomplice's prior statement had to have been inconsistent before admitting into evidence his videotaped statement to police. Although the accomplice testified he could not remember any details about the alleged unknown shooter, the videotaped interview showed that he had a clear recollection of many details of the evening of the murder. For example, he remembered the color of defendant's brother's jersey. The Supreme Court of Arizona held that those statements had substantive value. The accomplice told the court and the jury that he lied to the police, as he was scared, was threatened, and was intoxicated. Admission of the entire videotape allowed the jurors to assess his demeanor and credibility and helped them decide which of his accounts to believe. The trial judge did not abuse his discretion by admitting the videotaped interview. Regarding the death sentence, because Ring III had been issued, the supreme court, by separate order, directed the parties to submit supplemental briefing in accordance with that opinion.


OUTCOME: The convictions were affirmed. The sentences for armed robbery and attempted murder were affirmed. The sentence on the murder conviction was to be addressed in a supplemental opinion.

Dickey v. City of Flagstaff


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-99-0273-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


205 Ariz. 1; 66 P.3d 44; 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 34; 397 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 37



April 7, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals, Division One, No. 1 CA-CV 98-0026. Coconino County Superior Court No. CV 95-0564. Appeal from the Superior Court in Coconino County. The Honorable J. Michael Flournoy, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, 197 Ariz. 422, 4 P.3d 965 (App. 1999).
Dickey v. City of Flagstaff, 197 Ariz. 422, 4 P.3d 965, 1999 Ariz. App. LEXIS 95 (Ariz. Ct. App., 1999).



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court in Coconino County, AFFIRMED. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, VACATED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant parents sued appellee city to recover for injuries their child sustained in a city park. The city claimed that, because the park was held open for recreational use, the parents could not prevail unless they showed that city employees were grossly negligent. The trial court granted summary judgment for the city. The parents appealed. The Court of Appeals, Division One (Arizona), affirmed. The parents appealed.


OVERVIEW: The issue was whether Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 33-1551's, the recreational use statute, requirement that the parent's show gross negligence or wilful or malicious conduct abrogated a cause of action that their child would have otherwise enjoyed, in violation of the anti-abrogation provision of the Arizona Constitution, Ariz. Const. art. 18, § 6. The Supreme Court of Arizona concluded that the child failed to establish that a right of action for simple negligence, against a municipality engaged in a governmental function, existed at common law. Arizona's anti-abrogation provision was designed to protect rights of action in existence at the time it was adopted, but not necessarily those later created. Accordingly, because a suit against a city for simple negligence could not have been maintained at the time the anti-abrogation provision was instituted, it was not protected by that provision, if the city was acting in its governmental capacity. The supreme court concluded that the city held the park open to the public for recreational use. Its maintenance of the park was a governmental function, and the recreational use statute shielded the city's acts, which was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The opinion of the court of appeals was vacated, and the judgment of the trial court was reinstated.

Johnson, Judge. Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County. Nos. CR-94-11396 and CR-94-11397 (Consolidated). The Honorable Jeffrey A. Hotham, Judge. Appeal from the Superior Court of Pima County. No. CR-55947. The Honorable Michael Cruikshank, Judge Pro Tempore.
Ring v. Arizona, 536 U.S. 584, 122 S. Ct. 2428, 153 L. Ed. 2d 556, 2002 U.S. LEXIS 4651 (2002)



DISPOSITION: Issues addressed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The United States Supreme Court granted certiorari review to defendant who was convicted of first degree murder and other felonies and was sentenced to death. The Supreme Court held that Arizona's capital sentencing scheme violated the Sixth Amendment right to a jury trial. The Supreme Court remanded the matter to the state court for resentencing.


OVERVIEW: On remand, the state supreme court considered the sentences of 31 defendants sentenced to death and had matters pending on direct appeal before the supreme court. The state supreme court consolidated all 31 death penalty cases then on direct appeal. The Supreme Court ruled that a jury had to determine certain aggravating circumstances in order to impose a death penalty. The state supreme court found that in some of the consolidated cases, the state supreme court's review would not allow the conclusion, beyond a reasonable doubt, that the error did not contribute to or affect the capital sentence. In those cases, the state supreme court had to remand for resentencing. Other cases would involve aggravators that would be regarded as established. In those cases, because a trier of fact had to determine whether mitigating circumstances called for leniency, the state supreme court would affirm a capital sentence only if it concluded, beyond a reasonable doubt, that no rational trier of fact would determine that the mitigating circumstances were sufficiently substantial to call for leniency. If it could not reach that conclusion, the case had to be remanded for resentencing.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court determined that some cases would be remanded for resentencing so that a jury could weigh and determine the appropriate mitigating circumstances. However, the state supreme court would affirm a capital sentence, if the aggravating circumstances were regarded as established and no mitigating circumstances called for leniency.
