State v. Grell


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-01-0275-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


205 Ariz. 57; 66 P.3d 1234; 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 28; 396 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 10



April 1, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at, Remanded by State v. Grell, 2006 Ariz. LEXIS 75 (Ariz., June 6, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR 1999-095294. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Barbara M. Jarrett, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Conviction affirmed; remanded for determination whether Grell was mentally retarded at the time of the incident and thus ineligible for the death penalty.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court in Maricopa County (Arizona) convicted defendant of first degree murder and sentenced him to death. At the sentencing hearing, the court found no significant mitigating circumstances, as defendant failed to prove by a preponderance of the evidence that he was mentally retarded. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant drove his two year old daughter to a remote area, poured gasoline on her, then lit her on fire. She died from severe burns and smoke inhalation. The state's highest court affirmed the conviction as there was overwhelming direct and circumstantial evidence of defendant's reflection for premeditation. However, regarding the imposition of the death penalty, after defendant was sentenced, in Atkins, the Unites States Supreme Court held that the Eighth Amendment prohibited the execution of mentally retarded persons. Due process demanded that defendant's mental retardation claim receive a hearing at which the constitutional principles announced in Atkins were considered. Although defendant presented testimony from two experts, and the State responded in kind, the adversarial procedure by which defendant's mental retardation was considered differed in nature and scope from the process created by the legislature in Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703.02, which did not take effect until after defendant's sentencing but contemplated a more thorough examination by experts selected by the trial judge, in consultation with the parties.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for first degree murder was affirmed but remanded for a redetermination of whether defendant was mentally retarded and therefore, ineligible for the death penalty.

State v. Montano


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-99-0439-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


204 Ariz. 413; 65 P.3d 61; 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 22; 397 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 9



March 17, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Remanded by State v. Montano, 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 128 (Ariz., Oct. 21, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Pima County Superior Court No. CR-58016. Appeal from the Superior Court of Pima County. The Honorable Deborah Ward, Judge Pro Tempore.



DISPOSITION: Convictions and sentence on conspiracy charge affirmed; capital sentencing issues deferred.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Pima County Superior Court (Arizona), which convicted defendant on one count of first degree murder and one count of conspiracy to commit first degree murder.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant argued, inter alia, the delay between the crime and the indictment prejudiced him by denying his right to cross-examination. Defendant also challenged the sufficiency of the evidence on the murder conviction. The appellate court found that defendant failed to raise his arraignment argument in a pretrial motion. Because defendant did not make a motion to dismiss 20 days before trial, defendant was barred from doing so by Ariz. R. Crim. P. 16.1(b) and (c). In challenging the sufficiency of the evidence, defendant argued that the State's case was built on the improperly admitted videotaped eyewitness testimony and, had that been excluded, there would not have been sufficient evidence of first degree murder. The court found that the videotaped evidence was properly admitted. The State acted reasonably and in good faith to procure the witnesses attendance at trial. Further, the witnesses testified at the preliminary hearing and both were cross-examined under oath. Therefore, the preliminary hearing videotape bore sufficient indicia of reliability and afforded the trier of fact a satisfactory basis for evaluating the truth of the prior statement.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions were affirmed.

State v. Thompson


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-01-0435-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


204 Ariz. 471; 65 P.3d 420; 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 19; 395 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 6



March 12, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals Division One No. 1 CA-CR 00-0439. Maricopa County Superior No. CR 99-07826. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa Count. The Honorable Bernard J. Dougherty, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One. 201 Ariz. 273, 34 P.3d 382 (App. 2001).
State v. Thompson, 2002 Ariz. LEXIS 55 (Ariz., Apr. 23, 2002)



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricipa County Affirmed. Opinion of the Court of Appeal, Division One Vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted, before the Maricopa County Superior Court, Arizona, of first degree murder. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant shot and killed his wife. At trial, defendant did not deny killing his wife, but claimed that he did so in the heat of passion, making the killing manslaughter or, at most, second degree murder. On appeal, defendant challenged the constitutionality of Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-1105(A)(1) (2001). The supreme court was called upon to decide whether the definition of premeditation, contained within Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-1101(1) (2001), abolished the requirement of actual reflection altogether, whether it eliminated the requirement of direct proof of actual reflection, or whether it substituted for the necessary proof of actual reflection the mere passage of enough time to permit reflection. The supreme court concluded that the jury instruction given in this case was erroneous, but it also concluded that the flawed instruction did not affect the verdict. The supreme court directed trial judges, in the future, as to how to instruct juries with regard to premeditation, and clarified that the State could not use the passage of time as a proxy for premeditation. Finally, the supreme court concluded that § 13-1105(A)(1) was not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The supreme court vacated the appellate court's opinion, and affirmed the conviction.

State v. Ehrlich (In re Leon G.)


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-01-0062-PR CONSOLIDATED WITH Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-01-0063-SA


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


204 Ariz. 15; 59 P.3d 779; 2002 Ariz. LEXIS 230; 389 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 6



December 16, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals Division One No. 1 CA-MH 00-0004. Yuma County Superior Court No. SC98M00050. Court of Appeals Division One No. 1 CA-SA 01-0027. Maricopa County Superior Court No. CV-MH-99-1189. Appeal from the Superior Court of Yuma County. No. SC98M00050. The Honorable Kirby D. Kongable, Judge . Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, 199 Ariz. 375, 18 P.3d 169 (App. 2001). Special Action from the Superior Court of Maricopa County. No. CV-MH-99-1189. The Honorable Alan S. Kamin, Judge. Special Action from Order of the Court of Appeals, Division One.



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court of Yuma County AFFIRMED. Opinion of the Court of Appeals Division One VACATED. Special Action from the Superior Court of Maricopa County REVERSED and REMANDED. Special Action from Order of the Court of Appeals, Division One JURISDICTION ACCEPTED, RELIEF GRANTED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In two separate proceedings, the trial courts ordered civil commitments to a state mental hospital of the real parties in interest, alleged sexually violent individuals (individuals), as defined in Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 36-3701.7. The Court of Appeal, Arizona, vacated the orders of commitment. The state's petition for review was granted, and the matters were consolidated.


OVERVIEW: The individuals argued that Arizona's Sexually Violent Person Act (SVP), Ariz. Rev. Stat. §§ 36-3701 to 36-3717, lacked any requirement that the state prove an alleged SVP's mental disorder caused him or her to have serious difficulty in controlling behavior, and they argued that the statute was unconstitutional. The appellate court did not agree that due process mandated explicit references to words such as "control" or "capacity" in civil commitment statutes for several reasons. The appellate court determined that the SVP statute narrowed the class of persons subject to civil commitment. Further, the appellate court directed trial judges to instruct juries in future SVP proceedings that the state must prove, beyond a reasonable doubt, that the person had a mental disorder that made it highly probable that the person would engage in future acts of sexual violence. A finding of dangerousness, alone, would be insufficient. The individual's dangerousness must be caused by a mental disorder that caused the person to have serious difficulty in controlling his or her behavior.


OUTCOME: In the first case, the appellate court vacated the appellate court's decision and affirmed the trial court's decision. In the second case, the appellate court reversed the trial court's order releasing the individual from the state mental hospital and remanded for further proceedings.

Simat Corp. v. Ariz. Health Care Cost Containment Sys.


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-02-0324-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


203 Ariz. 454; 56 P.3d 28; 2002 Ariz. LEXIS 180; 385 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 12



October 22, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion for Clarication/Reconsideration Denied December 10, 2002.
Reconsideration denied by Simat Corp. v. Ariz. Health Care Cost Containment Sys., 2002 Ariz. LEXIS 217 (Ariz., Dec. 10, 2002)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals, Division One, No. 1 CA-CV 00-0334. Maricopa County Superior Court No. CV 99-014614. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Kenneth L. Fields, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, Simat Corp. v. Ariz. Health Care Cost Containment Sys., 200 Ariz. 506, 29 P.3d 281 (App. 2001).
Simat Corp. v. State Health Care Cost Containment Sys., 200 Ariz. 506, 29 P.3d 281, 2001 Ariz. App. LEXIS 113 (Ariz. Ct. App., 2001)



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County, AFFIRMED IN PART AND REMANDED. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, VACATED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In suit for declaratory and injunctive relief, the Arizona Court of Appeals, Division One, reversed the trial court and held that the state constitution permitted defendant state agency to refuse to fund medically necessary abortion procedures for pregnant women suffering from serious illness while funding such procedures for rape or incest victims or when the abortion was necessary to save the woman's life. The instant court granted review.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs were doctors who performed abortions. The agency provided Medicaid services to qualified Arizona women with incomes at or below 140 percent of the federally set poverty level. The doctors argued that the funding policy, set forth in Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 35-196.02, prevented medically necessary abortions for agency patients and violated the Arizona Constitution. Basing its decision entirely on the Arizona Constitution, the instant court concluded that, insofar as the state scheme permitted funding of abortions for one class of pregnant women, the state constitution did not permit it to deny funding for others for whom abortions were medically necessary to save the woman's health. Applying strict scrutiny to the fundamental right to choose, the instant court concluded that the state's legitimate interest in promoting childbirth was not so compelling as to permit it to effectively destroy an indigent woman's opportunity to choose to take medically necessary steps to preserve her health. It also observed that the trial court's requirement that the agency fund medically necessary abortions to the same extent that it funded other pregnancy-related services was too broad.


OUTCOME: The opinion of the court of appeals was vacated. The trial court's judgment was affirmed insofar as it precluded application of the statute to situations in which therapeutic abortions were medically necessary to enable doctors to administer treatment necessary to address serious health problems of pregnant agency patients. The case was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with the instant court's opinion.

May v. McNally


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-02-0215-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


203 Ariz. 425; 55 P.3d 768; 2002 Ariz. LEXIS 177; 384 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 22



October 11, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by May v. Brewer, 155 L. Ed. 2d 314, 123 S. Ct. 1583, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 2216 (U.S., 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals, Division One, No. 1 CA-SA 02-0073. Maricopa County Superior Court No. CV 2001-006078. Special Action from the Superior Court of Maricopa County. The Honorable Colleen A. McNally, Judge. Court of Appeals, Division One, 203 Ariz. 13, 49 P.3d 285 (App. 2002).
May v. McNally, 203 Ariz. 13, 49 P.3d 285, 2002 Ariz. App. LEXIS 93 (Ariz. Ct. App., 2002)



DISPOSITION: Special Action from the Superior Court of Maricopa County, JUDGMENT AFFIRMED. Court of Appeals, Division One, OPINION VACATED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Arizona voters approved the Citizens Clean Elections Act. The case presented a challenge to the Act's key funding provision. The instant court was asked to determine whether the 10 percent surcharge on criminal and civil fines required by the Act violated the First Amendment.


OVERVIEW: A state legislator received a parking ticket and fine, on which a 10 percent surcharge, authorized by the Act, was assessed. He refused to pay the surcharge, claiming it violated his First Amendment right to free speech because it might be used to fund candidates' campaigns whose views he opposed. He filed a federal t action, which was dismissed because the Tax Injunction Act, 28 U.S.C.S. § 1341, deprived the court of subject matter jurisdiction. He filed in Maricopa County Superior Court, urging the state courts to find the Act unconstitutional. The group that sponsored the initiative intervened in support of the Act and the trial court upheld the constitutionality of Act's surcharge on civil and criminal fines. The court of appeals reversed, finding the surcharge unconstitutionally restrained free speech, and enjoined the State from imposing it. The instant court stayed the court of appeals' opinion, and granted review to determine whether the Act's surcharge provision impermissibly compelled the surcharge payers' political speech, in violation of the First Amendment's freedom of speech guarantee. The instant court held the surcharge funding provision was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The surcharge funding provision of the Act was constitutional. The instant court therefore vacated the opinion of the court of appeals and reinstated the judgment in favor of the real parties in interest.

In re Gatti


Supreme Court No. SB-02-0128-D


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


2002 Ariz. LEXIS 154



September 12, 2002, Decided  
September 12, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] Disciplinary Commission No. 02-4002.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State Bar of Arizona brought a reciprocal disciplinary proceeding against respondent attorney who had been censured in another state. A hearing officer and the Attorney Disciplinary Commission of the Supreme Court of Arizona found that the attorney had violated Ariz. Sup. Ct. R. 42. The commission issued a recommendation that the attorney be censured and ordered to pay the costs of the proceedings.


OVERVIEW: The Oregon State Bar charged the attorney with violating Or. Code Prof. Resp. DR 1-102(A)(3), 7-102(A)(5), and Or. Rev. Stat. § 9.527(4), because he misrepresented himself as a chiropractor during his representation of several chiropractors who had been charged with racketeering and fraud regarding possibly fraudulent workers' compensation claims. The attorney claimed that he thought he could misrepresent his identity or purpose to gather information without violating the code of professional responsibility. The Supreme Court of Oregon reviewed the decision of the trial panel de novo and held that the bar was not estopped from prosecuting the attorney, that he had violated both the rules and statute identified above, and that the attorney's defense based on the state and federal constitutions was not well taken. The appropriate sanction was a public reprimand. The matter then came before the Disciplinary Commission of the Supreme Court of Arizona for its determination of whether to impose the same discipline upon the attorney as that imposed by the order of the Supreme Court of Oregon.


OUTCOME: The attorney was censured and ordered to pay costs.

State v. Smith


Supreme Court No. CR-01-0272-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


203 Ariz. 75; 50 P.3d 825; 2002 Ariz. LEXIS 123; 379 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 19



July 29, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Reconsideration denied by, 09/29/2002


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of Yuma County. The Honorable John N. Nelson. Nos. S1400CR198311801 and S1400CR198311817.
Smith v. Stewart, 140 F.3d 1263, 1998 U.S. App. LEXIS 6830 (9th Cir. Ariz., 1998)



DISPOSITION: Denial of defendant's motion for change of judge approved; decision regarding sentence reserved.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of murder. Prior to the sentencing hearing in the Superior Court of Yuma County (Arizona) the judge informed the parties that the victim's son and daughter-in-law were longtime employees of the Yuma County Superior Court with whom he had had some contact in the past. Defendant filed a motion for a change of judge under Ariz. R. Crim. P. 10.1.b. but the motion was not timely filed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant asserted that his motion was in fact a "hybrid" between an Ariz. R. Crim. P. 10.3 motion for change of venue and a Rule 10.1 motion for change of judge, and that, although the written motion referred only to the judge in question, its aim was to disqualify all the Yuma County Superior Court judges. The State argued that the motion could not be treated as a Rule 10.3 motion because that rule referred only to trials and specifically excluded motions based on "the interest or prejudice of the trial judge." The appellate court agreed that the hybrid motion was properly treated as a motion for change of judge for cause. The record indicated that defendant knew of the professional relationship at least six months prior to the disclosure. Defendant failed to timely file his Rule 10.1 motion. Defendant claimed on appeal that he based his motion on an ethical conflict, to which objection could not be waived. The appellate court held that defendant expressly disclaimed any reliance on the Arizona Code of Judicial Conduct as a basis for the recusal. The appellate court also held that the sentencing judge erred in applying Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703 after the Ring decision.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was approved in part.

 

172 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


Hughes v. Jorgenson


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-01-0336-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


203 Ariz. 71; 50 P.3d 821; 2002 Ariz. LEXIS 121; 378 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 78



July 23, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals, Division Two, No. 2 CA-SA 01-0058. Graham County Superior Court No. CR-2000-174. Special Action from the Superior Court in Graham County. The Honorable Cindy Jorgenson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State charged defendant with obstructing a criminal prosecution, conducting a fraudulent scheme, wilful concealment of evidence, and conflict of interest. The trial court found probable cause to believe that defendant had committed the crimes charged and the Arizona Court of Appeals declined jurisdiction of defendant's special action. Defendant appealed and the prosecution of all three charges were stayed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was a county sheriff and his sister was stopped for an apparent traffic violation. Defendant's sister was released to his custody and she was never arrested. Defendant asked the deputy who pulled his sister over to prepare a report of the incident, and after reviewing the report, asked the deputy to omit certain information and made suggested editorial changes. The edited report included relevant details of the traffic stop, but omitted facts that could be considered irrelevant or personal. The supreme court granted review to determine whether the term "substantial interest" as it was used in Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 38-503(B) (2001) encompassed defendant's conduct. The State argued that defendant's conduct fell within the ambit of the conflict of interest statute since both defendant and his sister had some financial interest to gain or lose by her arrest, incarceration and prosecution for drug possession. The supreme court ruled that the interests involved did not fall within the scope of the conflict of interest statute. Absent any proof of non-remote pecuniary or proprietary interests, the trial court's finding of probable cause was in error.


OUTCOME: The finding of probable cause was reversed, the conflict of interest charge was dismissed, and the stay was dissolved.

State v. Jones


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-99-0536-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


203 Ariz. 1; 49 P.3d 273; 2002 Ariz. LEXIS 112; 378 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 32



July 10, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Reconsideration denied by, 09/24/2002
Vacated by, Remanded by State v. Jones, 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 104 (Ariz., July 7, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR-96-04691. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Stephen A. Gerst, Judge.



DISPOSITION: CONVICTIONS AND ALL SENTENCES EXCEPT SENTENCE OF DEATH, AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury for the Superior Court in Maricopa County (Arizona) found defendant guilty of first-degree premeditated murder, kidnapping, and two counts of sexual assault of a minor. The trial judge sentenced him to death on the murder charge, in addition to several noncapital sentences. Because the trial judge sentenced defendant to death for the murder, direct appeal to the supreme court was automatic.


OVERVIEW: The victim's body was found in a dumpster. She had been stabbed and sexually assaulted. Defendant was a suspect because several items belonging to him were found under the victim's body. A detective asked defendant to come to the police station. Detectives interviewed him and ultimately decided to hold him for further questioning. He was read the Miranda warnings, and questioning continued until he requested counsel, at which point the questioning ceased. After defendant spoke with his mother, he made taped statements to the detective. The supreme court found that defendant initiated the discussion with the detective and that he made a voluntary, knowing, and intelligent waiver of his rights. Thus, defendant's post-Miranda statements to the detective were admissible. Additionally, the results of the blood test were admissible at trial. Also, the introduction of cumulative, non-essential, and gruesome photographs was harmless error because of the strength of the evidence against defendant. Finally, because the United States Supreme Court determined that Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703 was unconstitutional, defendant's sentence of death might have been improper.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions and sentences were affirmed except for the sentence of death.

Ring v. Arizona, 536 U.S. 584, 153 L. Ed. 2d 556, 122 S. Ct. 2428, 2002 U.S. LEXIS 4651 (2002)



DISPOSITION: Order requests that briefs be filed by counsel for the state and counsel for the defendants addressing the questions set forth by the court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After Arizona's death penalty statute, Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703, was declared unconstitutional, the Supreme Court of Arizona consolidated all 31 capital cases pending before it on appeal and issued an order to establish a procedure for case management.


OVERVIEW: The 31 capital cases pending before the supreme court were at various stages of the process on direct appeal. There were several cases among the 31 in which an opinion had not yet been filed. In them, the supreme court stated it would proceed in the normal course by filing opinions as and when ready. Those opinions, however, would address only the guilt phase of the prosecution. If the conviction was affirmed, the opinions would reserve for later determination all matters pertaining to sentencing. The supreme court directed the State and defense counsel to prepare briefs on several questions which appeared to remain unanswered in the aftermath of the decision striking down the capital sentencing statute. The issues to be addressed were : (1) what was the supreme court permitted to do in order properly to address and deal with the sentencing phase of the captioned cases; (2) what steps did counsel recommend be undertaken by the supreme court in order properly to address and deal with the sentencing issues of the 31 cases; and (3) assuming the state legislature enacted a statute in response to the decision, could such legislation affect sentencing in the 31 cases?


OUTCOME: The supreme court ordered that the cases be consolidated for compliance with its order that set forth the issues to be briefed, required designation of a lead counsel, established a briefing schedule, and set a pre-briefing conference date.

Browne v. Bayless


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-01-0383-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


202 Ariz. 405; 46 P.3d 416; 2002 Ariz. LEXIS 80; 378 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 83



May 24, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Writ of certiorari denied: Browne v. Bayless, 2002 U.S. LEXIS 9268 (U.S. Dec. 16, 2002).


PRIOR HISTORY: Court of Appeals, Division One, No. 1 CA-CV 00-0546. Maricopa County Superior Court No. CV 2000-015468. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Barry C. Schneider, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, 200 Ariz. 261, 25 P.3d 749 (App. 2001).



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County, AFFIRMED. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, VACATED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant secretary of state refused to accept plaintiff independent presidential candidate's nominating petitions to appear on a ballot, as they were filed two months beyond the statutory deadline. The candidate file suit, and the trial court denied him relief. The Court of Appeals, Division One (Arizona), reversed, holding that the deadlines in Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 16-341 impermissibly burdened the association rights of independent voters.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court rejected the secretary's arguments with respect to mootness, laches, and standing. Although the Arizona Supreme Court agreed with the court's resolution of the latter issues, it concluded that § 16-341 did not impose severe restrictions on the candidate's First Amendment rights and furthered important regulatory interests of the State. His exclusion from the ballot resulted not from a filing deadline that discriminated against independents, but rather from his failed attempt to be nominated by his party. Nowhere did it appear that the views he espoused or the voters he purported to represent were other than those of the Libertarian Party, which were already represented by the successful slate of Libertarian electors. Voters were not deprived of either the right to associate to advance their political beliefs or the right to cast their votes effectively. The restrictions imposed were reasonable and nondiscriminatory. The time frame created was rationally related to the state's legitimate interest in ensuring an orderly resolution of those challenges before the deadline for composing, translating, printing, and distributing the final ballots.


OUTCOME: The opinion of the court of appeals was vacated, and the trial court's decision was affirmed.

State v. Finch


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-99-0551-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


202 Ariz. 410; 46 P.3d 421; 2002 Ariz. LEXIS 84; 378 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 38



May 24, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at, Remanded by State v. Finch, 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 63 (Ariz., May 6, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Pima County Superior Court No. CR-61452. Appeal from the Superior Court of Pima County. Honorable Bernard P. Velasco, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court of Pima County, AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of first degree felony murder, as well as numerous non-homicide counts. Following a sentencing hearing, the Superior Court of Pima County (Arizona) sentenced defendant to death. Defendant raised numerous issues on appeal, inter alia, that officers continued to question him after he made a clear and unambiguous request for counsel, and therefore the trial court should have excluded his confession.


OVERVIEW: The sheriff's department conducted two videotaped interviews with defendant after his arrest. He was given Miranda warnings prior to the first interview and then proceeded to describe all three robberies and confess to his role in them. The appellate court held that defendant's statement that he wanted a lawyer, but that the detectives could ask questions, superseded his request for counsel. The interviewer clarified that the statement demonstrated an intent to speak with detectives by asking if he would talk to them at that point. Defendant replied that he would talk to them then. The state's supreme court held that defendant reinitiated contact with police after his request for counsel. Defendant also claimed that the trial court's reasonable doubt instruction improperly shifted the burden of proof to him. In its instruction, the trial court defined reasonable doubt as proof that left the jury firmly convinced of defendant's guilt. The trial court gave the reasonable doubt instruction approved in Portillo, which was not an error. The trial court did not err when it did not provide a proximate cause instruction to the jury sua sponte, because causation was not an issue.


OUTCOME: The defendant's convictions and sentences were affirmed.

Walk v. Ring


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-01-0090-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


202 Ariz. 310; 44 P.3d 990; 2002 Ariz. LEXIS 60; 378 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 61



April 24, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Yavapai County Superior Court No. CV 97-0596. The Honorable William T. Kiger, Judge. Court of Appeals, Division One, No. 1 CA-CV 00-0233. Memorandum Decision of the Court of Appeals, Division One, filed February 8, 2001.



DISPOSITION: Decision vacated, judgment reversed, and case remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff patient brought an action against defendant dentist in 1997 for dental malpractice for treatment in 1991-1992. The patient discovered the malpractice in 1996 and then consulted with counsel. The trial judge granted summary judgment to the dentist, holding that the two-year statute of limitations in Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 12-542 barred the patient's claim. The Arizona Court of Appeals, Division One, affirmed. The patient sought review.


OVERVIEW: In 1994, the dentist told the patient that, because the outcome of the reconstruction work was not what he hoped or expected or what the patient deserved, he was referring her to another dentist and would pay for that treatment. The Arizona Supreme Court granted review to examine application of the discovery rule to situations in which a patient was aware that she had been injured during the course of a physician's treatment, but unaware that the injury was attributable to fault or neglect. The court concluded that for the case at bar, the questions of discovery, and resulting accrual were for the jury and declined to adopt a rule that, in every case, required a patient or client, who suffered an adverse result, to question her doctors or lawyers about the possibility of negligence. The core question was whether a reasonable person would have been on notice to investigate. The dentist assured her he had done nothing wrong, and the court held that, as a matter of law, she was not on notice to commence investigating whether he was negligent, especially when the doctors to whom she was referred failed to disclose to her their belief that the dentist was negligent.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals' memorandum decision was vacated, the trial judge's order granting summary judgment was vacated, the judgment was reversed, and the case was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.

Sherman v. City of Tempe


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-01-0287-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


202 Ariz. 339; 45 P.3d 336; 2002 Ariz. LEXIS 53; 371 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 9



April 12, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals, Division One, No. 1 CA-CV 00-0348. Maricopa County Superior Court No. CV 00-010005. Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County. Honorable Arthur T. Anderson, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, 200 Ariz. 190, 24 P.3d 1285 (2001).



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County, AFFIRMED. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, VACATED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a general election, respondent city voters contested the election results by filing a special action complaint and statement of election contest in superior court under Ariz. Rev. Stat. §§ 16-672 through 16-674. The superior court granted summary judgment to appellee city. The voters appealed. The Court of Appeals, Division One (Arizona) invalidated the election. The city appealed.


OVERVIEW: The voter's complaint alleged that a proposition was invalid, as it was an unconstitutional special law and because the publicity pamphlets were not timely mailed pursuant to § 19-123. The Arizona Supreme Court concluded that the city had not violated the election statutes and that the charter amendment was not an unconstitutional special law. The court of appeals specifically construed § 19-141.A's requirement that pamphlets be distributed "not less than ten days before the election at which measures were to be voted upon," as requiring pamphlet distribution ten days before any voting could occur, not just ten days before the day of the election. By describing the early ballots as being "for the election at which the measures were to be voted on," the legislature distinguished early voting from election day. The former law, which was the law under which the city conducted its general election, required distribution ten days before election day. The city's charter amendment rested on a rational basis, encompassed all those who were and would become mayors, and allowed persons to move into and out of the class, and did not constitute a prohibited special law.


OUTCOME: The appeal from the superior court was affirmed, and the opinion of the court of appeals was vacated.

McDonald v. Thomas


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-01-0001-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


202 Ariz. 35; 40 P.3d 819; 2002 Ariz. LEXIS 24; 367 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 30



February 19, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals, Division One, No. 1 CA-HC 00-0001. Maricopa County Superior Court No. CV 99-15189. ON REVIEW FROM DENIAL OF PETITION FOR WRIT OF HABEAS CORPUS. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, 198 Ariz. 590, 12 P.3d 1194 (App. 2000).



DISPOSITION: ON REVIEW FROM DENIAL OF PETITION FOR WRIT OF HABEAS CORPUS, RELIEF GRANTED. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, VACATED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant filed a petition for writ of habeas corpus, alleging that the governor failed to take proper, timely action to deny the board of executive clemency's (board) unanimous recommendation that his sentence be commuted and that his commutation therefore took effect by operation of law. The trial judge summarily denied the application for the writ. A divided Court of Appeals affirmed.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was sentenced to life in prison after being convicted of an aggravated assault that he committed with a golf club. The life sentence was mandatory under the laws existing at the time, as he committed the crime while on probation for drug and property offenses. The legislature subsequently concluded that some of the mandatory sentences imposed under existing laws were unduly harsh and amended many of the statutes. Under the new legislation, if on review of the sentence, the board unanimously recommended commutation, and the governor failed to act on that recommendation within 90 days, the recommendation for commutation automatically became effective. A letter denying commutation of appellant's sentence was received. However, the letter did not bear sufficient indicia of authenticity, it lacked an official letterhead, a legible signature, and lacked any indication that it came from the governor's office, nor was it attested to by the secretary of state. The supreme court held that the denial of a clemency request was an official act that required the governor's signature, the attestation statute contemplated only the governor act to reject the recommendation.


OUTCOME: The requested relief was granted. The opinion of the court of appeals was vacated, and the department of corrections was ordered to release appellant forthwith.

State v. Canez


Supreme Court No. CR 98-0488-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


202 Ariz. 133; 42 P.3d 564; 2002 Ariz. LEXIS 20; 367 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 37



February 14, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Vacated by, Remanded by State v. Canez, 205 Ariz. 620, 74 P.3d 932, 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 96 (Ariz., 2003)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Canez v. Ryan, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 80732 (D. Ariz., Jan. 28, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Pinal County Superior Court No. CR 96-021235. Appeal from the Superior Court of Pinal County. Honorable Boyd T. Johnson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED IN PART; REVERSED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury convicted defendant of first degree felony murder, first degree burglary, and two counts of armed robbery. The Pinal County Superior Court (Arizona) imposed the death penalty on the murder conviction. Defendant appealed and, inter alia, made a Batson challenge based on the state's removal of five of the seven Hispanic members of the jury pool.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was Hispanic, and the trial judge found that fact met defendant's burden of establishing a prima facie case of discrimination and asked the state to explain its strikes. The court ruled that the reasons offered by the state were race-neutral and denied defendant's Batson challenge. Defendant contended that this was error. The supreme court held that Batson challenges were governed by a three-step analysis: (1) the party challenging the strikes must make a prima facie showing of discrimination; (2) the striking party must provide a race-neutral reason for the strike; and (3) if a race-neutral explanation was provided, the trial court must determine whether the challenger carried its burden of proving purposeful racial discrimination. Although the fact that the state accepted other Hispanic jurors on the venire was indicative of a nondiscriminatory motive, it was not dispositive. Four of the five challenged strikes were based on objective facts, for example, one of the jurors knew one of the state's witnesses, and the fifth was based on a subjective belief that the juror's family harbored ill feelings toward the prosecutor's office. All were race-neutral challenges.


OUTCOME: The conviction and sentence on the second allegation of armed robbery was reversed, and all remaining convictions and sentences were affirmed.

State v. Lehr


Supreme Court No. CR-97-0317-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


201 Ariz. 509; 38 P.3d 1172; 2002 Ariz. LEXIS 15; 378 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 6



January 30, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of certiorari denied: Lehr v. Arizona, 2002 U.S. LEXIS 8359 (U.S. Nov. 12, 2002).
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Lehr v. Arizona, 154 L. Ed. 2d 428, 123 S. Ct. 550, 2002 U.S. LEXIS 8359 (U.S., 2002)
Appeal after remand at, Remanded by State v. Lehr, 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 60 (Ariz., Apr. 30, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County No. CR-92-05731. Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County. The Honorable Stephen A. Gerst, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED IN PART, REVERSED IN PART AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from his convictions and sentences by the Superior Court of Maricopa County (Arizona) for, inter alia, several murders, attempted first degree murders, and sexual assaults. He claimed, inter alia, that limits placed upon cross-examination at trial violated his fundamental right to confront adverse witnesses under the Sixth and Fourteenth Amendments.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court held that defendant's right to confront adverse witnesses had been violated. Because the appellate court could not say beyond a reasonable doubt that the error had no effect on seven of the thirty-seven verdicts, including two of the first-degree murder convictions, it had to reverse them. The court also agreed that defendant's Sixth Amendment rights were violated because of a ruling that precluded him from presenting expert testimony regarding the protocol, validation studies, and match window of the DNA analysis. The trial judge's application of Ariz. R. Evid. 403 was based on the erroneous assumption that evidence from the Frye hearing would play no significant role in the jury's consideration. Thus, the trial court abused its discretion when it found that the probative value of that evidence was outweighed by the risk of juror confusion. However, for all but three of the victims, there was credible eye-witness identification, physical evidence apart from DNA, or other corroborating facts that clearly implicated the defendant, and the error was harmless as to them. The counts relating to the attacks upon three of the other victims were another matter.


OUTCOME: The convictions and sentences were affirmed in part, reversed in part, and the case was remanded.

Forest Guardians v. Wells


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-00-0177-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


201 Ariz. 255; 34 P.3d 364; 2001 Ariz. LEXIS 206; 361 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 29



November 21, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals Division One No. 1 CA-CV 99-0258. Maricopa County Superior Court Nos. CV 98-07787, CV 98-07788 (Consolidated). Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. the Honorable Michael J. O'Melia, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One.

 Forest Guardians v. Wells, 197 Ariz. 511, 4 P.3d 1054, 2000 Ariz. App. LEXIS 59 (Ct. App. 2000) 



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County REVERSED AND REMANDED. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One VACATED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant state land commissioner (Commissioner) denied the applications of plaintiff environmentalists who were the highest bidders for grazing leases on three parcels of land that were part of the state's school land trust, which was administered by defendant state land department (Department). The Court of Appeals, Division One (Arizona) affirmed the commissioner and trial court. The environmentalists requested review.


OVERVIEW: The commissioner's denial was premised on the conclusion that the statutory scheme did not permit the issuance of grazing leases for the purpose of restoring the land. Though the commissioner had classified all the parcels of land as grazing land, the environmentalists did not intend to graze livestock on any of the parcels in question. They intended that the land would be rested for the entire term of the ten-year leases. They requested that the commissioner permitted them to use the land for purposes other than domestic livestock grazing. The Supreme Court of Arizona examined the denial in light of the commissioner's fiduciary duty with respect to the administration of the school land trust. The court held that the Ariz. Const. art. X did not permit the commissioner to reject a high bid for a grazing lease without considering the benefit to the land in question or the trust lands in general, merely because the high bidder proposed to rest and restore land that the commissioner had classified as usable only for grazing. The commissioner's fiduciary duty required him to consider the bids and ascertain whether they were best for the corpus of the trust and its beneficiaries.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals' opinion was vacated, and the case was remanded to the trial court with instructions to enter judgment requiring the commissioner to determine whether the environmentalists high bids were, in the long term, best for the school trust lands and their beneficiaries.

Ariz. Dep't of Revenue v. Dougherty


Arizona Supreme Court No. CV-00-0242-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


200 Ariz. 515; 29 P.3d 862; 2001 Ariz. LEXIS 122; 355 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 16



August 29, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals Division One No. 1 CA-SA 99-0084. Arizona Tax Court No. TX 97-00075. Special Action in the Superior Court for Maricopa County. The Honorable Bernard J. Dougherty, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, 198 Ariz. 1, 6 P.3d 306 (App. 2001).



DISPOSITION: Special Action in the Superior Court for Maricopa County ORDER APPROVED. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, APPROVED IN PART, VACATED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A taxpayer filed a claim with petitioner, the Arizona Department of Revenue (ADOR), and a class action in the Arizona Tax Court. The taxpayer exhausted her administrative remedies and re-filed her complaint. The tax court granted summary judgment for the class. The ADOR brought a special action in the Superior Court for Maricopa County, and the Court of Appeals, Division One, held the class would be limited to individually filed claimants.


OVERVIEW: On review, the supreme court considered the question of whether a class may include taxpayers who had not filed individual administrative claims once a tax court judge decides that the requirements for a class action have been met. The supreme court held that: (1) Arizona law permitted class action lawsuits in tax court; (2) the class device was a suitable vehicle for exhaustion of administrative remedies when not expressly prohibited by statute; (3) the tax court did not err when it certified the class in the taxpayer's lawsuit; and (4) only those taxpayers whose claims were not barred by the statute of limitations, and who therefore could have filed separate, individual administrative refund claims at the time the taxpayer filed her representative claim, and whose administrative remedies were therefore preserved by the taxpayer's filing, were not barred by the statute of limitations and could join as members of the class in the tax court.


OUTCOME: The superior court's order was approved, and the court of appeals' opinion was approved in part. The court of appeals' opinion was vacated in part as to that portion of the decision relating to the trial judge's ruling that the taxpayer could use the class device as a vehicle for bringing and exhausting those administrative claims not already barred by the statute of limitations at the time the representative claim was filed.

State v. Pandeli


Supreme Court No. CR 98-0376-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


200 Ariz. 365; 26 P.3d 1136; 2001 Ariz. LEXIS 107



July 17, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Vacated by, Remanded by, Motion granted by Pandeli v. Arizona, 536 U.S. 953, 122 S. Ct. 2654, 153 L. Ed. 2d 830, 2002 U.S. LEXIS 4894 (2002)
Subsequent appeal at, Petition granted by State v. Pandeli, 2016 Ariz. LEXIS 88 (Ariz., Mar. 15, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR 93-08116. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Gregory H. Martin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The defendant was convicted by a jury of first degree murder and was sentenced to death in the Maricopa County Superior Court (Arizona). The defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The defendant argued, inter alia, that the trial court erred by not admitting into evidence his confession to the police: (1) under the hearsay exception for a statement against interest; or (2) under the hearsay exception for a statement of a declarant's then existing state of mind. The Arizona Supreme Court held that the trial court had not erred because: (1) the defendant was not "unavailable" to himself and had the ability and the right to testify, should he decline by asserting his Fifth Amendment privilege, he could not then seek to introduce his statement to police yet at the same time avoid cross-examination under oath and impeachment; and (2) the defendant's statements that he and the victim disrobed in the back of the van, that he became angry when he could not perform sexually and the victim refused to return the money, that she pulled a knife and defendant took it from her, and that he excised breast parts because of his anger, described neither present feeling nor future intent, the statements contained nothing more than asserted memory of past events. Therefore, the trial court did not abuse its discretion in excluding the confession as inadmissible hearsay.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

185 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


State v. Harrod


Supreme Court No. CR-98-0289-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


200 Ariz. 309; 26 P.3d 492; 2001 Ariz. LEXIS 104; 352 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 11



July 16, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County No. CR-95-09046. Appeal from the Superior Court of Arizona in Maricopa County. The Honorable Ronald S. Reinstein, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury convicted defendant of premeditated murder and felony murder. The Superior Court of Arizona in Maricopa County sentenced him to death, and defendant appealed his conviction and death sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murdering a wealthy widow. The State claimed that defendant agreed to kill the woman in exchange for $ 100,000 to be paid by the son of the victim's deceased husband. On appeal, the Arizona Supreme Court held: (1) the superior court did not err in excluding third party culpability evidence; (2) the superior court did not err in finding that a State's witness had not been successfully hypnotized and permitting her to testify about an identification she made of defendant after the failed hypnosis session; (3) the superior court did not err in permitting defendant's ex-wife to testify she left him because she could not live with someone who could be involved in a murder; (4) the superior court did not err in permitting defendant's ex-wife to testify about acts she observed and to impeach defendant by testifying about otherwise privileged marital communications after he denied having such conversations; (5) the superior court did not err in refusing to admit the results of a polygraph examination at the aggravation/mitigation hearing; and (6) the Arizona death penalty statute was not unconstitutional on its face and/or as applied in this case.


OUTCOME: The conviction and the death sentence were affirmed.

State v. Sansing


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-99-0438-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


200 Ariz. 347; 26 P.3d 1118; 2001 Ariz. LEXIS 96; 351 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 3



July 2, 2001, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR-98-003520. Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County. The Honorable Ronald S. Reinstein.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The defendant pleaded guilty to, inter alia, first-degree murder in the Maricopa County Superior Court (Arizona). The defendant was sentenced to death. There was an automatic, direct appeal.


OVERVIEW: The defendant argued, inter alia, that the trial judge improperly: (1) considered factors in aggravation; and (2) did not consider factors in mitigation. The supreme court held that: (1) it was improper for the court to find that the murder was motivated by pecuniary gain because the murder took place an hour after the victim had been subdued, and was not necessary to aid the defendant in committing the robbery, but, the trial court did not err in finding that the crime was aggravated because the defendant stabbed the victim three times while she was conscious; and (2) the trial court did not err in refusing to allow the victim's daughter to testify in mitigation because Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703.D expressly forbids the consideration of any sentencing recommendation to be made by the victim, the defendant's difficult childhood was irrelevant to sentencing because there was no nexus between that and the crime, and evidence that the defendant was high on crack cocaine, anxious, and spaced out were insufficient to establish that he could not appreciate the wrongfulness of his conduct.


OUTCOME: The sentence of death was affirmed.

State v. Ring


Supreme Court No. CR-97-0428-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


200 Ariz. 267; 25 P.3d 1139; 2001 Ariz. LEXIS 90; 350 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 5



June 20, 2001, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Granted January 11, 2002, Reported at: 2002 U.S. LEXIS 409.
Writ of certiorari granted Ring v. Arizona, 534 U.S. 1103, 122 S. Ct. 865, 151 L. Ed. 2d 738, 2002 U.S. LEXIS 409 (2002)
Reversed by, Remanded by Ring v. Arizona, 536 U.S. 584, 122 S. Ct. 2428, 153 L. Ed. 2d 556, 2002 U.S. LEXIS 4651 (2002)


PRIOR HISTORY: Maricopa County No. CR95-01754(A). Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Gregory H. Martin, Judge.
State v. Smith, 146 Ariz. 491, 707 P.2d 289, 1985 Ariz. LEXIS 248 (Ariz., 1985)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A Maricopa County Superior Court (Arizona) jury found defendant guilty of first degree murder, conspiracy to commit armed robbery, armed robbery, burglary, and theft. Defendant was sentenced to terms of imprisonment for the robbery, burglary, theft, and conspiracy convictions. He was sentenced to death for the murder. Direct appeal to the instant court was automatic.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that Arizona's capital punishment sentencing scheme was unconstitutional and that the trial judge erred in: (1) denying his motion to suppress because, among other things, the affidavit supporting the necessity of wiretap evidence was deficient; (2) denying defendant's motion for new trial; (3) finding that defendant killed the victim; and (4) finding aggravating circumstances. The instant court held that the affidavit fully complied with the necessity requirement and that the trial judge's reasons for denying defendant's motion to suppress were well articulated. Thus, the trial judge did not abuse his discretion in denying defendant's motion to suppress on necessity grounds. Defendant's alleged inability to access his entire Federal Bureau of Investigation file did not render his trial so unfair as to require the granting of a new trial. Arizona's death penalty scheme was not unconstitutional. Although the court disapproved the trial court's finding that defendant murdered the victim in a heinous or depraved manner, it concluded that the application of the pecuniary gain aggravating factor was supported. No other evidence existed in the record to merit leniency.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions and sentences were affirmed.

State v. Hoskins


Supreme Court No. CR-97-0349-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


199 Ariz. 127; 14 P.3d 997; 2000 Ariz. LEXIS 132; 339 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 39



December 29, 2000, Filed


NOTICE:

 [***1] [EDITOR'S NOTE: PART 1 OF 2. THIS DOCUMENT HAS BEEN SPLIT INTO MULTIPLE PARTS ON LEXIS TO ACCOMMODATE ITS LARGE SIZE. EACH PART CONTAINS THE SAME LEXIS CITE.]


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion for Oral Argument Denied September 24, 2002.
Writ of certiorari denied Hoskins v. Arizona, 534 U.S. 970, 151 L. Ed. 2d 294, 122 S. Ct. 386, 2001 U.S. LEXIS 9755 (2001)
Remanded by State v. Hoskins, 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 32 (Ariz., Apr. 3, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: Maricopa County Nos. CR-94-11396 and CR-94-11397 (Consolidated). Appeal from the Maricopa County Superior Court. The Honorable Jeffrey A. Hotham, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's death sentence for premeditated murder, from the Maricopa County Superior Court (Arizona) where at the same time he was also convicted of kidnapping, armed robbery, and theft, received mandatory appeal.


OVERVIEW: Witnesses identified defendant and another as the ones who left the overturned vehicle of a woman who had been found shot dead. Defendant had the gun, taken from his neighbor, that had shot the woman. Two of defendant's acquaintances testified that he had stated that he wanted to do a car-jacking and killing. Defendant was convicted, among other things, of premeditated murder; the Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(F)(5) pecuniary gain aggravator, and a youth mitigator, were found; and he was sentenced to death. The court affirmed. Witness identification of defendant had been detailed. Defendant's arrest statements had been voluntary, but when he chose not to testify, he waived the right to claim erroneous admission of involuntary statements for impeachment. Admission of testimony of the prior consistent statements of a witness had been cumulative, thus not an abuse of discretion. The jury's failure to convict of felony murder did not mandate rejection of the § 13-703(F)(5) factor. Defendant failed to causally connect the mitigators of his personality disorder or family dysfunction to his crime when committed. Defendant had not killed impulsively, but with deliberation and delay.


OUTCOME: Judgment was affirmed. Single aggravator of murder for pecuniary gain sufficiently warranted death penalty, although defendant was only 20. No causal connection between personality disorder and family dysfunction mitigators had been found. Evidence against defendant was strong.

 

189 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


In re General Adjudication of All Rights to Use Water in the Gila River System an Source


Supreme Court Nos. WC-90-0001-IR, WC-90-0002-IR, WC-90-0003-IR, WC-90-0004-IR, WC-90-0005-IR, WC-90-0006-IR, WC-90-0007-IR, WC-79-0001, WC-79-0002, WC-79-0003, WC-79-0004.


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


198 Ariz. 330; 9 P.3d 1069; 2000 Ariz. LEXIS 94



September 22, 2000, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied June 25, 2001, Reported at: 2001 U.S. LEXIS 4896.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] The Honorable Stanley Z. Goodfarb, Judge (Retired).



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On return from remand, in a case involving complex general stream adjudication, the court considered whether the order of the trial court (Arizona) properly determined what underground water constituted "subflow" of a surface stream, thus making it appropriable under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 45-141(A).


OVERVIEW: The state supreme court remanded a case involving complex general stream adjudication to the trial court. On remand, the trial court entered an order defining "subflow" as the saturated floodplain Holocene alluvium, and concluding that: (1) all wells located within the lateral limits of the subflow zone were subject to the adjudication; (2) all wells located outside the subflow zone that were pumping water from a stream or its subflow, as determined by an analysis of the well's cone of depression, were included in the adjudication; and (3) wells that, although pumping subflow, had a de minimus effect on the river system might be excluded from the adjudication based on rational guidelines. When the case returned from remand, the issue before the state supreme court was whether the trial court had properly determined what underground water constituted "subflow" of a surface stream, thus making it appropriable under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 45-141(A). The state supreme court affirmed the trial court's order, because the record reasonably supported the trial court's findings, and the ruling comported with prior decisions, as well as hydrological reality.


OUTCOME: The trial court's order was affirmed, on the grounds that its findings with regards to the definition of "subflow" and the determination of which wells were pumping subflow and were thus subject to the adjudication, were reasonably supported by the record. In addition, the trial court's ruling comported with prior decisions relating to subflow, and with hydrological reality as it was currently understood.

 

190 of 1194 DOCUMENTS


State v. Poyson


Arizona Supreme Court No. CR-99-0335-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


198 Ariz. 70; 7 P.3d 79; 2000 Ariz. LEXIS 63; 325 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 11



July 6, 2000, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied February 20, 2001, Reported at: 2001 U.S. LEXIS 1411.
Writ of certiorari denied Poyson v. Arizona, 531 U.S. 1165, 121 S. Ct. 1125, 148 L. Ed. 2d 992, 2001 U.S. LEXIS 1411 (2001)
Writ of habeas corpus dismissed Poyson v. Schriro, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 56019 (D. Ariz., July 24, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Mohave County No. CR-96-865. Appeal from the Superior Court of Mohave County. The Honorable Steven F. Conn, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant filed an appeal from a decision of the Superior Court of Mohave County (Arizona) which, after a jury trial, convicted appellant of three counts of first degree murder and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of three counts of first degree murder and sentenced to death. He appealed, arguing admitting statements made to police was error, and he was not given Miranda warnings. Affirming, the court found that appellant's statements were properly admitted because there was no suggestion of police overreaching. Appellant failed to point to any evidence in the record indicating that police exploited his mental state, remorse, his age, or his fear to gain a confession, thus, the State proved that appellant's statements were voluntary. The court found that appellant's statement in which he admitted an officer read him his rights both from a card and from memory was made only minutes after the warnings were read, when appellant's recollection was fresh, thus, appellant was fully advised. Finally, the court's independent reweigh of aggravating and mitigating circumstances showed that even crediting appellant's cooperation with police, his age, his potential for rehabilitation, and family support, was not sufficiently substantial to call for leniency.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed. Although appellant's age was a mitigating circumstance, in light of appellant's criminal history and his extensive participation in the murders, the court accorded his age little weight in its independent reweigh of aggravating and mitigating circumstances and found that the death sentence was appropriate.

Citizens Clean Elections Comm'n v. Myers


Supreme Court No. CV-00-0054-SA CONSOLIDATED with Supreme Court No. CV-00-0055-SA


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


196 Ariz. 516; 1 P.3d 706; 2000 Ariz. LEXIS 60; 343 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 8



June 16, 2000, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Superior Court of Arizona in Maricopa County. The Honorable Robert D. Myers, Judge. Maricopa County No. CV 99-11937 CONSOLIDATED with Maricopa County No. CV 99-11937.



DISPOSITION: ORDER VACATED; REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners brought a special action, seeking expedited review of an order of the Superior Court of Arizona, Maricopa County, which held that the Citizens Clean Elections Act, Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 16-940 to -961, violated the Arizona Constitution and was thus invalid.


OVERVIEW: The Citizens Clean Elections Act (Act), Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 16-940 to -961, was adopted as a ballot initiative. The trial court held that the Act violated the state constitution and was invalid. Petitioners, members of a commission responsible for administering the program, and several citizens groups, sought expedited review. Respondents' concerns involved the ways in which persons became members of, and were removed from, the commission. The court accepted special action jurisdiction and affirmed in part, holding that those provisions of the Act that purported to involve the state commission on appellate court appointments, and members of the supreme court, in the appointment of members to the commission violated Ariz. Const. art. VI, §§ 36, 37. The court severed those provisions from the Act. The court also affirmed that the provision for senatorial concurrence in the removal of members of the commission did not violate the doctrine of separation of powers.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part, holding that those provisions of the act that purported to involve the state commission on appellate court appointments, and members of the supreme court, in the appointment of members to the commission were unconstitutional, but held that those provisions were severable.

OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of six counts of first-degree murder, one count of first-degree attempted murder, three counts of aggravated assault, three counts of armed robbery, and two counts of first-degree burglary. State v. Denson


No. 1 CA-CR 15-0592


COURT OF APPEALS OF ARIZONA, DIVISION ONE


241 Ariz. 6; 382 P.3d 1221; 2016 Ariz. App. LEXIS 263; 751 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 4



November 1, 2016, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by State v. Denson, 2017 Ariz. LEXIS 139 (Ariz., May 15, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. No. CR2014-113634-001. The Honorable Jeffrey A. Rueter, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-In a case where defendant was convicted of second degree burglary and possession of burglary tools, the statute criminalizing possession of burglary tools, Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-1505(A)(1) (2016), was not unconstitutionally vague on its face because the statute only applied to a person who possessed a tool, article or instrument that was commonly used to commit a burglary, and who intended to use the tool in the commission of a burglary; the statute only applied to items a person actually intended to use as burglary tools, which effectively eliminated any possible vagueness in the statute, as innocent possession of an item that could be used as a burglary tool was not a crime; and a person of ordinary intelligence would be able to understand what was prohibited under the burglary tools statute.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

State v. Florez

COURT OF APPEALS OF ARIZONA, DIVISION TWO

CASE SUMMARY:

McLaughlin v. Jones


No. 2 CA-SA 2016-0035


COURT OF APPEALS OF ARIZONA, DIVISION TWO


240 Ariz. 560; 382 P.3d 118; 2016 Ariz. App. LEXIS 256; 749 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 32



October 11, 2016, Decided

DISPOSITION: JURISDICTION ACCEPTED; RELIEF DENIED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The superior court correctly found a birth mother's wife was a child's legal parent under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 25-401(4) because the mother was estopped from rebutting the presumption of parenthood pursuant to Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 25-814(C); [2]-The mother and wife were lawfully married when the mother became pregnant, the wife entered into an agreement that guaranteed her equal parental rights with the mother, and by agreeing to the mother's artificial insemination, the wife bound herself under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 25-501 to provide support for the child; [3]-Having two parents to love and support the child was in his best interest; [4]-Section 25-814 accommodates a gender-neutral application and the United States Supreme Court decision that same-sex couples could exercise the fundamental right to marry required the courts to apply it in that manner.


OUTCOME: Petition for special-action relief denied.

State v. Snyder


No. 2 CA-CR 2015-0077


COURT OF APPEALS OF ARIZONA, DIVISION TWO


240 Ariz. 551; 382 P.3d 109; 2016 Ariz. App. LEXIS 251; 749 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 5



October 7, 2016, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Superior Court in Pima County. No. CR20131331001. The Honorable Richard D. Nichols, Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED; VACATED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Defendant's convictions for possession of a deadly weapon by a prohibited possessor, possession of a dangerous drug, and possession of drug paraphernalia were improper because the search of his backpack was unconstitutional under U.S. Const. amend. IV; [2]-The search of the backpack was not inevitable since the officer intended to arrest defendant for shoplifting, mostly likely a misdemeanor, and thus, it was left to the officer's discretion whether to take him to jail or release him; [3]-The record contained no expression of any reason to believe that defendant's backpack contained an immediately dangerous instrumentality prior to the search.
